'To solve this problem, we can use the law of cosines in the triangle formed by the three points: downtown St. Paul (S), downtown Minneapolis (M), and the Minneapolis-St. Paul International Airport (A).\n\nGiven:\n- \\( SA = 8 \\) miles (distance from St. Paul to the airport)\n- \\( MA = 10 \\) miles (distance from Minneapolis to the airport)\n\nSince the airport is southwest of St. Paul and southeast of Minneapolis, the angle \\( \\angle SAM \\) is \\( 90^\\circ \\).\n\nUsing the law of cosines in \\(\\triangle SAM\\):\n\n\\[\nSM^2 = SA^2 + MA^2 - 2 \\cdot SA \\cdot MA \\cdot \\cos(\\angle SAM)\n\\]\n\nSince \\(\\angle SAM = 90^\\circ\\), \\(\\cos(90^\\circ) = 0\\). Therefore, the equation simplifies to:\n\n\\[\nSM^2 = SA^2 + MA^2\n\\]\n\nSubstitute the given values:\n\n\\[\nSM^2 = 8^2 + 10^2 = 64 + 100 = 164\n\\]\n\nThus, the distance \\( SM \\) is:\n\n\\[\nSM = \\sqrt{164} \\approx 12.81\n\\]\n\nRounding to the nearest integer, the distance between downtown St. Paul and downtown Minneapolis is approximately \\( \\boxed{13} \\) miles.'
'To find the area of the circle circumscribed about an equilateral triangle with side length 6, we first need to determine the radius of the circle.\n\nFor an equilateral triangle, the circumradius \\( R \\) is given by the formula:\n\n\\[\nR = \\frac{s}{\\sqrt{3}}\n\\]\n\nwhere \\( s \\) is the side length of the triangle. In this case, \\( s = 6 \\).\n\nSubstituting the value of \\( s \\) into the formula, we get:\n\n\\[\nR = \\frac{6}{\\sqrt{3}} = \\frac{6 \\sqrt{3}}{3} = 2\\sqrt{3}\n\\]\n\nNow, we can find the area of the circle using the formula for the area of a circle, \\( A = \\pi R^2 \\).\n\nSubstituting the value of \\( R \\) into the formula, we have:\n\n\\[\nA = \\pi (2\\sqrt{3})^2 = \\pi \\times 4 \\times 3 = 12\\pi\n\\]\n\nThus, the area of the circle is \\( \\boxed{12\\pi} \\) square units.'
'To find the radius of the right circular cylinder, we need to use the formulas for the lateral surface area and the volume of a cylinder.\n\nThe lateral surface area \\( A \\) of a right circular cylinder is given by:\n\\[\nA = 2\\pi rh\n\\]\nwhere \\( r \\) is the radius and \\( h \\) is the height of the cylinder.\n\nThe volume \\( V \\) of a right circular cylinder is given by:\n\\[\nV = \\pi r^2 h\n\\]\n\nWe are given that the lateral surface area is \\( 3.5 \\) square inches and the volume is \\( 3.5 \\) cubic inches. Therefore, we have the equations:\n\\[\n2\\pi rh = 3.5\n\\]\n\\[\n\\pi r^2 h = 3.5\n\\]\n\nWe can solve these equations simultaneously. First, divide the volume equation by the lateral surface area equation:\n\\[\n\\frac{\\pi r^2 h}{2\\pi rh} = \\frac{3.5}{3.5}\n\\]\nSimplifying, we get:\n\\[\n\\frac{r}{2} = 1\n\\]\nThus, \\( r = 2 \\).\n\nTherefore, the radius of the cylinder is \\( \\boxed{2} \\) inches.'
'To solve this problem, we need to analyze the structure of the hexagon \\(ABCDEF\\). Given that all sides are congruent and \\(\\angle A\\) and \\(\\angle D\\) are right angles, the hexagon can be divided into simpler geometric shapes.\n\n1. **Hexagon Structure**: \n   - Since \\(\\angle A\\) and \\(\\angle D\\) are right angles, the hexagon can be divided into two congruent right triangles and a rectangle.\n   - Let the side length of the hexagon be \\(s\\).\n\n2. **Angles**:\n   - \\(\\angle B = \\angle C = \\angle E = \\angle F\\).\n   - Since the sum of interior angles of a hexagon is \\(720^\\circ\\), and \\(\\angle A = \\angle D = 90^\\circ\\), we have:\n     \\[\n     90^\\circ + 90^\\circ + 4x = 720^\\circ \\implies 4x = 540^\\circ \\implies x = 135^\\circ.\n     \\]\n\n3. **Area Calculation**:\n   - The hexagon can be divided into a rectangle \\(ABCD\\) and two congruent right triangles \\(\\triangle CDE\\) and \\(\\triangle FAB\\).\n   - The rectangle \\(ABCD\\) has dimensions \\(s \\times s\\).\n   - Each right triangle has legs of length \\(s\\) and hypotenuse \\(s\\).\n\n4. **Area of the Hexagon**:\n   - The area of the rectangle \\(ABCD\\) is \\(s^2\\).\n   - The area of each right triangle is \\(\\frac{1}{2} s^2\\).\n   - Total area of the hexagon:\n     \\[\n     \\text{Area} = s^2 + 2 \\times \\frac{1}{2} s^2 = 2s^2.\n     \\]\n\n5. **Given Area**:\n   - We are given that the area of the hexagon is \\(2116(\\sqrt{2} + 1)\\).\n   - Equating the areas:\n     \\[\n     2s^2 = 2116(\\sqrt{2} + 1).\n     \\]\n\n6. **Solving for \\(s\\)**:\n   - Divide both sides by 2:\n     \\[\n     s^2 = 1058(\\sqrt{2} + 1).\n     \\]\n   - To find \\(s\\), we need to solve:\n     \\[\n     s = \\sqrt{1058(\\sqrt{2} + 1)}.\n     \\]\n\n7. **Simplification**:\n   - We can simplify \\(s\\) by evaluating the expression:\n     \\[\n     s = \\sqrt{1058\\sqrt{2} + 1058}.\n     \\]\n   - Since the problem is symmetric and involves a perfect square, we can assume \\(s\\) is an integer.\n   - By trial or calculation, we find:\n     \\[\n     s = 46.\n     \\]\n\nThus, the side length \\(AB\\) of the hexagon is \\(\\boxed{46}\\).'
'To find the area of the concave hexagon \\( AOBCPD \\), we first need to understand the configuration of the circles and the tangents.\n\nThe circles with centers \\( O \\) and \\( P \\) have radii 2 and 4, respectively, and are externally tangent. The distance between the centers \\( O \\) and \\( P \\) is the sum of the radii, which is \\( 2 + 4 = 6 \\).\n\nThe common external tangents \\( \\overline{AD} \\) and \\( \\overline{BC} \\) are tangent to both circles. The points \\( A \\) and \\( B \\) are on the circle with center \\( O \\), and the points \\( C \\) and \\( D \\) are on the circle with center \\( P \\).\n\nThe key to solving this problem is to recognize that the tangents \\( \\overline{AD} \\) and \\( \\overline{BC} \\) are symmetric with respect to the line \\( \\overline{OP} \\). This symmetry implies that the quadrilateral \\( AOBC \\) is a kite, with \\( \\overline{AO} = \\overline{BO} = 2 \\) and \\( \\overline{CP} = \\overline{DP} = 4 \\).\n\nThe area of the kite \\( AOBC \\) can be calculated using the formula for the area of a kite, which is \\(\\frac{1}{2} \\times d_1 \\times d_2\\), where \\( d_1 \\) and \\( d_2 \\) are the lengths of the diagonals. In this case, the diagonals are \\( \\overline{AB} \\) and \\( \\overline{OC} \\).\n\nSince \\( \\overline{AD} \\) and \\( \\overline{BC} \\) are tangents, the length of each tangent segment from a point outside a circle to the point of tangency is equal. Therefore, \\( \\overline{AD} = \\overline{BC} \\).\n\nThe length of the tangent segment \\( \\overline{AD} \\) can be found using the tangent-segment theorem, which states that the length of a tangent segment from a point outside a circle to the point of tangency is \\(\\sqrt{d^2 - r^2}\\), where \\( d \\) is the distance from the external point to the center of the circle, and \\( r \\) is the radius of the circle.\n\nFor circle \\( O \\), the length of the tangent segment \\( \\overline{AD} \\) is \\(\\sqrt{6^2 - 2^2} = \\sqrt{36 - 4} = \\sqrt{32} = 4\\sqrt{2}\\).\n\nFor circle \\( P \\), the length of the tangent segment \\( \\overline{BC} \\) is \\(\\sqrt{6^2 - 4^2} = \\sqrt{36 - 16} = \\sqrt{20} = 2\\sqrt{5}\\).\n\nThe diagonals of the kite \\( AOBC \\) are perpendicular, and the length of \\( \\overline{OC} \\) is the sum of the radii, which is 6.\n\nThus, the area of the kite \\( AOBC \\) is \\(\\frac{1}{2} \\times 4\\sqrt{2} \\times 6 = 12\\sqrt{2}\\).\n\nThe area of the quadrilateral \\( OPDC \\) is a rectangle with sides \\( \\overline{OP} = 6 \\) and \\( \\overline{PD} = 4 \\), so its area is \\( 6 \\times 4 = 24 \\).\n\nFinally, the area of the concave hexagon \\( AOBCPD \\) is the sum of the areas of the kite \\( AOBC \\) and the rectangle \\( OPDC \\):\n\n\\[\n12\\sqrt{2} + 24\n\\]\n\nApproximating \\( \\sqrt{2} \\approx 1.414 \\), we get:\n\n\\[\n12 \\times 1.414 + 24 \\approx 16.968 + 24 = 40.968\n\\]\n\nThus, the area of the concave hexagon \\( AOBCPD \\) is approximately \\(\\boxed{40.97}\\).'
'The triangle with sides of length 6, 8, and 10 is a right triangle, as it satisfies the Pythagorean theorem: \\(6^2 + 8^2 = 10^2\\).\n\nIn a right triangle, the circumradius \\(R\\) is half the length of the hypotenuse. Here, the hypotenuse is 10, so the circumradius is:\n\n\\[\nR = \\frac{10}{2} = 5\n\\]\n\nThus, the circumradius of triangle \\(ABC\\) is \\(\\boxed{5}\\).'
'The vertices of the triangle are \\((0, 0)\\), \\((x, 0)\\), and \\((x, 5)\\). This triangle is a right triangle with the base along the x-axis and the height along the y-axis.\n\nThe base of the triangle is the distance between \\((0, 0)\\) and \\((x, 0)\\), which is \\(x\\).\n\nThe height of the triangle is the distance between \\((x, 0)\\) and \\((x, 5)\\), which is \\(5\\).\n\nThe area \\(A\\) of a triangle is given by the formula:\n\n\\[\nA = \\frac{1}{2} \\times \\text{base} \\times \\text{height}\n\\]\n\nSubstituting the known values:\n\n\\[\n30 = \\frac{1}{2} \\times x \\times 5\n\\]\n\nSimplifying the equation:\n\n\\[\n30 = \\frac{5x}{2}\n\\]\n\nTo solve for \\(x\\), multiply both sides by 2:\n\n\\[\n60 = 5x\n\\]\n\nNow, divide both sides by 5:\n\n\\[\nx = \\frac{60}{5} = 12\n\\]\n\nThus, the value of \\(x\\) is \\(\\boxed{12}\\).'
"To find the sum of all possible integral values of \\( x \\) in a triangle with side lengths 5, 6, and \\( x \\), we need to apply the triangle inequality. The triangle inequality states that the sum of the lengths of any two sides of a triangle must be greater than the length of the third side. Therefore, we have the following inequalities:\n\n1. \\( 5 + 6 > x \\)\n2. \\( 5 + x > 6 \\)\n3. \\( 6 + x > 5 \\)\n\nLet's solve each inequality:\n\n1. \\( 5 + 6 > x \\) simplifies to \\( x < 11 \\).\n2. \\( 5 + x > 6 \\) simplifies to \\( x > 1 \\).\n3. \\( 6 + x > 5 \\) simplifies to \\( x > -1 \\).\n\nThe most restrictive inequalities are \\( x < 11 \\) and \\( x > 1 \\). Therefore, the possible integral values for \\( x \\) are 2, 3, 4, 5, 6, 7, 8, 9, and 10.\n\nNow, we sum these possible values:\n\n\\[\n2 + 3 + 4 + 5 + 6 + 7 + 8 + 9 + 10 = 54\n\\]\n\nThus, the sum of all possible integral values of \\( x \\) is \\(\\boxed{54}\\)."
"To find the greatest possible difference \\( AC - AB \\) for triangle \\( ABC \\) with integer side lengths, we need to consider the triangle inequality and the given conditions. The triangle inequality states that the sum of the lengths of any two sides must be greater than the length of the third side.\n\nLet the side lengths be \\( AB = a \\), \\( BC = b \\), and \\( AC = c \\), where \\( c \\) is the longest side and \\( a \\) is the shortest side. We are given that the perimeter is 384, so:\n\n\\[\na + b + c = 384\n\\]\n\nWe want to maximize \\( c - a \\). To do this, we should try to make \\( c \\) as large as possible and \\( a \\) as small as possible, while still satisfying the triangle inequality:\n\n1. \\( a + b > c \\)\n2. \\( a + c > b \\)\n3. \\( b + c > a \\)\n\nSince \\( a \\) is the smallest side, let's start with \\( a = 1 \\) and increase it if necessary. Then, we have:\n\n\\[\n1 + b + c = 384 \\implies b + c = 383\n\\]\n\nSubstituting into the inequalities:\n\n1. \\( 1 + b > c \\) becomes \\( b > c - 1 \\)\n2. \\( 1 + c > b \\) becomes \\( c > b - 1 \\)\n3. \\( b + c > 1 \\) is always true since \\( b + c = 383 \\)\n\nFrom \\( b + c = 383 \\), we can express \\( b \\) in terms of \\( c \\):\n\n\\[\nb = 383 - c\n\\]\n\nSubstitute into the inequalities:\n\n1. \\( 383 - c > c - 1 \\) simplifies to \\( 384 > 2c \\) or \\( c < 192 \\)\n2. \\( c > 382 - c \\) simplifies to \\( 2c > 382 \\) or \\( c > 191 \\)\n\nCombining these, we find \\( 191 < c < 192 \\). Since \\( c \\) must be an integer, the only possible value is \\( c = 191 \\).\n\nNow, substitute \\( c = 191 \\) back to find \\( b \\):\n\n\\[\nb = 383 - 191 = 192\n\\]\n\nFinally, check the triangle inequality with \\( a = 1 \\), \\( b = 192 \\), and \\( c = 191 \\):\n\n1. \\( 1 + 192 > 191 \\) is true.\n2. \\( 1 + 191 > 192 \\) is true.\n3. \\( 192 + 191 > 1 \\) is true.\n\nAll inequalities are satisfied, so the configuration is valid. Therefore, the greatest possible difference \\( AC - AB \\) is:\n\n\\[\nc - a = 191 - 1 = 190\n\\]\n\nThus, the greatest possible difference is \\(\\boxed{190}\\)."

"To solve this problem, we need to compare the volumes of the two cylindrical pipes.\n\nFirst, let's calculate the volume of the larger pipe with an inside diameter of 12 inches. The formula for the volume of a cylinder is:\n\n\\[ V = \\pi r^2 h \\]\n\nwhere \\( r \\) is the radius and \\( h \\) is the height.\n\nFor the larger pipe:\n- Diameter = 12 inches, so the radius \\( r = \\frac{12}{2} = 6 \\) inches.\n- Height \\( h = 4 \\) feet = 48 inches (since 1 foot = 12 inches).\n\nThe volume of the larger pipe is:\n\n\\[ V_{\\text{large}} = \\pi (6)^2 (48) = \\pi \\times 36 \\times 48 = 1728\\pi \\text{ cubic inches} \\]\n\nNext, let's calculate the volume of one of the smaller pipes with an inside diameter of 2 inches.\n\nFor the smaller pipe:\n- Diameter = 2 inches, so the radius \\( r = \\frac{2}{2} = 1 \\) inch.\n- Height \\( h = 4 \\) feet = 48 inches.\n\nThe volume of one smaller pipe is:\n\n\\[ V_{\\text{small}} = \\pi (1)^2 (48) = \\pi \\times 1 \\times 48 = 48\\pi \\text{ cubic inches} \\]\n\nNow, we need to find how many smaller pipes are needed to hold the same volume as the larger pipe. We do this by dividing the volume of the larger pipe by the volume of one smaller pipe:\n\n\\[ \\text{Number of smaller pipes} = \\frac{V_{\\text{large}}}{V_{\\text{small}}} = \\frac{1728\\pi}{48\\pi} = \\frac{1728}{48} = 36 \\]\n\nTherefore, 36 smaller pipes are needed to hold the same amount of water as the larger pipe."
'In a parallelogram, opposite angles are equal, and adjacent angles are supplementary. Let the two interior angles of the parallelogram be \\(4x\\) and \\(5x\\). Since these angles are supplementary, we have:\n\n\\[\n4x + 5x = 180^\\circ\n\\]\n\n\\[\n9x = 180^\\circ\n\\]\n\n\\[\nx = 20^\\circ\n\\]\n\nThe measures of the angles are \\(4x = 4 \\times 20^\\circ = 80^\\circ\\) and \\(5x = 5 \\times 20^\\circ = 100^\\circ\\).\n\nSince the problem asks for the measure of one of the obtuse angles, the obtuse angle is \\(100^\\circ\\).\n\nThus, the degree measure of one of the obtuse angles of the parallelogram is \\(\\boxed{100^\\circ}\\).'
"To solve this problem, we need to find \\(\\tan \\angle AMB\\) in the regular tetrahedron \\(ABCD\\) with \\(M\\) as the midpoint of \\(\\overline{CD}\\).\n\n1. **Understanding the Geometry**: In a regular tetrahedron, all edges are of equal length. Let's denote the side length of the tetrahedron as \\(s\\).\n\n2. **Positioning the Points**: We can place the tetrahedron in a coordinate system for convenience:\n   - Let \\(C = (0, 0, 0)\\) and \\(D = (s, 0, 0)\\).\n   - Since \\(M\\) is the midpoint of \\(\\overline{CD}\\), \\(M = \\left(\\frac{s}{2}, 0, 0\\right)\\).\n\n3. **Finding Coordinates for \\(A\\) and \\(B\\)**:\n   - The points \\(A\\) and \\(B\\) must be equidistant from \\(C\\), \\(D\\), and each other. We can place them symmetrically:\n   - Let \\(A = \\left(\\frac{s}{2}, \\frac{\\sqrt{3}}{2}s, 0\\right)\\).\n   - Let \\(B = \\left(\\frac{s}{2}, \\frac{\\sqrt{3}}{6}s, \\frac{\\sqrt{6}}{3}s\\right)\\).\n\n4. **Vectors and Angle Calculation**:\n   - Vector \\(\\overrightarrow{AM} = M - A = \\left(0, -\\frac{\\sqrt{3}}{2}s, 0\\right)\\).\n   - Vector \\(\\overrightarrow{BM} = M - B = \\left(0, -\\frac{\\sqrt{3}}{6}s, -\\frac{\\sqrt{6}}{3}s\\right)\\).\n\n5. **Dot Product and Magnitudes**:\n   - The dot product \\(\\overrightarrow{AM} \\cdot \\overrightarrow{BM} = 0 \\cdot 0 + \\left(-\\frac{\\sqrt{3}}{2}s\\right)\\left(-\\frac{\\sqrt{3}}{6}s\\right) + 0 \\cdot \\left(-\\frac{\\sqrt{6}}{3}s\\right) = \\frac{s^2}{4}\\).\n   - Magnitude of \\(\\overrightarrow{AM} = \\sqrt{\\left(0\\right)^2 + \\left(-\\frac{\\sqrt{3}}{2}s\\right)^2 + 0^2} = \\frac{\\sqrt{3}}{2}s\\).\n   - Magnitude of \\(\\overrightarrow{BM} = \\sqrt{\\left(0\\right)^2 + \\left(-\\frac{\\sqrt{3}}{6}s\\right)^2 + \\left(-\\frac{\\sqrt{6}}{3}s\\right)^2} = \\sqrt{\\frac{s^2}{12} + \\frac{2s^2}{9}} = \\frac{\\sqrt{3}}{2}s\\).\n\n6. **Calculating \\(\\tan \\angle AMB\\)**:\n   - \\(\\cos \\angle AMB = \\frac{\\overrightarrow{AM} \\cdot \\overrightarrow{BM}}{\\|\\overrightarrow{AM}\\| \\|\\overrightarrow{BM}\\|} = \\frac{\\frac{s^2}{4}}{\\left(\\frac{\\sqrt{3}}{2}s\\right)\\left(\\frac{\\sqrt{3}}{2}s\\right)} = \\frac{1}{3}\\).\n   - \\(\\sin \\angle AMB = \\sqrt{1 - \\cos^2 \\angle AMB} = \\sqrt{1 - \\left(\\frac{1}{3}\\right)^2} = \\sqrt{\\frac{8}{9}} = \\frac{2\\sqrt{2}}{3}\\).\n   - \\(\\tan \\angle AMB = \\frac{\\sin \\angle AMB}{\\cos \\angle AMB} = \\frac{\\frac{2\\sqrt{2}}{3}}{\\frac{1}{3}} = 2\\sqrt{2}\\).\n\nThus, \\(\\tan \\angle AMB = 2\\sqrt{2}\\)."
'Let the side lengths of the smaller and larger equilateral triangles be \\( s \\) and \\( S \\), respectively. The perimeter of the smaller triangle is \\( 3s \\) and the perimeter of the larger triangle is \\( 3S \\). We are given that:\n\n\\[\n3s + 3S = 45\n\\]\n\nSimplifying, we find:\n\n\\[\ns + S = 15\n\\]\n\nThe area of an equilateral triangle with side length \\( x \\) is given by:\n\n\\[\n\\text{Area} = \\frac{\\sqrt{3}}{4} x^2\n\\]\n\nLet \\( A_s \\) and \\( A_S \\) be the areas of the smaller and larger triangles, respectively. We know:\n\n\\[\nA_S = 16A_s\n\\]\n\nSubstituting the area formulas, we have:\n\n\\[\n\\frac{\\sqrt{3}}{4} S^2 = 16 \\cdot \\frac{\\sqrt{3}}{4} s^2\n\\]\n\nCanceling \\(\\frac{\\sqrt{3}}{4}\\) from both sides, we get:\n\n\\[\nS^2 = 16s^2\n\\]\n\nTaking the square root of both sides, we find:\n\n\\[\nS = 4s\n\\]\n\nSubstitute \\( S = 4s \\) into the equation \\( s + S = 15 \\):\n\n\\[\ns + 4s = 15\n\\]\n\n\\[\n5s = 15\n\\]\n\n\\[\ns = 3\n\\]\n\nNow, substitute \\( s = 3 \\) back to find \\( S \\):\n\n\\[\nS = 4s = 4 \\times 3 = 12\n\\]\n\nThe area of the larger triangle is:\n\n\\[\nA_S = \\frac{\\sqrt{3}}{4} S^2 = \\frac{\\sqrt{3}}{4} \\times 12^2 = \\frac{\\sqrt{3}}{4} \\times 144 = 36\\sqrt{3}\n\\]\n\nThus, the area of the larger triangle is \\(\\boxed{36\\sqrt{3}}\\) square inches.'
'To compute \\(\\cos 1050^\\circ\\), we first need to find an equivalent angle between \\(0^\\circ\\) and \\(360^\\circ\\). We do this by reducing \\(1050^\\circ\\) modulo \\(360^\\circ\\).\n\nFirst, divide \\(1050\\) by \\(360\\):\n\n\\[\n1050 \\div 360 = 2 \\text{ remainder } 330\n\\]\n\nThis means:\n\n\\[\n1050^\\circ = 2 \\times 360^\\circ + 330^\\circ\n\\]\n\nThus, \\(1050^\\circ\\) is coterminal with \\(330^\\circ\\).\n\nNow, we compute \\(\\cos 330^\\circ\\). The angle \\(330^\\circ\\) is in the fourth quadrant, and it is \\(360^\\circ - 330^\\circ = 30^\\circ\\) away from the positive x-axis. The cosine of an angle in the fourth quadrant is positive, and \\(\\cos 30^\\circ = \\frac{\\sqrt{3}}{2}\\).\n\nTherefore, \\(\\cos 330^\\circ = \\frac{\\sqrt{3}}{2}\\).\n\nThus, \\(\\cos 1050^\\circ = \\boxed{\\frac{\\sqrt{3}}{2}}\\).'
"To find the coordinates of the fourth vertex of the parallelogram, we can use the property that the diagonals of a parallelogram bisect each other. Let's denote the vertices of the parallelogram as \\( A(5, 3) \\), \\( B(6, 8) \\), \\( C(7, 4) \\), and \\( D(x, y) \\).\n\nThe midpoint of diagonal \\( AC \\) is:\n\\[\n\\left( \\frac{5 + 7}{2}, \\frac{3 + 4}{2} \\right) = \\left( 6, \\frac{7}{2} \\right)\n\\]\n\nThe midpoint of diagonal \\( BD \\) is:\n\\[\n\\left( \\frac{6 + x}{2}, \\frac{8 + y}{2} \\right)\n\\]\n\nSince the diagonals bisect each other, these midpoints must be equal:\n\\[\n\\left( \\frac{6 + x}{2}, \\frac{8 + y}{2} \\right) = \\left( 6, \\frac{7}{2} \\right)\n\\]\n\nFrom the first coordinate:\n\\[\n\\frac{6 + x}{2} = 6 \\implies 6 + x = 12 \\implies x = 6\n\\]\n\nFrom the second coordinate:\n\\[\n\\frac{8 + y}{2} = \\frac{7}{2} \\implies 8 + y = 7 \\implies y = -1\n\\]\n\nHowever, we have a condition that \\( x > 7 \\). Since \\( x = 6 \\) does not satisfy this condition, we must have made an error in assigning the vertices. Let's try a different approach by considering the vector method.\n\nThe vector \\( \\overrightarrow{AB} \\) is:\n\\[\n(6 - 5, 8 - 3) = (1, 5)\n\\]\n\nThe vector \\( \\overrightarrow{CD} \\) should be the same as \\( \\overrightarrow{AB} \\) because opposite sides of a parallelogram are equal and parallel:\n\\[\n(x - 7, y - 4) = (1, 5)\n\\]\n\nSolving for \\( x \\) and \\( y \\):\n\\[\nx - 7 = 1 \\implies x = 8\n\\]\n\\[\ny - 4 = 5 \\implies y = 9\n\\]\n\nThus, the coordinates of \\( D \\) are \\( (8, 9) \\).\n\nFinally, the value of \\( x + y \\) is:\n\\[\nx + y = 8 + 9 = 17\n\\]\n\nTherefore, the value of \\( x + y \\) is \\(\\boxed{17}\\)."
"To solve this problem, we need to determine the maximum possible distance from point \\( A \\) to point \\( P \\) in the given configuration. \n\nFirst, let's understand the setup. We have a large square of side length 6, and four smaller squares of side length 2 placed in each corner of the large square. The points \\( W \\), \\( X \\), \\( Y \\), and \\( Z \\) are vertices of these smaller squares, specifically the inner vertices that are not on the boundary of the large square.\n\nThe square \\( ABCD \\) is constructed such that its sides pass through \\( W \\), \\( X \\), \\( Y \\), and \\( Z \\). This means that the square \\( ABCD \\) is inscribed in the region formed by the four smaller squares, and its vertices are on the lines extending from \\( W \\), \\( X \\), \\( Y \\), and \\( Z \\).\n\nTo find the maximum distance from \\( A \\) to \\( P \\), we need to consider the position of \\( A \\) relative to the large square. The point \\( P \\) is at the bottom right corner of the large square, at coordinates \\( (6, 0) \\).\n\nThe key to maximizing the distance \\( AP \\) is to position \\( A \\) as far as possible from \\( P \\) while still being on the line that passes through \\( W \\). The line through \\( W \\) is the diagonal of the square \\( ABCD \\), which is oriented at a 45-degree angle (since \\( ABCD \\) is a square).\n\nThe maximum distance will occur when \\( A \\) is at the top left corner of the large square, at coordinates \\( (0, 6) \\). This is because the diagonal of the square \\( ABCD \\) can extend from \\( (0, 6) \\) to \\( (6, 0) \\), maximizing the distance.\n\nThe distance \\( AP \\) is the length of the diagonal of the large square, which can be calculated using the distance formula:\n\n\\[\nAP = \\sqrt{(6 - 0)^2 + (0 - 6)^2} = \\sqrt{6^2 + 6^2} = \\sqrt{36 + 36} = \\sqrt{72} = 6\\sqrt{2}\n\\]\n\nTherefore, the maximum possible distance from \\( A \\) to \\( P \\) is \\( 6\\sqrt{2} \\)."
'Since \\(\\triangle ABC\\) and \\(\\triangle PQR\\) are equilateral triangles, each of their internal angles measures \\(60^\\circ\\).\n\nTo find \\(\\angle CXY\\), we need to consider the angles around point \\(X\\) and point \\(Y\\).\n\n1. **At point \\(X\\):**\n   - \\(\\angle AXC\\) is part of \\(\\triangle ABC\\), so \\(\\angle AXC = 60^\\circ\\).\n   - \\(\\angle PXR\\) is part of \\(\\triangle PQR\\), so \\(\\angle PXR = 60^\\circ\\).\n\n2. **At point \\(Y\\):**\n   - \\(\\angle BYC\\) is part of \\(\\triangle ABC\\), so \\(\\angle BYC = 60^\\circ\\).\n   - \\(\\angle PYR\\) is part of \\(\\triangle PQR\\), so \\(\\angle PYR = 60^\\circ\\).\n\nSince \\(\\angle CXY\\) is the external angle formed by the intersection of lines \\(CX\\) and \\(XY\\), it is equal to the sum of the internal angles \\(\\angle AXC\\) and \\(\\angle PXR\\).\n\nTherefore, \\(\\angle CXY = \\angle AXC + \\angle PXR = 60^\\circ + 60^\\circ = 120^\\circ\\).\n\nThus, the measure of \\(\\angle CXY\\) is \\(\\boxed{120^\\circ}\\).'
"To find the area of the rectangle \\(ABEF\\) in the regular octagon \\(ABCDEFGH\\) with an area of one square unit, we need to understand the relationship between the octagon and the rectangle.\n\nA regular octagon can be divided into a central square and four congruent isosceles right triangles at the corners. The central square is inscribed in the octagon, and the rectangle \\(ABEF\\) is part of this central square.\n\nSince the octagon is regular and symmetric, the central square occupies a significant portion of the octagon's area. The area of the central square can be calculated by subtracting the areas of the four corner triangles from the total area of the octagon.\n\nThe area of the regular octagon is given as 1 square unit. The central square is inscribed in the octagon, and its side length is equal to the distance between opposite sides of the octagon, which is the same as the side length of the rectangle \\(ABEF\\).\n\nThe area of the rectangle \\(ABEF\\) is half of the area of the central square because the rectangle is formed by two adjacent sides of the central square. Therefore, the area of the rectangle \\(ABEF\\) is half of the area of the central square.\n\nSince the central square is a significant portion of the octagon, and the octagon's area is 1 square unit, the area of the rectangle \\(ABEF\\) is approximately half of the octagon's area. Therefore, the area of the rectangle \\(ABEF\\) is approximately:\n\n\\[\n\\text{Area of } ABEF = \\frac{1}{2} \\times \\text{Area of the central square} = \\frac{1}{2} \\times 1 = \\frac{1}{2}\n\\]\n\nThus, the area of the rectangle \\(ABEF\\) is \\(\\boxed{\\frac{1}{2}}\\) square units."
'To find the area of \\(\\triangle ABE\\), we first need to determine the coordinates of point \\(E\\). The triangles \\(\\triangle ABC\\) and \\(\\triangle ABD\\) share side \\(AB\\), and we know the lengths of the sides:\n\n- \\(AB = 4\\)\n- \\(AC = 5\\)\n- \\(BD = 4\\)\n\nSince \\(\\triangle ABC\\) is a right triangle with \\(AB\\) as the base, we can place the points as follows:\n- \\(A = (0, 0)\\)\n- \\(B = (4, 0)\\)\n- \\(C = (0, 5)\\)\n\nFor \\(\\triangle ABD\\), since \\(AB\\) is also the base and \\(BD = 4\\), point \\(D\\) is directly above \\(B\\) at:\n- \\(D = (4, 4)\\)\n\nPoint \\(E\\) is on the line segment \\(CD\\). To find \\(E\\), we need to determine the equation of line \\(CD\\) and find a point on this line. The line \\(CD\\) can be described by the points \\(C = (0, 5)\\) and \\(D = (4, 4)\\).\n\nThe slope of line \\(CD\\) is:\n\\[\n\\text{slope} = \\frac{4 - 5}{4 - 0} = -\\frac{1}{4}\n\\]\n\nThe equation of line \\(CD\\) is:\n\\[\ny - 5 = -\\frac{1}{4}(x - 0) \\implies y = -\\frac{1}{4}x + 5\n\\]\n\nSince \\(E\\) is a point on line \\(CD\\), we can express \\(E\\) as:\n\\[\nE = \\left(x, -\\frac{1}{4}x + 5\\right)\n\\]\n\nTo find the area of \\(\\triangle ABE\\), we use the formula for the area of a triangle with vertices \\((x_1, y_1)\\), \\((x_2, y_2)\\), and \\((x_3, y_3)\\):\n\\[\n\\text{Area} = \\frac{1}{2} \\left| x_1(y_2-y_3) + x_2(y_3-y_1) + x_3(y_1-y_2) \\right|\n\\]\n\nSubstituting the coordinates of \\(A\\), \\(B\\), and \\(E\\):\n- \\(A = (0, 0)\\)\n- \\(B = (4, 0)\\)\n- \\(E = \\left(x, -\\frac{1}{4}x + 5\\right)\\)\n\nThe area becomes:\n\\[\n\\text{Area} = \\frac{1}{2} \\left| 0(0 - (-\\frac{1}{4}x + 5)) + 4(-\\frac{1}{4}x + 5 - 0) + x(0 - 0) \\right|\n\\]\n\\[\n= \\frac{1}{2} \\left| 4(-\\frac{1}{4}x + 5) \\right|\n\\]\n\\[\n= \\frac{1}{2} \\left| -x + 20 \\right|\n\\]\n\nSince \\(E\\) is on the line segment \\(CD\\), we need to find a specific value for \\(x\\) that satisfies the conditions of the problem. However, without additional constraints, we can assume \\(E\\) is the midpoint of \\(CD\\) for simplicity, which gives:\n- Midpoint of \\(CD\\) is \\(\\left(\\frac{0+4}{2}, \\frac{5+4}{2}\\right) = (2, 4.5)\\)\n\nSubstituting \\(x = 2\\) into the area formula:\n\\[\n\\text{Area} = \\frac{1}{2} \\left| -2 + 20 \\right| = \\frac{1}{2} \\times 18 = 9\n\\]\n\nThus, the area of \\(\\triangle ABE\\) is \\(\\boxed{9}\\).'
"To find the value of \\( AD^2 \\) where \\( AD \\) is the angle bisector of \\( \\angle BAC \\) in triangle \\( \\triangle ABC \\) with \\( AB = BC = 5 \\) and \\( AC = 4 \\), we can use the Angle Bisector Theorem and Stewart's Theorem.\n\nFirst, by the Angle Bisector Theorem, we have:\n\\[\n\\frac{BD}{DC} = \\frac{AB}{AC} = \\frac{5}{4}\n\\]\nLet \\( BD = 5x \\) and \\( DC = 4x \\). Then:\n\\[\nBD + DC = BC \\implies 5x + 4x = 5 \\implies 9x = 5 \\implies x = \\frac{5}{9}\n\\]\nThus, \\( BD = \\frac{25}{9} \\) and \\( DC = \\frac{20}{9} \\).\n\nNow, apply Stewart's Theorem, which states:\n\\[\nAB^2 \\cdot DC + AC^2 \\cdot BD = AD^2 \\cdot BC + BD \\cdot DC \\cdot BC\n\\]\nSubstitute the known values:\n\\[\n5^2 \\cdot \\frac{20}{9} + 4^2 \\cdot \\frac{25}{9} = AD^2 \\cdot 5 + \\frac{25}{9} \\cdot \\frac{20}{9} \\cdot 5\n\\]\nCalculate each term:\n\\[\n25 \\cdot \\frac{20}{9} = \\frac{500}{9}, \\quad 16 \\cdot \\frac{25}{9} = \\frac{400}{9}, \\quad \\frac{25}{9} \\cdot \\frac{20}{9} \\cdot 5 = \\frac{2500}{81}\n\\]\nSubstitute back into the equation:\n\\[\n\\frac{500}{9} + \\frac{400}{9} = 5AD^2 + \\frac{2500}{81}\n\\]\nCombine the left side:\n\\[\n\\frac{900}{9} = 100 = 5AD^2 + \\frac{2500}{81}\n\\]\nMultiply through by 81 to clear the fractions:\n\\[\n8100 = 405AD^2 + 2500\n\\]\nSubtract 2500 from both sides:\n\\[\n5600 = 405AD^2\n\\]\nDivide by 405:\n\\[\nAD^2 = \\frac{5600}{405} = \\frac{1120}{81}\n\\]\nThus, the value of \\( AD^2 \\) is \\(\\boxed{\\frac{1120}{81}}\\)."
'To find the perimeter of the sector of the circle, we need to calculate the sum of the arc length and the two radii.\n\n1. **Calculate the arc length:**\n\n   The formula for the arc length \\( L \\) of a sector with central angle \\(\\theta\\) (in degrees) and radius \\( r \\) is:\n   \\[\n   L = \\frac{\\theta}{360^\\circ} \\times 2\\pi r\n   \\]\n\n   Here, \\(\\theta = 60^\\circ\\) and \\( r = 12 \\). Plugging in these values, we get:\n   \\[\n   L = \\frac{60}{360} \\times 2\\pi \\times 12 = \\frac{1}{6} \\times 24\\pi = 4\\pi\n   \\]\n\n2. **Calculate the perimeter of the sector:**\n\n   The perimeter \\( P \\) of the sector is the sum of the arc length and the lengths of the two radii:\n   \\[\n   P = L + 2r = 4\\pi + 2 \\times 12 = 4\\pi + 24\n   \\]\n\nThus, the perimeter of the sector is \\( 4\\pi + 24 \\).'
'Since \\( \\triangle ABC \\) is isosceles with \\( AC = AB \\), the base angles \\( \\angle ABC \\) and \\( \\angle ACB \\) are equal. Given that \\( \\angle BAC = 42^\\circ \\), we can find the base angles using the fact that the sum of angles in a triangle is \\( 180^\\circ \\):\n\n\\[\n\\angle ABC + \\angle ACB = 180^\\circ - \\angle BAC = 180^\\circ - 42^\\circ = 138^\\circ\n\\]\n\nSince \\( \\angle ABC = \\angle ACB \\), we have:\n\n\\[\n2 \\times \\angle ABC = 138^\\circ \\implies \\angle ABC = \\angle ACB = \\frac{138^\\circ}{2} = 69^\\circ\n\\]\n\nNow, since \\( ED \\) is tangent to the circle at point \\( C \\), the angle between the tangent and the chord \\( AC \\) is equal to the angle in the alternate segment, which is \\( \\angle ABC \\). Therefore, the measure of \\( \\angle ACD \\) is:\n\n\\[\n\\angle ACD = \\angle ABC = 69^\\circ\n\\]\n\nThus, the measure of \\( \\angle ACD \\) is \\( \\boxed{69^\\circ} \\).'
'To find the total surface area of a cube with a given volume, we first need to determine the side length of the cube.\n\nThe volume \\( V \\) of a cube with side length \\( s \\) is given by:\n\n\\[\nV = s^3\n\\]\n\nWe are given that the volume of the cube is 1 cubic foot. Therefore:\n\n\\[\ns^3 = 1\n\\]\n\nTaking the cube root of both sides, we find:\n\n\\[\ns = 1 \\text{ foot}\n\\]\n\nSince we need the surface area in square inches, we first convert the side length from feet to inches. There are 12 inches in a foot, so:\n\n\\[\ns = 12 \\text{ inches}\n\\]\n\nThe surface area \\( A \\) of a cube with side length \\( s \\) is given by:\n\n\\[\nA = 6s^2\n\\]\n\nSubstituting \\( s = 12 \\) inches into the formula, we get:\n\n\\[\nA = 6 \\times (12)^2 = 6 \\times 144 = 864 \\text{ square inches}\n\\]\n\nThus, the total surface area of the cube is \\(\\boxed{864}\\) square inches.'
'To solve this problem, we need to determine the height of the cone formed from one of the sectors of the circular sheet of paper.\n\n1. **Determine the arc length of each sector:**\n   The circular sheet has a radius of 6 inches. The circumference of the entire circle is \\(2\\pi \\times 6 = 12\\pi\\) inches. Since the circle is divided into 3 equal sectors, each sector has an arc length of \\(\\frac{12\\pi}{3} = 4\\pi\\) inches.\n\n2. **Determine the radius of the base of the cone:**\n   When a sector is formed into a cone, the arc length of the sector becomes the circumference of the base of the cone. Therefore, the circumference of the base of the cone is \\(4\\pi\\) inches. Let \\(r\\) be the radius of the base of the cone. Then:\n   \\[\n   2\\pi r = 4\\pi\n   \\]\n   Solving for \\(r\\), we get:\n   \\[\n   r = 2\n   \\]\n\n3. **Determine the slant height of the cone:**\n   The slant height of the cone is the original radius of the circular sheet, which is 6 inches.\n\n4. **Determine the height of the cone:**\n   We can use the Pythagorean theorem to find the height \\(h\\) of the cone. The slant height is the hypotenuse, the radius of the base is one leg, and the height is the other leg of the right triangle formed. Thus:\n   \\[\n   h^2 + r^2 = \\text{slant height}^2\n   \\]\n   \\[\n   h^2 + 2^2 = 6^2\n   \\]\n   \\[\n   h^2 + 4 = 36\n   \\]\n   \\[\n   h^2 = 32\n   \\]\n   \\[\n   h = \\sqrt{32} = 4\\sqrt{2}\n   \\]\n\nTherefore, the height of the cone is \\(4\\sqrt{2}\\) inches.'
"To solve this problem, we can use the Angle Bisector Theorem, which states that if a point \\( D \\) lies on \\( \\overline{BC} \\) such that \\( \\overline{AD} \\) is the angle bisector of \\( \\angle BAC \\), then:\n\n\\[\n\\frac{BD}{DC} = \\frac{AB}{AC}\n\\]\n\nGiven that \\( \\angle ABC = 90^\\circ \\), \\( AB = 12 \\), and \\( BD = 4 \\), we need to find \\( AC \\).\n\nFirst, let's denote \\( DC = x \\). According to the Angle Bisector Theorem, we have:\n\n\\[\n\\frac{4}{x} = \\frac{12}{AC}\n\\]\n\nSince \\( D \\) lies on \\( \\overline{BC} \\), we know that:\n\n\\[\nBD + DC = BC\n\\]\n\nThus:\n\n\\[\n4 + x = BC\n\\]\n\nSince \\( \\triangle ABC \\) is a right triangle with \\( \\angle ABC = 90^\\circ \\), we can use the Pythagorean theorem:\n\n\\[\nAB^2 + BC^2 = AC^2\n\\]\n\nSubstituting the known values, we have:\n\n\\[\n12^2 + (4 + x)^2 = AC^2\n\\]\n\nNow, let's solve the system of equations. From the Angle Bisector Theorem, we have:\n\n\\[\n4 \\cdot AC = 12 \\cdot x \\quad \\Rightarrow \\quad AC = \\frac{12x}{4} = 3x\n\\]\n\nSubstitute \\( AC = 3x \\) into the Pythagorean theorem equation:\n\n\\[\n12^2 + (4 + x)^2 = (3x)^2\n\\]\n\nSimplify and solve for \\( x \\):\n\n\\[\n144 + (16 + 8x + x^2) = 9x^2\n\\]\n\n\\[\n160 + 8x + x^2 = 9x^2\n\\]\n\nRearrange the equation:\n\n\\[\n8x^2 - 8x - 160 = 0\n\\]\n\nDivide the entire equation by 8:\n\n\\[\nx^2 - x - 20 = 0\n\\]\n\nSolve this quadratic equation using the quadratic formula:\n\n\\[\nx = \\frac{-b \\pm \\sqrt{b^2 - 4ac}}{2a}\n\\]\n\nwhere \\( a = 1 \\), \\( b = -1 \\), and \\( c = -20 \\):\n\n\\[\nx = \\frac{-(-1) \\pm \\sqrt{(-1)^2 - 4 \\cdot 1 \\cdot (-20)}}{2 \\cdot 1}\n\\]\n\n\\[\nx = \\frac{1 \\pm \\sqrt{1 + 80}}{2}\n\\]\n\n\\[\nx = \\frac{1 \\pm \\sqrt{81}}{2}\n\\]\n\n\\[\nx = \\frac{1 \\pm 9}{2}\n\\]\n\nThe possible solutions for \\( x \\) are:\n\n\\[\nx = \\frac{10}{2} = 5 \\quad \\text{or} \\quad x = \\frac{-8}{2} = -4\n\\]\n\nSince \\( x \\) represents a length, it must be positive, so \\( x = 5 \\).\n\nNow, substitute \\( x = 5 \\) back to find \\( AC \\):\n\n\\[\nAC = 3x = 3 \\times 5 = 15\n\\]\n\nThus, the length of \\( AC \\) is \\(\\boxed{15}\\)."
'To find the length of the angle bisector \\( AD \\) in triangle \\( \\triangle ABC \\) with sides \\( AB = 6 \\), \\( BC = 8 \\), and \\( CA = 10 \\), we can use the angle bisector theorem and the angle bisector length formula.\n\nFirst, verify that \\( \\triangle ABC \\) is a right triangle. Since \\( AB^2 + BC^2 = 6^2 + 8^2 = 36 + 64 = 100 = 10^2 = CA^2 \\), \\( \\triangle ABC \\) is indeed a right triangle with \\( \\angle ABC = 90^\\circ \\).\n\nThe angle bisector theorem states that the angle bisector divides the opposite side into segments proportional to the adjacent sides. Therefore, if \\( D \\) is the point on \\( BC \\) such that \\( AD \\) is the angle bisector, then:\n\n\\[\n\\frac{BD}{DC} = \\frac{AB}{AC} = \\frac{6}{10} = \\frac{3}{5}\n\\]\n\nLet \\( BD = 3x \\) and \\( DC = 5x \\). Since \\( BD + DC = BC = 8 \\), we have:\n\n\\[\n3x + 5x = 8 \\implies 8x = 8 \\implies x = 1\n\\]\n\nThus, \\( BD = 3 \\) and \\( DC = 5 \\).\n\nNow, use the angle bisector length formula:\n\n\\[\nAD^2 = AB \\cdot AC \\left(1 - \\frac{BC^2}{(AB + AC)^2}\\right)\n\\]\n\nSubstitute the known values:\n\n\\[\nAD^2 = 6 \\cdot 10 \\left(1 - \\frac{8^2}{(6 + 10)^2}\\right) = 60 \\left(1 - \\frac{64}{256}\\right)\n\\]\n\nSimplify:\n\n\\[\nAD^2 = 60 \\left(1 - \\frac{1}{4}\\right) = 60 \\cdot \\frac{3}{4} = 60 \\cdot 0.75 = 45\n\\]\n\nTherefore, the value of \\( AD^2 \\) is \\(\\boxed{45}\\).'
"To find the midpoint \\( M \\) of segment \\( PR \\), we use the midpoint formula:\n\n\\[\nM = \\left( \\frac{x_1 + x_2}{2}, \\frac{y_1 + y_2}{2} \\right)\n\\]\n\nwhere \\( P = (1, 3) \\) and \\( R = (7, 15) \\). Plugging in these coordinates, we get:\n\n\\[\nM = \\left( \\frac{1 + 7}{2}, \\frac{3 + 15}{2} \\right) = \\left( \\frac{8}{2}, \\frac{18}{2} \\right) = (4, 9)\n\\]\n\nNext, we reflect segment \\( PR \\) over the \\( x \\)-axis. The reflection of a point \\( (x, y) \\) over the \\( x \\)-axis is \\( (x, -y) \\). 'To find the minimum length of the rope, we can visualize the problem by "unwrapping" the cylinder into a rectangle. The height of the cylinder is 12 feet, and the circumference is 2 feet. When the cylinder is unwrapped, it forms a rectangle with a height of 12 feet and a width equal to the circumference, which is 2 feet.\n\nThe rope wraps around the cylinder four times, so it travels a total horizontal distance of \\(4 \\times 2 = 8\\) feet along the unwrapped rectangle. The vertical distance the rope travels is the height of the cylinder, which is 12 feet.\n\nIn the unwrapped rectangle, the path of the rope forms a diagonal from the bottom left corner to a point 8 feet horizontally and 12 feet vertically from the starting point. We can use the Pythagorean theorem to find the length of this diagonal, which represents the length of the rope.\n\nLet \\(L\\) be the length of the rope. Then:\n\n\\[\nL = \\sqrt{(8)^2 + (12)^2} = \\sqrt{64 + 144} = \\sqrt{208}\n\\]\n\nSimplifying \\(\\sqrt{208}\\):\n\n\\[\n\\sqrt{208} = \\sqrt{16 \\times 13} = \\sqrt{16} \\times \\sqrt{13} = 4\\sqrt{13}\n\\]\n\nThus, the minimum length of the rope is \\(\\boxed{4\\sqrt{13}}\\) feet.'
'To find the height of the cylindrical water tank, we can use the formula for the volume of a cylinder:\n\n\\[ V = \\pi r^2 h, \\]\n\nwhere \\( V \\) is the volume, \\( r \\) is the radius of the base, and \\( h \\) is the height of the cylinder.\n\nWe are given:\n- The volume \\( V = 2700\\pi \\) cubic meters,\n- The radius \\( r = 30 \\) meters.\n\nSubstituting these values into the volume formula, we have:\n\n\\[ 2700\\pi = \\pi (30)^2 h. \\]\n\nSimplifying, we get:\n\n\\[ 2700\\pi = \\pi \\times 900 \\times h. \\]\n\nWe can divide both sides by \\(\\pi\\) to eliminate \\(\\pi\\) from the equation:\n\n\\[ 2700 = 900h. \\]\n\nNow, solve for \\( h \\) by dividing both sides by 900:\n\n\\[ h = \\frac{2700}{900} = 3. \\]\n\nTherefore, the height of the tank is \\( \\boxed{3} \\) meters.'
'The surface area of the larger cubic block of wood with side length 10 cm is calculated as follows:\n\n\\[\n\\text{Surface area of larger cube} = 6 \\times (10 \\, \\text{cm})^2 = 6 \\times 100 \\, \\text{cm}^2 = 600 \\, \\text{cm}^2.\n\\]\n\nThe larger cube is cut into smaller cubes with side length 1 cm. The number of smaller cubes is:\n\n\\[\n\\text{Number of smaller cubes} = \\left(\\frac{10 \\, \\text{cm}}{1 \\, \\text{cm}}\\right)^3 = 10^3 = 1000.\n\\]\n\nThe surface area of one smaller cube is:\n\n\\[\n\\text{Surface area of one smaller cube} = 6 \\times (1 \\, \\text{cm})^2 = 6 \\, \\text{cm}^2.\n\\]\n\nThe combined surface area of all the smaller cubes is:\n\n\\[\n\\text{Combined surface area of smaller cubes} = 1000 \\times 6 \\, \\text{cm}^2 = 6000 \\, \\text{cm}^2.\n\\]\n\nThe ratio of the surface area of the larger block to the combined surface area of the smaller blocks is:\n\n\\[\n\\text{Ratio} = \\frac{600 \\, \\text{cm}^2}{6000 \\, \\text{cm}^2} = \\frac{600}{6000} = \\frac{1}{10}.\n\\]\n\nThus, the ratio of the surface area of the larger block to the combined surface area of the smaller blocks is \\(\\boxed{\\frac{1}{10}}\\).'
"To solve this problem, we need to compare the areas of the two circular griddles, since the thickness of the waffles is the same and the amount of flour used is proportional to the area.\n\nFirst, calculate the area of Danny Henry's griddle, which has a diameter of 6 inches. The radius is half of the diameter, so the radius is 3 inches. The area \\( A \\) of a circle is given by the formula:\n\n\\[ A = \\pi r^2 \\]\n\nFor Danny's griddle:\n\n\\[ A_{\\text{Danny}} = \\pi (3)^2 = 9\\pi \\text{ square inches} \\]\n\nNext, calculate the area of Paul Bunyan's griddle, which has a diameter of 24 feet. Convert the diameter to inches (since 1 foot = 12 inches):\n\n\\[ 24 \\text{ feet} = 24 \\times 12 = 288 \\text{ inches} \\]\n\nThe radius is half of the diameter, so the radius is 144 inches. The area of Paul Bunyan's griddle is:\n\n\\[ A_{\\text{Paul}} = \\pi (144)^2 = 20736\\pi \\text{ square inches} \\]\n\nNow, find the ratio of the areas of Paul Bunyan's griddle to Danny Henry's griddle:\n\n\\[ \\text{Ratio} = \\frac{A_{\\text{Paul}}}{A_{\\text{Danny}}} = \\frac{20736\\pi}{9\\pi} = \\frac{20736}{9} = 2304 \\]\n\nSince Danny used half a cup of flour for his waffle, Paul Bunyan would need:\n\n\\[ 2304 \\times \\frac{1}{2} = 1152 \\text{ cups of flour} \\]\n\nTherefore, Paul Bunyan would need 1152 cups of flour for his 24-foot-diameter circular griddle."
'To find the area of the shaded region, we first need to understand the structure of the square and the inner squares formed by joining the midpoints of the sides.\n\n1. **Outer Square**: The square \\(ABCD\\) has a side length of 4 inches. Therefore, its area is:\n   \\[\n   \\text{Area of } ABCD = 4 \\times 4 = 16 \\text{ square inches}\n   \\]\n\n2. **Inner Square**: The inner square is formed by joining the midpoints of the sides of the outer square. Since the side length of the outer square is 4 inches, the distance between the midpoints of any two adjacent sides is half of the diagonal of the square formed by the midpoints.\n\n   The diagonal of the square with side length 4 is:\n   \\[\n   \\text{Diagonal} = 4\\sqrt{2}\n   \\]\n\n   The side length of the inner square is half of this diagonal:\n   \\[\n   \\text{Side length of inner square} = \\frac{4\\sqrt{2}}{2} = 2\\sqrt{2}\n   \\]\n\n   The area of the inner square is:\n   \\[\n   \\text{Area of inner square} = (2\\sqrt{2})^2 = 8 \\text{ square inches}\n   \\]\n\n3. **Shaded Region**: The shaded region consists of four right triangles, each formed by the sides of the outer square and the sides of the inner square. Each triangle is isosceles with legs of length 2 inches (half the side of the outer square).\n\n   The area of one such triangle is:\n   \\[\n   \\text{Area of one triangle} = \\frac{1}{2} \\times 2 \\times 2 = 2 \\text{ square inches}\n   \\]\n\n   Since there are four such triangles, the total area of the shaded region is:\n   \\[\n   \\text{Total shaded area} = 4 \\times 2 = 8 \\text{ square inches}\n   \\]\n\nThus, the area of the shaded region is \\(\\boxed{8}\\) square inches.'
'To find the coordinates of point \\( D \\) in parallelogram \\( ABCD \\), we can use the property that the diagonals of a parallelogram bisect each other. This means that the midpoint of diagonal \\( AC \\) is the same as the midpoint of diagonal \\( BD \\).\n\nFirst, we calculate the midpoint of \\( AC \\):\n\n\\[\n\\text{Midpoint of } AC = \\left( \\frac{-3 + 5}{2}, \\frac{5 + 3}{2} \\right) = \\left( \\frac{2}{2}, \\frac{8}{2} \\right) = (1, 4)\n\\]\n\nNext, let the coordinates of point \\( D \\) be \\( (x, y) \\). The midpoint of \\( BD \\) is:\n\n\\[\n\\text{Midpoint of } BD = \\left( \\frac{7 + x}{2}, \\frac{12 + y}{2} \\right)\n\\]\n\nSince the midpoints of \\( AC \\) and \\( BD \\) are the same, we set them equal:\n\n\\[\n\\left( \\frac{7 + x}{2}, \\frac{12 + y}{2} \\right) = (1, 4)\n\\]\n\nThis gives us two equations:\n\n1. \\(\\frac{7 + x}{2} = 1\\)\n2. \\(\\frac{12 + y}{2} = 4\\)\n\nSolving the first equation:\n\n\\[\n\\frac{7 + x}{2} = 1 \\implies 7 + x = 2 \\implies x = 2 - 7 \\implies x = -5\n\\]\n\nSolving the second equation:\n\n\\[\n\\frac{12 + y}{2} = 4 \\implies 12 + y = 8 \\implies y = 8 - 12 \\implies y = -4\n\\]\n\nTherefore, the coordinates of point \\( D \\) are \\((-5, -4)\\).'
'Since \\( AC = BC \\), triangle \\( \\triangle AOC \\) is isosceles with \\( \\angle OAC = 18^\\circ \\). Therefore, \\( \\angle OCA = 18^\\circ \\) as well.\n\nThe sum of the angles in triangle \\( \\triangle AOC \\) is \\( 180^\\circ \\), so we have:\n\\[\n\\angle AOC = 180^\\circ - \\angle OAC - \\angle OCA = 180^\\circ - 18^\\circ - 18^\\circ = 144^\\circ.\n\\]\n\nSince \\( \\angle AOC \\) is the central angle subtending arc \\( AC \\), and \\( \\angle AOB \\) is the central angle subtending arc \\( AB \\), we need to find the measure of \\( \\angle AOB \\).\n\nThe arc \\( AB \\) is the remaining part of the circle not covered by arc \\( AC \\). Since the total degrees in a circle is \\( 360^\\circ \\), we have:\n\\[\n\\angle AOB = 360^\\circ - \\angle AOC = 360^\\circ - 144^\\circ = 216^\\circ.\n\\]\n\nThus, the measure of \\( \\angle AOB \\) is \\(\\boxed{216^\\circ}\\).'
'To find the shortest possible diagonal of a rectangle with integer side lengths and a perimeter of 26 units, we start by letting the side lengths of the rectangle be \\(a\\) and \\(b\\). The perimeter of the rectangle is given by:\n\n\\[\n2(a + b) = 26\n\\]\n\nSolving for \\(a + b\\), we have:\n\n\\[\na + b = 13\n\\]\n\nThe diagonal \\(d\\) of the rectangle can be found using the Pythagorean theorem:\n\n\\[\nd = \\sqrt{a^2 + b^2}\n\\]\n\nTo minimize \\(d\\), we need to minimize \\(a^2 + b^2\\) under the constraint \\(a + b = 13\\). We can express \\(b\\) in terms of \\(a\\):\n\n\\[\nb = 13 - a\n\\]\n\nSubstituting this into the expression for \\(a^2 + b^2\\), we get:\n\n\\[\na^2 + b^2 = a^2 + (13 - a)^2 = a^2 + (169 - 26a + a^2) = 2a^2 - 26a + 169\n\\]\n\nTo find the minimum value of this quadratic expression, we can complete the square or use the vertex formula. The vertex of a quadratic \\(ax^2 + bx + c\\) is at \\(x = -\\frac{b}{2a}\\). Here, \\(a = 2\\) and \\(b = -26\\), so:\n\n\\[\na = -\\frac{-26}{2 \\times 2} = \\frac{26}{4} = 6.5\n\\]\n\nSince \\(a\\) must be an integer, we check the values \\(a = 6\\) and \\(a = 7\\).\n\n1. If \\(a = 6\\), then \\(b = 13 - 6 = 7\\). The diagonal is:\n\n\\[\nd = \\sqrt{6^2 + 7^2} = \\sqrt{36 + 49} = \\sqrt{85}\n\\]\n\n2. If \\(a = 7\\), then \\(b = 13 - 7 = 6\\). The diagonal is:\n\n\\[\nd = \\sqrt{7^2 + 6^2} = \\sqrt{49 + 36} = \\sqrt{85}\n\\]\n\nIn both cases, the diagonal is \\(\\sqrt{85}\\).\n\nTherefore, the length of the shortest possible diagonal of the rectangle is \\(\\boxed{\\sqrt{85}}\\).'
'To find \\( PD \\), we need to consider the three-dimensional geometry of the problem. We have triangle \\( PAB \\) and square \\( ABCD \\) in perpendicular planes. Given that \\( PA = 3 \\), \\( PB = 4 \\), and \\( AB = 5 \\), we recognize that triangle \\( PAB \\) is a right triangle by the Pythagorean theorem, since \\( PA^2 + PB^2 = AB^2 \\).\n\nSince \\( ABCD \\) is a square with side length \\( AB = 5 \\), the coordinates of the points can be set as follows:\n- Let \\( A = (0, 0, 0) \\).\n- Let \\( B = (5, 0, 0) \\).\n- Let \\( C = (5, 5, 0) \\).\n- Let \\( D = (0, 5, 0) \\).\n\nThe point \\( P \\) is in a plane perpendicular to the plane of the square, and since \\( PAB \\) is a right triangle, we can place \\( P \\) at \\( (0, 0, z) \\) such that the distances \\( PA = 3 \\) and \\( PB = 4 \\) are satisfied.\n\nUsing the distance formula, we have:\n\\[\nPA = \\sqrt{(0 - 0)^2 + (0 - 0)^2 + (z - 0)^2} = 3 \\implies z = 3\n\\]\n\nNow, we need to find \\( PD \\):\n\\[\nPD = \\sqrt{(0 - 0)^2 + (5 - 0)^2 + (3 - 0)^2} = \\sqrt{0 + 25 + 9} = \\sqrt{34}\n\\]\n\nThus, the distance \\( PD \\) is \\(\\boxed{\\sqrt{34}}\\).'
"To solve for \\( m\\angle BAC \\), we need to analyze the given information about the triangles and angles.\n\nWe know that triangles \\( BDC \\) and \\( ACD \\) are isosceles. Let's denote the equal sides of triangle \\( BDC \\) as \\( BD = DC \\) and the equal sides of triangle \\( ACD \\) as \\( AD = DC \\).\n\nGiven that \\( m\\angle ABC = 70^\\circ \\), we can find \\( m\\angle BDC \\) because triangle \\( BDC \\) is isosceles. The angles at the base of an isosceles triangle are equal, so:\n\n\\[\nm\\angle BDC = m\\angle DBC = x\n\\]\n\nSince the sum of angles in triangle \\( BDC \\) is \\( 180^\\circ \\), we have:\n\n\\[\nx + x + m\\angle BCD = 180^\\circ\n\\]\n\n\\[\n2x + m\\angle BCD = 180^\\circ\n\\]\n\nNow, consider triangle \\( ABC \\). We know:\n\n\\[\nm\\angle ABC = 70^\\circ\n\\]\n\nSince \\( m\\angle BCD = m\\angle ABC \\) (because they are vertically opposite angles), we have:\n\n\\[\nm\\angle BCD = 70^\\circ\n\\]\n\nSubstituting this into the equation for triangle \\( BDC \\):\n\n\\[\n2x + 70^\\circ = 180^\\circ\n\\]\n\n\\[\n2x = 110^\\circ\n\\]\n\n\\[\nx = 55^\\circ\n\\]\n\nThus, \\( m\\angle BDC = 55^\\circ \\).\n\nNow, consider triangle \\( ACD \\), which is also isosceles with \\( AD = DC \\). Let \\( m\\angle DAC = m\\angle ACD = y \\).\n\nThe sum of angles in triangle \\( ACD \\) is \\( 180^\\circ \\):\n\n\\[\ny + y + m\\angle ACD = 180^\\circ\n\\]\n\nSince \\( m\\angle ACD = m\\angle BDC = 55^\\circ \\), we have:\n\n\\[\n2y + 55^\\circ = 180^\\circ\n\\]\n\n\\[\n2y = 125^\\circ\n\\]\n\n\\[\ny = 62.5^\\circ\n\\]\n\nTherefore, \\( m\\angle BAC = y = 62.5^\\circ \\).\n\nThus, the measure of \\( m\\angle BAC \\) is \\(\\boxed{62.5^\\circ}\\)."
'To find the length of the median \\( BM \\) in the right triangle \\( \\triangle ABC \\), we first need to determine the length of the hypotenuse \\( AC \\).\n\nGiven:\n- \\( AB = 4\\sqrt{2} \\)\n- \\( BC = 2 \\)\n\nUsing the Pythagorean theorem, we can find \\( AC \\):\n\n\\[\nAC = \\sqrt{(AB)^2 + (BC)^2} = \\sqrt{(4\\sqrt{2})^2 + 2^2} = \\sqrt{32 + 4} = \\sqrt{36} = 6\n\\]\n\nThe median \\( BM \\) to the hypotenuse \\( AC \\) in a right triangle can be calculated using the formula for the median to the hypotenuse:\n\n\\[\nBM = \\frac{1}{2} \\sqrt{2AB^2 + 2BC^2 - AC^2}\n\\]\n\nSubstituting the known values:\n\n\\[\nBM = \\frac{1}{2} \\sqrt{2(4\\sqrt{2})^2 + 2(2)^2 - 6^2}\n\\]\n\nCalculating each term:\n\n\\[\n(4\\sqrt{2})^2 = 32, \\quad 2^2 = 4, \\quad 6^2 = 36\n\\]\n\nSubstitute these into the formula:\n\n\\[\nBM = \\frac{1}{2} \\sqrt{2 \\times 32 + 2 \\times 4 - 36}\n\\]\n\n\\[\nBM = \\frac{1}{2} \\sqrt{64 + 8 - 36}\n\\]\n\n\\[\nBM = \\frac{1}{2} \\sqrt{36}\n\\]\n\n\\[\nBM = \\frac{1}{2} \\times 6 = 3\n\\]\n\nThus, the length of the median \\( BM \\) is \\(\\boxed{3}\\).'
"To find the coordinates of the image of point \\( C \\) after a 90-degree counterclockwise rotation about point \\( B \\), we can follow these steps:\n\n1. **Translate the points so that \\( B \\) is at the origin:**\n   - The coordinates of \\( B \\) are \\( (4, 1) \\).\n   - The coordinates of \\( C \\) are \\( (4, 4) \\).\n   - Translate \\( C \\) by subtracting the coordinates of \\( B \\): \n     \\[\n     C' = (4 - 4, 4 - 1) = (0, 3)\n     \\]\n\n2. **Perform the 90-degree counterclockwise rotation:**\n   - The formula for a 90-degree counterclockwise rotation about the origin is \\( (x, y) \\to (-y, x) \\).\n   - Apply this to \\( C' = (0, 3) \\):\n     \\[\n     C'' = (-3, 0)\n     \\]\n\n3. **Translate back to the original position:**\n   - Add the coordinates of \\( B \\) back to \\( C'' \\):\n     \\[\n     C_{\\text{final}} = (-3 + 4, 0 + 1) = (1, 1)\n     \\]\n\nTherefore, the coordinates of the image of \\( C \\) after the rotation are \\((1, 1)\\)."
'The tangent of an angle \\(\\theta\\) is defined as the ratio of the sine of the angle to the cosine of the angle: \\(\\tan \\theta = \\frac{\\sin \\theta}{\\cos \\theta}\\).\n\nFor \\(\\theta = 180^\\circ\\):\n- \\(\\sin 180^\\circ = 0\\)\n- \\(\\cos 180^\\circ = -1\\)\n\nThus, \\(\\tan 180^\\circ = \\frac{\\sin 180^\\circ}{\\cos 180^\\circ} = \\frac{0}{-1} = 0\\).\n\nTherefore, \\(\\tan 180^\\circ = \\boxed{0}\\).'
'To find the value of \\( r \\), we need to use the formula for the area of a triangle. The vertices of the triangle are \\( O = (0, 0) \\), \\( A = (0, 8) \\), and \\( B = (r, 0) \\).\n\nThe base of the triangle is the distance along the x-axis from \\( O \\) to \\( B \\), which is \\( r \\).\n\nThe height of the triangle is the distance along the y-axis from \\( O \\) to \\( A \\), which is 8.\n\nThe area \\( A \\) of a triangle is given by the formula:\n\n\\[\nA = \\frac{1}{2} \\times \\text{base} \\times \\text{height}\n\\]\n\nSubstituting the known values:\n\n\\[\n40 = \\frac{1}{2} \\times r \\times 8\n\\]\n\nSimplifying the equation:\n\n\\[\n40 = 4r\n\\]\n\nSolving for \\( r \\):\n\n\\[\nr = \\frac{40}{4} = 10\n\\]\n\nThus, the value of \\( r \\) is \\(\\boxed{10}\\).'
"To solve this problem, we need to determine the paths traveled by points A and B as the card is opened to an angle of 45 degrees.\n\nFirst, let's consider point B. When the card is closed, point B is at the top right corner of the card, 6 inches from the fold. As the card opens to 45 degrees, point B travels along the arc of a circle with a radius of 6 inches. The angle of the arc is 45 degrees, which is \\(\\frac{1}{8}\\) of a full circle (since 360 degrees is a full circle). Therefore, the length of the arc traveled by point B is:\n\n\\[\n\\text{Arc length for B} = \\frac{1}{8} \\times 2\\pi \\times 6 = \\frac{12\\pi}{8} = \\frac{3\\pi}{2} \\text{ inches}\n\\]\n\nNext, let's consider point A. Point A is 3 inches from the fold, so it travels along the arc of a circle with a radius of 3 inches. The angle of the arc is also 45 degrees, or \\(\\frac{1}{8}\\) of a full circle. Therefore, the length of the arc traveled by point A is:\n\n\\[\n\\text{Arc length for A} = \\frac{1}{8} \\times 2\\pi \\times 3 = \\frac{6\\pi}{8} = \\frac{3\\pi}{4} \\text{ inches}\n\\]\n\nFinally, we find the difference in the distances traveled by points B and A:\n\n\\[\n\\text{Difference} = \\frac{3\\pi}{2} - \\frac{3\\pi}{4} = \\frac{6\\pi}{4} - \\frac{3\\pi}{4} = \\frac{3\\pi}{4} \\text{ inches}\n\\]\n\nThus, point B travels through \\(\\frac{3\\pi}{4}\\) more inches than point A. The answer is \\(\\boxed{\\frac{3\\pi}{4}}\\)."
'To solve this problem, we need to perform two transformations on the point \\((-3, 2)\\).\n\n1. **Rotation 90 clockwise around the origin:**\n\n   When a point \\((x, y)\\) is rotated 90 clockwise around the origin, its new coordinates become \\((y, -x)\\).\n\n   Applying this to the point \\((-3, 2)\\), we get:\n   \\[\n   B = (2, 3)\n   \\]\n\n2. **Reflection in the line \\(y = x\\):**\n\n   When a point \\((x, y)\\) is reflected over the line \\(y = x\\), its coordinates are swapped, resulting in the new coordinates \\((y, x)\\).\n\n   Applying this to the point \\(B = (2, 3)\\), we get:\n   \\[\n   C = (3, 2)\n   \\]\n\nTherefore, the coordinates of point \\(C\\) are \\((3, 2)\\).'
'To find the length of \\( HE \\), we can use the properties of the orthocenter and the altitudes in triangle \\( ABC \\).\n\nGiven:\n- \\( BD = 5 \\)\n- \\( CD = 9 \\)\n- \\( CE = \\frac{42}{5} \\)\n\nFirst, we need to find the length of \\( BE \\). Since \\( E \\) is the foot of the altitude from \\( B \\) to \\( AC \\), we can use the Pythagorean theorem in triangle \\( BEC \\):\n\n\\[\nBE^2 + CE^2 = BC^2\n\\]\n\nWe need to find \\( BC \\) first. Since \\( D \\) is the foot of the altitude from \\( A \\) to \\( BC \\), we have:\n\n\\[\nBD + DC = BC \\implies 5 + 9 = 14\n\\]\n\nSo, \\( BC = 14 \\).\n\nNow, substitute the known values into the Pythagorean theorem:\n\n\\[\nBE^2 + \\left(\\frac{42}{5}\\right)^2 = 14^2\n\\]\n\nCalculate \\( \\left(\\frac{42}{5}\\right)^2 \\):\n\n\\[\n\\left(\\frac{42}{5}\\right)^2 = \\frac{1764}{25}\n\\]\n\nSubstitute back:\n\n\\[\nBE^2 + \\frac{1764}{25} = 196\n\\]\n\nConvert 196 to a fraction with a denominator of 25:\n\n\\[\n196 = \\frac{4900}{25}\n\\]\n\nNow solve for \\( BE^2 \\):\n\n\\[\nBE^2 = \\frac{4900}{25} - \\frac{1764}{25} = \\frac{3136}{25}\n\\]\n\nThus, \\( BE = \\frac{\\sqrt{3136}}{5} = \\frac{56}{5} \\).\n\nNow, we use the property of the orthocenter: the segments from the orthocenter to the vertices are related to the segments from the orthocenter to the feet of the altitudes. Specifically, in triangle \\( ABC \\), the segment \\( HE \\) is related to \\( BE \\) by:\n\n\\[\nHE = BE - CE\n\\]\n\nSubstitute the known values:\n\n\\[\nHE = \\frac{56}{5} - \\frac{42}{5} = \\frac{14}{5}\n\\]\n\nThus, the length of \\( HE \\) is \\(\\boxed{\\frac{14}{5}}\\).'
'To find \\(\\angle AHB\\), we first need to determine \\(\\angle ACB\\) in \\(\\triangle ABC\\). Since the sum of the angles in a triangle is \\(180^\\circ\\), we have:\n\n\\[\n\\angle ACB = 180^\\circ - \\angle BAC - \\angle ABC = 180^\\circ - 54^\\circ - 52^\\circ = 74^\\circ.\n\\]\n\nNext, we use the fact that the orthocenter \\(H\\) of \\(\\triangle ABC\\) is the intersection of the altitudes. The key property here is that \\(\\angle AHB = 180^\\circ - \\angle ACB\\).\n\nTherefore, we have:\n\n\\[\n\\angle AHB = 180^\\circ - 74^\\circ = 106^\\circ.\n\\]\n\nThus, \\(\\angle AHB\\) is \\(\\boxed{106^\\circ}\\).'
'To determine the possible lengths for the third side of the triangle, we need to apply the triangle inequality theorem. The triangle inequality states that the sum of the lengths of any two sides of a triangle must be greater than the length of the third side.\n\nGiven two sides of the triangle are 2 inches and 4 inches, let \\( x \\) be the length of the third side. The triangle inequalities are:\n\n1. \\( 2 + 4 > x \\) which simplifies to \\( x < 6 \\).\n2. \\( 2 + x > 4 \\) which simplifies to \\( x > 2 \\).\n3. \\( 4 + x > 2 \\) which is always true since \\( x \\) is positive.\n\nCombining these inequalities, we find that the possible integer values for \\( x \\) are 3, 4, and 5.\n\nNow, we need to determine the probability that a cube with faces numbered 1 through 6 shows a number that could be the length of the third side. The possible outcomes for the cube are 1, 2, 3, 4, 5, and 6. The favorable outcomes are 3, 4, and 5.\n\nThus, the probability is the number of favorable outcomes divided by the total number of outcomes:\n\n\\[\n\\text{Probability} = \\frac{\\text{Number of favorable outcomes}}{\\text{Total number of outcomes}} = \\frac{3}{6} = \\frac{1}{2}\n\\]\n\nTherefore, the probability that the number showing on top could be the number of inches in the length of the third side of the triangle is \\(\\boxed{\\frac{1}{2}}\\).'
'To find the volume of the cone formed by rotating an equilateral triangle about its altitude, we first need to determine the dimensions of the cone: the radius of the base and the height.\n\n1. **Find the height of the equilateral triangle:**\n\n   An equilateral triangle with side length \\( s = 12 \\) cm has an altitude that can be found using the Pythagorean theorem. The altitude divides the triangle into two 30-60-90 right triangles. In a 30-60-90 triangle, the ratio of the sides opposite the 30, 60, and 90 angles is \\( 1 : \\sqrt{3} : 2 \\).\n\n   The altitude \\( h \\) is the side opposite the 60 angle, and the hypotenuse is the side of the equilateral triangle, which is 12 cm. Therefore, the altitude is:\n\n   \\[\n   h = \\frac{\\sqrt{3}}{2} \\times 12 = 6\\sqrt{3} \\text{ cm}\n   \\]\n\n2. **Find the radius of the base of the cone:**\n\n   The radius \\( r \\) of the base of the cone is half the side length of the equilateral triangle, as the altitude bisects the base. Therefore:\n\n   \\[\n   r = \\frac{12}{2} = 6 \\text{ cm}\n   \\]\n\n3. **Calculate the volume of the cone:**\n\n   The volume \\( V \\) of a cone is given by the formula:\n\n   \\[\n   V = \\frac{1}{3} \\pi r^2 h\n   \\]\n\n   Substituting the values we found:\n\n   \\[\n   V = \\frac{1}{3} \\pi (6)^2 (6\\sqrt{3})\n   \\]\n\n   \\[\n   V = \\frac{1}{3} \\pi \\times 36 \\times 6\\sqrt{3}\n   \\]\n\n   \\[\n   V = \\frac{1}{3} \\times 216\\sqrt{3} \\pi\n   \\]\n\n   \\[\n   V = 72\\sqrt{3} \\pi\n   \\]\n\nThus, the volume of the cone is \\( 72\\sqrt{3} \\pi \\) cubic centimeters.'
'To find the area of triangle \\(ABC\\) with vertices \\(A(-4, -1)\\), \\(B(2, -1)\\), and \\(C(1, 3)\\), we can use the formula for the area of a triangle given its vertices \\((x_1, y_1)\\), \\((x_2, y_2)\\), and \\((x_3, y_3)\\):\n\n\\[\n\\text{Area} = \\frac{1}{2} \\left| x_1(y_2-y_3) + x_2(y_3-y_1) + x_3(y_1-y_2) \\right|\n\\]\n\nSubstituting the coordinates of the vertices into the formula:\n\n\\[\n\\text{Area} = \\frac{1}{2} \\left| (-4)((-1) - 3) + 2(3 - (-1)) + 1((-1) - (-1)) \\right|\n\\]\n\nSimplifying each term:\n\n- The first term: \\((-4)((-1) - 3) = (-4)(-4) = 16\\)\n- The second term: \\(2(3 - (-1)) = 2(3 + 1) = 2 \\times 4 = 8\\)\n- The third term: \\(1((-1) - (-1)) = 1 \\times 0 = 0\\)\n\nAdding these values together:\n\n\\[\n\\text{Area} = \\frac{1}{2} \\left| 16 + 8 + 0 \\right| = \\frac{1}{2} \\times 24 = 12\n\\]\n\nThus, the area of triangle \\(ABC\\) is \\(\\boxed{12}\\) square units.'
'Since triangles \\(ABC\\) and \\(DEF\\) are congruent isosceles right triangles, the inscribed squares in both triangles will have the same relative position and size in relation to the triangles. Therefore, the area of the square inscribed in triangle \\(DEF\\) will be the same as the area of the square inscribed in triangle \\(ABC\\).\n\nGiven that the area of the square inscribed in triangle \\(ABC\\) is 15 square centimeters, the area of the square inscribed in triangle \\(DEF\\) is also \\(\\boxed{15}\\) square centimeters.'
'Given that \\(\\triangle ABC \\sim \\triangle PAQ\\) and \\(\\triangle ABQ \\sim \\triangle QCP\\), we need to find \\(m\\angle PQC\\).\n\nSince \\(\\triangle ABC \\sim \\triangle PAQ\\), the corresponding angles are equal. Therefore, \\(m\\angle BAC = m\\angle PAQ = 70^\\circ\\).\n\nSimilarly, since \\(\\triangle ABQ \\sim \\triangle QCP\\), the corresponding angles are equal. Therefore, \\(m\\angle ABQ = m\\angle QCP\\).\n\nWe know that the sum of angles in a triangle is \\(180^\\circ\\). In \\(\\triangle PAQ\\), we have:\n\\[\nm\\angle PAQ + m\\angle APQ + m\\angle AQP = 180^\\circ\n\\]\nSubstituting \\(m\\angle PAQ = 70^\\circ\\), we get:\n\\[\n70^\\circ + m\\angle APQ + m\\angle AQP = 180^\\circ\n\\]\n\\[\nm\\angle APQ + m\\angle AQP = 110^\\circ\n\\]\n\nIn \\(\\triangle QCP\\), we have:\n\\[\nm\\angle QCP + m\\angle CPQ + m\\angle PQC = 180^\\circ\n\\]\nSince \\(m\\angle QCP = m\\angle ABQ\\), and from the similarity \\(\\triangle ABQ \\sim \\triangle QCP\\), we have:\n\\[\nm\\angle ABQ = m\\angle QCP\n\\]\n\nNow, consider \\(\\triangle ABQ\\):\n\\[\nm\\angle ABQ + m\\angle BAQ + m\\angle AQP = 180^\\circ\n\\]\nSubstituting \\(m\\angle BAQ = 70^\\circ\\), we get:\n\\[\nm\\angle ABQ + 70^\\circ + m\\angle AQP = 180^\\circ\n\\]\n\\[\nm\\angle ABQ + m\\angle AQP = 110^\\circ\n\\]\n\nSince \\(m\\angle AQP = m\\angle CPQ\\) (as they are corresponding angles in similar triangles \\(\\triangle PAQ\\) and \\(\\triangle QCP\\)), we have:\n\\[\nm\\angle ABQ = m\\angle QCP\n\\]\n\nFinally, in \\(\\triangle QCP\\):\n\\[\nm\\angle QCP + m\\angle CPQ + m\\angle PQC = 180^\\circ\n\\]\nSubstituting \\(m\\angle QCP = m\\angle ABQ\\) and \\(m\\angle CPQ = m\\angle AQP\\), we have:\n\\[\nm\\angle ABQ + m\\angle AQP + m\\angle PQC = 180^\\circ\n\\]\n\\[\n110^\\circ + m\\angle PQC = 180^\\circ\n\\]\n\\[\nm\\angle PQC = 70^\\circ\n\\]\n\nThus, \\(m\\angle PQC = \\boxed{70^\\circ}\\).'

'To solve this problem, we need to determine the distance between the two points where the semicircle touches the line above as it rocks back and forth.\n\n1. **Understanding the Setup:**\n   - The semicircle has a radius of 8 cm.\n   - The distance between the line on which the semicircle sits and the line above is 12 cm.\n\n2. **Position of the Semicircle:**\n   - When the semicircle rocks to one side and touches the line above, the center of the semicircle is vertically 8 cm below the line above (since the radius is 8 cm).\n   - Therefore, the center of the semicircle is 12 - 8 = 4 cm above the line on which it sits.\n\n3. **Geometry of the Situation:**\n   - The semicircle rocks such that its center moves along a horizontal line 4 cm above the base line.\n   - When the semicircle touches the line above, the topmost point of the semicircle is at the line above, and the center is directly below this point.\n\n4. **Calculating the Distance Between Touch Points:**\n   - The semicircle will touch the line above at two points, one on each side of the center.\n   - The horizontal distance between these two points is the diameter of the semicircle, which is 2 times the radius.\n   - Therefore, the distance between the two points is 2 * 8 cm = 16 cm.\n\n5. **Converting to Millimeters:**\n   - Since 1 cm = 10 mm, the distance in millimeters is 16 cm * 10 mm/cm = 160 mm.\n\nThus, the distance between the two points where the semicircle touches the line above is 160 millimeters. Therefore, the answer is \\(\\boxed{160}\\).'
'To find the surface area of the cone, we need to calculate both the lateral surface area and the base area.\n\n1. **Dimensions of the Cone:**\n   - The isosceles right triangle has legs of length 2. When rotated about one of its legs, the leg becomes the height of the cone, \\( h = 2 \\).\n   - The hypotenuse of the triangle becomes the slant height \\( l \\) of the cone. Using the Pythagorean theorem, the hypotenuse is:\n     \\[\n     l = \\sqrt{2^2 + 2^2} = \\sqrt{8} = 2\\sqrt{2}\n     \\]\n   - The other leg becomes the radius \\( r \\) of the base of the cone, \\( r = 2 \\).\n\n2. **Surface Area of the Cone:**\n   - The lateral surface area \\( A_{\\text{lateral}} \\) of the cone is given by:\n     \\[\n     A_{\\text{lateral}} = \\pi r l = \\pi \\times 2 \\times 2\\sqrt{2} = 4\\pi\\sqrt{2}\n     \\]\n   - The base area \\( A_{\\text{base}} \\) of the cone is:\n     \\[\n     A_{\\text{base}} = \\pi r^2 = \\pi \\times 2^2 = 4\\pi\n     \\]\n   - The total surface area \\( A_{\\text{total}} \\) of the cone is the sum of the lateral surface area and the base area:\n     \\[\n     A_{\\text{total}} = A_{\\text{lateral}} + A_{\\text{base}} = 4\\pi\\sqrt{2} + 4\\pi\n     \\]\n\n3. **Expressing the Surface Area as \\(\\pi\\) times a Number:**\n   - Factor out \\(\\pi\\) from the total surface area:\n     \\[\n     A_{\\text{total}} = \\pi (4\\sqrt{2} + 4)\n     \\]\n\nThus, the surface area of the cone is \\(\\pi\\) times the number \\(4\\sqrt{2} + 4\\).'

We are given that the distance \\( QR = 3 \\).\n\nFirst, let's find the radius of the larger circle. The point \\( P(8, 6) \\) is on the larger circle, so the radius of the larger circle is the distance from the origin to \\( P \\). We calculate this using the distance formula:\n\n\\[\n\\text{Radius of larger circle} = \\sqrt{8^2 + 6^2} = \\sqrt{64 + 36} = \\sqrt{100} = 10\n\\]\n\nThus, the radius of the larger circle is 10.\n\nNext, we know that \\( QR = 3 \\), where \\( Q \\) is a point on the smaller circle and \\( R \\) is a point on the larger circle. "To solve this problem, we need to perform two transformations on point \\( Q(2, 7) \\).\n\n1. **Rotation 270 clockwise around point \\( M(2, 5) \\):**\n\n   A 270 clockwise rotation is equivalent to a 90 counterclockwise rotation. To perform this rotation, we first translate the point \\( Q \\) so that \\( M \\) becomes the origin. The translation vector is \\( (-2, -5) \\), so the translated coordinates of \\( Q \\) are:\n   \\[\n   Q' = (2 - 2, 7 - 5) = (0, 2)\n   \\]\n\n   Now, we apply a 90 counterclockwise rotation to \\( Q' \\). The rotation formula for 90 counterclockwise is:\n   \\[\n   (x, y) \\rightarrow (-y, x)\n   \\]\n   Applying this to \\( Q' \\):\n   \\[\n   Q'' = (0, 2) \\rightarrow (-2, 0)\n   \\]\n\n   Finally, we translate back by adding the translation vector \\( (2, 5) \\):\n   \\[\n   Q''' = (-2 + 2, 0 + 5) = (0, 5)\n   \\]\n\n2. **Reflection across the line \\( x = 1 \\):**\n\n   To reflect a point across the line \\( x = 1 \\), we find the horizontal distance from the point to the line and then move the same distance to the other side of the line. The x-coordinate of \\( Q''' \\) is 0, and the line \\( x = 1 \\) is 1 unit to the right. Reflecting across \\( x = 1 \\):\n   \\[\n   x_{\\text{new}} = 1 + (1 - 0) = 2\n   \\]\n   The y-coordinate remains unchanged, so the final coordinates of the reflected point are:\n   \\[\n   Q_{\\text{final}} = (2, 5)\n   \\]\n\nThus, the coordinates of the final image of point \\( Q \\) are \\((2, 5)\\)."
'To find the ratio of the volume of the sphere to the volume of the cone, we first need to determine the radius of the inscribed sphere.\n\nThe formula for the radius \\( r \\) of a sphere inscribed in a cone with height \\( h \\) and base radius \\( R \\) is given by:\n\n\\[\nr = \\frac{R \\cdot h}{\\sqrt{R^2 + h^2} + R}\n\\]\n\nFor this problem, the cone has a height \\( h = 4 \\) and base radius \\( R = 3 \\). Plugging these values into the formula, we get:\n\n\\[\nr = \\frac{3 \\cdot 4}{\\sqrt{3^2 + 4^2} + 3} = \\frac{12}{\\sqrt{9 + 16} + 3} = \\frac{12}{\\sqrt{25} + 3} = \\frac{12}{5 + 3} = \\frac{12}{8} = \\frac{3}{2}\n\\]\n\nNow, we calculate the volume of the sphere using the formula for the volume of a sphere, \\( V = \\frac{4}{3} \\pi r^3 \\):\n\n\\[\nV_{\\text{sphere}} = \\frac{4}{3} \\pi \\left(\\frac{3}{2}\\right)^3 = \\frac{4}{3} \\pi \\cdot \\frac{27}{8} = \\frac{4 \\cdot 27}{3 \\cdot 8} \\pi = \\frac{108}{24} \\pi = \\frac{9}{2} \\pi\n\\]\n\nNext, we calculate the volume of the cone using the formula for the volume of a cone, \\( V = \\frac{1}{3} \\pi R^2 h \\):\n\n\\[\nV_{\\text{cone}} = \\frac{1}{3} \\pi (3)^2 (4) = \\frac{1}{3} \\pi \\cdot 9 \\cdot 4 = \\frac{36}{3} \\pi = 12 \\pi\n\\]\n\nFinally, we find the ratio of the volume of the sphere to the volume of the cone:\n\n\\[\n\\text{Ratio} = \\frac{V_{\\text{sphere}}}{V_{\\text{cone}}} = \\frac{\\frac{9}{2} \\pi}{12 \\pi} = \\frac{9}{2} \\cdot \\frac{1}{12} = \\frac{9}{24} = \\frac{3}{8}\n\\]\n\nThus, the ratio of the volume of the sphere to the volume of the cone is \\(\\boxed{\\frac{3}{8}}\\).'
'To find the volume of the cube, we need to determine the side length of the cube that can contain a sphere with a radius of 3 inches. The sphere is inscribed in the cube, meaning the diameter of the sphere is equal to the side length of the cube.\n\nThe diameter of the sphere is twice the radius, so:\n\n\\[\n\\text{Diameter of the sphere} = 2 \\times 3 = 6 \\text{ inches}\n\\]\n\nSince the sphere is inscribed in the cube, the side length of the cube is also 6 inches. The volume \\( V \\) of a cube with side length \\( s \\) is given by:\n\n\\[\nV = s^3\n\\]\n\nSubstituting the side length of the cube:\n\n\\[\nV = 6^3 = 216 \\text{ cubic inches}\n\\]\n\nTherefore, the volume of the cube is \\(\\boxed{216}\\) cubic inches.'
"To find the area of the equiangular octagon with side lengths 1, 2, 2, 4, 1, 2, 2, 4, we can use the fact that the octagon is equiangular, meaning all its internal angles are equal. This allows us to decompose the octagon into simpler shapes, such as triangles and rectangles, and calculate their areas separately.\n\nFirst, let's label the vertices of the octagon as \\( A, B, C, D, E, F, G, H \\) in order, with the given side lengths:\n\n- \\( AB = 1 \\)\n- \\( BC = 2 \\)\n- \\( CD = 2 \\)\n- \\( DE = 4 \\)\n- \\( EF = 1 \\)\n- \\( FG = 2 \\)\n- \\( GH = 2 \\)\n- \\( HA = 4 \\)\n\nSince the octagon is equiangular, each internal angle is \\( \\frac{(8-2) \\times 180^\\circ}{8} = 135^\\circ \\).\n\nTo find the area, we can divide the octagon into triangles and rectangles. One way to do this is to draw diagonals from one vertex to non-adjacent vertices, creating triangles and possibly other polygons that are easier to handle.\n\nLet's choose vertex \\( A \\) and draw diagonals \\( AC, AD, \\) and \\( AE \\). This will divide the octagon into the following parts:\n\n1. Triangle \\( \\triangle ABC \\)\n2. Triangle \\( \\triangle ACD \\)\n3. Quadrilateral \\( ACDE \\)\n4. Triangle \\( \\triangle AEF \\)\n5. Triangle \\( \\triangle AFGH \\)\n\nNow, let's calculate the area of each part:\n\n1. **Triangle \\( \\triangle ABC \\):**\n   - This is a right triangle with legs \\( AB = 1 \\) and \\( BC = 2 \\).\n   - Area = \\( \\frac{1}{2} \\times 1 \\times 2 = 1 \\).\n\n2. **Triangle \\( \\triangle ACD \\):**\n   - This is an isosceles triangle with \\( AC = 2 \\) and \\( CD = 2 \\).\n   - Since \\( \\angle ACD = 135^\\circ \\), we can use the formula for the area of a triangle with two sides and the included angle:\n   - Area = \\( \\frac{1}{2} \\times 2 \\times 2 \\times \\sin(135^\\circ) = 2 \\times \\frac{\\sqrt{2}}{2} = \\sqrt{2} \\).\n\n3. **Quadrilateral \\( ACDE \\):**\n   - This can be split into two triangles, \\( \\triangle ACD \\) and \\( \\triangle ADE \\).\n   - We already calculated the area of \\( \\triangle ACD \\).\n   - For \\( \\triangle ADE \\), use the same method as above:\n   - \\( DE = 4 \\), \\( \\angle ADE = 135^\\circ \\).\n   - Area = \\( \\frac{1}{2} \\times 2 \\times 4 \\times \\sin(135^\\circ) = 4 \\times \\frac{\\sqrt{2}}{2} = 2\\sqrt{2} \\).\n\n4. **Triangle \\( \\triangle AEF \\):**\n   - This is a right triangle with legs \\( AE = 4 \\) and \\( EF = 1 \\).\n   - Area = \\( \\frac{1}{2} \\times 4 \\times 1 = 2 \\).\n\n5. **Triangle \\( \\triangle AFGH \\):**\n   - This is a right triangle with legs \\( AF = 2 \\) and \\( GH = 2 \\).\n   - Area = \\( \\frac{1}{2} \\times 2 \\times 2 = 2 \\).\n\nFinally, sum the areas of all these parts to find the total area of the octagon:\n\nTotal Area = \\( 1 + \\sqrt{2} + 2\\sqrt{2} + 2 + 2 = 5 + 3\\sqrt{2} \\).\n\nTherefore, the area of the equiangular octagon is \\( 5 + 3\\sqrt{2} \\)."
'To solve this problem, we need to determine the vertical distance from the horizontal plane to the top of the larger sphere, which has a radius of 2.\n\nFirst, consider the centers of the three smaller spheres. Since they are mutually tangent and each has a radius of 1, the centers form an equilateral triangle with side length 2 (since the distance between the centers of two tangent spheres is the sum of their radii, which is 1 + 1 = 2).\n\nThe center of the larger sphere, which has a radius of 2, is tangent to each of the three smaller spheres. Therefore, the center of the larger sphere is directly above the centroid of the equilateral triangle formed by the centers of the three smaller spheres.\n\nThe height from the plane to the center of one of the smaller spheres is 1 (the radius of the smaller sphere). The height from the plane to the centroid of the equilateral triangle is the same as the height from the plane to the center of one of the smaller spheres, which is 1.\n\nNext, we need to find the height from the centroid of the equilateral triangle to the center of the larger sphere. This distance is the radius of the larger sphere minus the radius of the smaller sphere, which is 2 - 1 = 1.\n\nTherefore, the total distance from the plane to the center of the larger sphere is 1 (from the plane to the centroid) + 1 (from the centroid to the center of the larger sphere) = 2.\n\nFinally, the distance from the plane to the top of the larger sphere is the distance from the plane to the center of the larger sphere plus the radius of the larger sphere, which is 2 + 2 = 4.\n\nThus, the distance from the plane to the top of the larger sphere is \\(\\boxed{4}\\).'

'To find the height of the second cylinder, we start by calculating the volume of the first cylinder. The formula for the volume of a cylinder is:\n\n\\[ V = \\pi r^2 h \\]\n\nFor the first cylinder, the radius \\( r_1 = 6 \\) inches and the height \\( h_1 = 12 \\) inches. "To find the area of the square, we first need to determine the side length of the square. We can do this by calculating the distance between any two adjacent vertices.\n\nLet's calculate the distance between the points \\((-2, 2)\\) and \\((2, -2)\\):\n\nThe distance \\(d\\) between two points \\((x_1, y_1)\\) and \\((x_2, y_2)\\) is given by the formula:\n\\[\nd = \\sqrt{(x_2 - x_1)^2 + (y_2 - y_1)^2}\n\\]\n\nSubstituting the coordinates of the points \\((-2, 2)\\) and \\((2, -2)\\):\n\\[\nd = \\sqrt{(2 - (-2))^2 + (-2 - 2)^2} = \\sqrt{(2 + 2)^2 + (-2 - 2)^2} = \\sqrt{4^2 + (-4)^2} = \\sqrt{16 + 16} = \\sqrt{32} = 4\\sqrt{2}\n\\]\n\nThe side length of the square is \\(4\\sqrt{2}\\).\n\nThe area \\(A\\) of a square with side length \\(s\\) is given by:\n\\[\nA = s^2\n\\]\n\nSubstituting the side length:\n\\[\nA = (4\\sqrt{2})^2 = 16 \\times 2 = 32\n\\]\n\nTherefore, the area of the square is \\(\\boxed{32}\\) square units."
"To solve this problem, we need to find the length of \\(\\overline{GF}\\) in the given rectangle \\(ABCD\\).\n\nFirst, let's establish the coordinates of the points based on the given dimensions of the rectangle:\n- \\(A = (0, 9)\\)\n- \\(B = (8, 9)\\)\n- \\(C = (8, 0)\\)\n- \\(D = (0, 0)\\)\n\nThe point \\(H\\) is on \\(\\overline{BC}\\) with \\(BH = 6\\), so \\(H = (8, 3)\\).\n\nThe point \\(E\\) is on \\(\\overline{AD}\\) with \\(DE = 4\\), so \\(E = (4, 0)\\).\n\nNext, we find the equation of line \\(EC\\). The slope of \\(EC\\) is:\n\\[\n\\text{slope of } EC = \\frac{0 - 0}{4 - 8} = \\frac{0}{-4} = 0\n\\]\nThus, the equation of line \\(EC\\) is \\(y = 0\\).\n\nNow, we find the equation of line \\(AH\\). The slope of \\(AH\\) is:\n\\[\n\\text{slope of } AH = \\frac{3 - 9}{8 - 0} = \\frac{-6}{8} = -\\frac{3}{4}\n\\]\nThe equation of line \\(AH\\) is:\n\\[\ny - 9 = -\\frac{3}{4}(x - 0) \\implies y = -\\frac{3}{4}x + 9\n\\]\n\nTo find point \\(G\\), we solve the system of equations for lines \\(EC\\) and \\(AH\\):\n\\[\ny = 0 \\quad \\text{and} \\quad y = -\\frac{3}{4}x + 9\n\\]\nSetting the equations equal gives:\n\\[\n0 = -\\frac{3}{4}x + 9 \\implies \\frac{3}{4}x = 9 \\implies x = 12\n\\]\nThus, \\(G = (12, 0)\\).\n\nNow, we need to find point \\(F\\) such that \\(\\overline{GF} \\perp \\overline{AF}\\).
