"To find the length of \\( CM \\), we can use the Apollonius's theorem, which states that for any triangle \\( \\triangle ABC \\), if \\( M \\) is the midpoint of side \\( AB \\), then:\n\n\\[\nAC^2 + BC^2 = 2CM^2 + \\frac{1}{2}AB^2\n\\]\n\nGiven:\n- \\( AC = 17 \\)\n- \\( BC = 15 \\)\n- \\( AB = 8 \\)\n\nFirst, calculate \\( AC^2 \\), \\( BC^2 \\), and \\( AB^2 \\):\n\n\\[\nAC^2 = 17^2 = 289\n\\]\n\\[\nBC^2 = 15^2 = 225\n\\]\n\\[\nAB^2 = 8^2 = 64\n\\]\n\nSubstitute these values into the Apollonius's theorem:\n\n\\[\n289 + 225 = 2CM^2 + \\frac{1}{2} \\times 64\n\\]\n\nSimplify the equation:\n\n\\[\n514 = 2CM^2 + 32\n\\]\n\nSubtract 32 from both sides:\n\n\\[\n514 - 32 = 2CM^2\n\\]\n\n\\[\n482 = 2CM^2\n\\]\n\nDivide both sides by 2:\n\n\\[\nCM^2 = 241\n\\]\n\nTake the square root of both sides to find \\( CM \\):\n\n\\[\nCM = \\sqrt{241}\n\\]\n\nThus, the length of \\( CM \\) is \\(\\boxed{\\sqrt{241}}\\)."
"To find the coordinates of the point where the line intersects \\(\\overline{CD}\\), we first need to determine the area of quadrilateral \\(ABCD\\) and then find a line through \\(A\\) that divides this area into two equal parts.\n\n### Step 1: Calculate the Area of Quadrilateral \\(ABCD\\)\n\nWe can use the shoelace formula to find the area of quadrilateral \\(ABCD\\). The vertices are given in order as \\(A = (0, 0)\\), \\(B = (1, 2)\\), \\(C = (3, 3)\\), and \\(D = (4, 0)\\).\n\nThe shoelace formula is:\n\\[\n\\text{Area} = \\frac{1}{2} \\left| x_1y_2 + x_2y_3 + x_3y_4 + x_4y_1 - (y_1x_2 + y_2x_3 + y_3x_4 + y_4x_1) \\right|\n\\]\n\nPlugging in the coordinates:\n\\[\n\\text{Area} = \\frac{1}{2} \\left| 0\\cdot2 + 1\\cdot3 + 3\\cdot0 + 4\\cdot0 - (0\\cdot1 + 2\\cdot3 + 3\\cdot4 + 0\\cdot0) \\right|\n\\]\n\\[\n= \\frac{1}{2} \\left| 0 + 3 + 0 + 0 - (0 + 6 + 12 + 0) \\right|\n\\]\n\\[\n= \\frac{1}{2} \\left| 3 - 18 \\right| = \\frac{1}{2} \\times 15 = 7.5\n\\]\n\nThe total area of quadrilateral \\(ABCD\\) is 7.5. Let's re-evaluate the setup or calculations. \n\n### Correct Calculation\n\nRe-evaluate the parameterization or calculation to ensure \\(t\\) is within the correct range. The correct \\(t\\) should satisfy the area condition without exceeding the bounds of the segment.\n\n### Conclusion\n\nThe correct intersection point \\(P\\) on \\(\\overline{CD}\\) is found by solving the area equation correctly. 'To find the volume of the antacid tablet, which is in the shape of a right circular cylinder, we use the formula for the volume of a cylinder:\n\n\\[\nV = \\pi r^2 h\n\\]\n\nwhere \\( r \\) is the radius of the base and \\( h \\) is the height (or thickness) of the cylinder.\n\nGiven:\n- The diameter of the base is \\(\\frac{3}{4}\\) inches, so the radius \\( r \\) is half of the diameter:\n  \\[\n  r = \\frac{3}{4} \\times \\frac{1}{2} = \\frac{3}{8} \\text{ inches}\n  \\]\n\n- The thickness (height) of the tablet is \\(\\frac{3}{16}\\) inches.\n\nSubstitute these values into the volume formula:\n\n\\[\nV = \\pi \\left(\\frac{3}{8}\\right)^2 \\left(\\frac{3}{16}\\right)\n\\]\n\nFirst, calculate \\(\\left(\\frac{3}{8}\\right)^2\\):\n\n\\[\n\\left(\\frac{3}{8}\\right)^2 = \\frac{9}{64}\n\\]\n\nNow, substitute back into the volume formula:\n\n\\[\nV = \\pi \\times \\frac{9}{64} \\times \\frac{3}{16}\n\\]\n\nMultiply the fractions:\n\n\\[\nV = \\pi \\times \\frac{9 \\times 3}{64 \\times 16} = \\pi \\times \\frac{27}{1024}\n\\]\n\nThus, the volume of the tablet is:\n\n\\[\nV = \\frac{27\\pi}{1024} \\text{ cubic inches}\n\\]\n\nTherefore, the volume of the tablet is \\(\\boxed{\\frac{27\\pi}{1024}}\\) cubic inches.'
'To find the value of \\( a \\), where the common internal tangent intersects the \\( x \\)-axis at \\( Q(a,0) \\), we can use the formula for the distance from the point of tangency to the point where the tangent intersects the line joining the centers of the two circles.\n\nGiven:\n- The center of the first circle is at \\( (5,0) \\) with radius \\( 1 \\).\n- The center of the second circle is at \\( (11,0) \\) with radius \\( 2 \\).\n\nThe distance between the centers of the two circles is:\n\\[\nd = 11 - 5 = 6\n\\]\n\nThe formula for the distance from the point of tangency to the point where the tangent intersects the line joining the centers is given by:\n\\[\na = \\frac{r_1 \\cdot x_2 + r_2 \\cdot x_1}{r_1 + r_2}\n\\]\nwhere \\( r_1 = 1 \\), \\( r_2 = 2 \\), \\( x_1 = 5 \\), and \\( x_2 = 11 \\).\n\nSubstituting the values, we get:\n\\[\na = \\frac{1 \\cdot 11 + 2 \\cdot 5}{1 + 2} = \\frac{11 + 10}{3} = \\frac{21}{3} = 7\n\\]\n\nThus, the value of \\( a \\) is \\(\\boxed{7}\\).'
"To find the volume of the silo, we need to calculate the volumes of both the cylindrical and conical parts and then sum them.\n\nFirst, let's determine the dimensions of each part:\n\n1. **Cylinder:**\n   - The diameter of the base is 10 meters, so the radius \\( r \\) is \\( \\frac{10}{2} = 5 \\) meters.\n   - Let the height of the cylinder be \\( h_c \\).\n   - The total height of the silo is 27 meters, and the height of the cone is half the height of the cylinder. Therefore, if the height of the cone is \\( h_k \\), then \\( h_k = \\frac{1}{2}h_c \\).\n   - The total height is given by \\( h_c + h_k = 27 \\).\n   - Substituting \\( h_k = \\frac{1}{2}h_c \\) into the equation, we get:\n     \\[\n     h_c + \\frac{1}{2}h_c = 27\n     \\]\n     \\[\n     \\frac{3}{2}h_c = 27\n     \\]\n     \\[\n     h_c = \\frac{27 \\times 2}{3} = 18 \\text{ meters}\n     \\]\n   - Therefore, the height of the cone \\( h_k = \\frac{1}{2} \\times 18 = 9 \\text{ meters} \\).\n\n2. **Volume Calculations:**\n   - **Volume of the Cylinder:**\n     \\[\n     V_{\\text{cylinder}} = \\pi r^2 h_c = \\pi (5)^2 (18) = 450\\pi \\text{ cubic meters}\n     \\]\n   - **Volume of the Cone:**\n     \\[\n     V_{\\text{cone}} = \\frac{1}{3} \\pi r^2 h_k = \\frac{1}{3} \\pi (5)^2 (9) = \\frac{1}{3} \\pi (25)(9) = 75\\pi \\text{ cubic meters}\n     \\]\n\n3. **Total Volume of the Silo:**\n   \\[\n   V_{\\text{total}} = V_{\\text{cylinder}} + V_{\\text{cone}} = 450\\pi + 75\\pi = 525\\pi \\text{ cubic meters}\n   \\]\n\nThus, the volume of the silo is \\(\\boxed{525\\pi}\\) cubic meters."
"To find the area of triangle \\( \\triangle DEC \\), we first need to determine the coordinates of points \\( D \\) and \\( E \\).\n\nGiven that \\( \\triangle ABC \\) is isosceles with \\( AB = BC = 5 \\) and \\( AC = 6 \\), we can place the triangle in the coordinate plane for convenience. Let's place point \\( A \\) at \\( (0, 0) \\) and point \\( C \\) at \\( (6, 0) \\). Since \\( B \\) is equidistant from \\( A \\) and \\( C \\), it lies on the perpendicular bisector of \\( AC \\).\n\nThe midpoint of \\( AC \\) is \\( (3, 0) \\). The perpendicular bisector of \\( AC \\) is the vertical line \\( x = 3 \\). We choose \\( B = (3, 4) \\) without loss of generality.\n\nNext, we find the coordinates of \\( D \\) and \\( E \\):\n\n- \\( D \\) is the foot of the altitude from \\( A \\) to \\( \\overline{BC} \\). "To solve this problem, we need to understand the geometric configuration involving the triangle \\( \\triangle ABC \\), its midpoints \\( M \\) and \\( N \\), and the perpendicular bisectors of sides \\( BC \\) and \\( AC \\).\n\n1. **Understanding the Configuration:**\n   - \\( M \\) is the midpoint of \\( BC \\).\n   - \\( N \\) is the midpoint of \\( AC \\).\n   - The perpendicular bisectors of \\( BC \\) and \\( AC \\) intersect at point \\( O \\).\n\n2. **Properties of Perpendicular Bisectors:**\n   - The perpendicular bisector of a segment is the locus of points equidistant from the segment's endpoints.\n   - Therefore, point \\( O \\) is equidistant from \\( B \\) and \\( C \\), and also from \\( A \\) and \\( C \\).\n\n3. **Implications for \\( \\triangle ABC \\):**\n   - Since \\( O \\) is equidistant from \\( B \\) and \\( C \\), and from \\( A \\) and \\( C \\), it lies on the circumcircle of \\( \\triangle ABC \\) (if \\( O \\) is the circumcenter, which is not necessarily the case here, but it is a point on the perpendicular bisectors).\n\n4. **Angle Calculation:**\n   - Since \\( M \\) and \\( N \\) are midpoints, \\( \\overline{MN} \\) is a midsegment of \\( \\triangle ABC \\), parallel to \\( AB \\) and half its length.\n   - The line \\( \\overline{MO} \\) is perpendicular to \\( \\overline{BC} \\), and \\( \\overline{NO} \\) is perpendicular to \\( \\overline{AC} \\).\n\n5. **Finding \\( \\angle MON \\):**\n   - Since \\( O \\) is on the perpendicular bisectors, \\( \\angle MON \\) is the angle between the perpendicular bisectors of \\( BC \\) and \\( AC \\).\n   - The angle between the perpendicular bisectors of two sides of a triangle is equal to \\( 180^\\circ - \\angle C \\).\n\nGiven \\( \\angle C = 47^\\circ \\), the measure of \\( \\angle MON \\) is:\n\\[\n\\angle MON = 180^\\circ - \\angle C = 180^\\circ - 47^\\circ = 133^\\circ.\n\\]\n\nThus, the measure of \\( \\angle MON \\) is \\(\\boxed{133^\\circ}\\)."
"To find the area of quadrilateral \\( AEIF \\), we first need to determine the area of triangle \\( ABC \\) and the inradius \\( r \\).\n\n### Step 1: Calculate the area of triangle \\( ABC \\)\n\nUsing Heron's formula, we first calculate the semi-perimeter \\( s \\) of triangle \\( ABC \\):\n\n\\[\ns = \\frac{AB + AC + BC}{2} = \\frac{13 + 15 + 14}{2} = 21\n\\]\n\nNow, apply Heron's formula to find the area \\( K \\):\n\n\\[\nK = \\sqrt{s(s - AB)(s - AC)(s - BC)} = \\sqrt{21(21 - 13)(21 - 15)(21 - 14)}\n\\]\n\n\\[\nK = \\sqrt{21 \\times 8 \\times 6 \\times 7} = \\sqrt{7056} = 84\n\\]\n\n### Step 2: Calculate the inradius \\( r \\)\n\nThe inradius \\( r \\) is given by the formula:\n\n\\[\nr = \\frac{K}{s} = \\frac{84}{21} = 4\n\\]\n\n### Step 3: Calculate the area of quadrilateral \\( AEIF \\)\n\nThe area of quadrilateral \\( AEIF \\) can be found by subtracting the area of triangle \\( AID \\) from the area of triangle \\( AIC \\).\n\nThe area of triangle \\( AIC \\) is:\n\n\\[\n\\text{Area of } \\triangle AIC = \\frac{1}{2} \\times AC \\times r = \\frac{1}{2} \\times 15 \\times 4 = 30\n\\]\n\nThe area of triangle \\( AID \\) is:\n\n\\[\n\\text{Area of } \\triangle AID = \\frac{1}{2} \\times AD \\times r\n\\]\n\nTo find \\( AD \\), we use the fact that \\( D \\) is the point where the incircle touches \\( BC \\). The length \\( BD \\) is given by:\n\n\\[\nBD = s - AC = 21 - 15 = 6\n\\]\n\nThus, \\( AD = AB - BD = 13 - 6 = 7 \\).\n\nNow, calculate the area of triangle \\( AID \\):\n\n\\[\n\\text{Area of } \\triangle AID = \\frac{1}{2} \\times 7 \\times 4 = 14\n\\]\n\nFinally, the area of quadrilateral \\( AEIF \\) is:\n\n\\[\n\\text{Area of } AEIF = \\text{Area of } \\triangle AIC - \\text{Area of } \\triangle AID = 30 - 14 = 16\n\\]\n\nThus, the area of quadrilateral \\( AEIF \\) is \\(\\boxed{16}\\)."
"To find the area of trapezoid \\(ABCD\\), we can use the formula for the area of a trapezoid:\n\n\\[\n\\text{Area} = \\frac{1}{2} \\times (\\text{Base}_1 + \\text{Base}_2) \\times \\text{Height}\n\\]\n\nIn this problem, the bases are \\(\\overline{AB} = 52\\) and \\(\\overline{CD} = 39\\). "To find the perimeter of the trapezoid, we first need to determine the length of the other base. We know the area of a trapezoid is given by the formula:\n\n\\[\n\\text{Area} = \\frac{1}{2} \\times (\\text{Base}_1 + \\text{Base}_2) \\times \\text{Height}\n\\]\n\nGiven that the area is \\(162 \\text{ cm}^2\\), the height is \\(12 \\text{ cm}\\), and one base is \\(16 \\text{ cm}\\), we can set up the equation:\n\n\\[\n162 = \\frac{1}{2} \\times (16 + \\text{Base}_2) \\times 12\n\\]\n\nSolving for \\(\\text{Base}_2\\), we first multiply both sides by 2 to eliminate the fraction:\n\n\\[\n324 = (16 + \\text{Base}_2) \\times 12\n\\]\n\nNext, divide both sides by 12:\n\n\\[\n27 = 16 + \\text{Base}_2\n\\]\n\nSubtract 16 from both sides to find \\(\\text{Base}_2\\):\n\n\\[\n\\text{Base}_2 = 11\n\\]\n\nNow that we have both bases, \\(16 \\text{ cm}\\) and \\(11 \\text{ cm}\\), and the height, \\(12 \\text{ cm}\\), we need to find the lengths of the non-parallel sides to determine the perimeter. Let's denote the non-parallel sides as \\(a\\) and \\(b\\).\n\nSince the trapezoid is not specified as isosceles, we assume it is a right trapezoid based on the diagram, where one of the non-parallel sides is vertical (the height). Therefore, one of the non-parallel sides is \\(12 \\text{ cm}\\).\n\nTo find the other non-parallel side, we use the Pythagorean theorem. The difference in the lengths of the bases is \\(16 - 11 = 5 \\text{ cm}\\). This difference forms the base of a right triangle with the height as one leg and the other non-parallel side as the hypotenuse.\n\nLet \\(b\\) be the hypotenuse:\n\n\\[\nb = \\sqrt{12^2 + 5^2} = \\sqrt{144 + 25} = \\sqrt{169} = 13\n\\]\n\nNow, we can calculate the perimeter of the trapezoid:\n\n\\[\n\\text{Perimeter} = 16 + 11 + 12 + 13 = 52 \\text{ cm}\n\\]\n\nThus, the perimeter of the trapezoid is \\(\\boxed{52 \\text{ cm}}\\)."
'To find the area of the shaded region, we need to calculate the area of the largest semicircle and subtract the areas of the two smaller semicircles.\n\n1. **Calculate the radius of each semicircle:**\n\n   - The largest semicircle has a diameter \\( AB = 68 + 68 = 136 \\). Therefore, its radius is \\( \\frac{136}{2} = 68 \\).\n\n   - The middle semicircle has a diameter \\( AC = 32 + 32 = 64 \\). Therefore, its radius is \\( \\frac{64}{2} = 32 \\).\n\n   - The smallest semicircle has a diameter \\( CB = 36 + 36 = 72 \\). Therefore, its radius is \\( \\frac{72}{2} = 36 \\).\n\n2. **Calculate the area of each semicircle:**\n\n   - The area of a semicircle is given by \\( \\frac{1}{2} \\pi r^2 \\).\n\n   - Area of the largest semicircle:\n     \\[\n     \\frac{1}{2} \\pi (68)^2 = \\frac{1}{2} \\pi \\times 4624 = 2312\\pi\n     \\]\n\n   - Area of the middle semicircle:\n     \\[\n     \\frac{1}{2} \\pi (32)^2 = \\frac{1}{2} \\pi \\times 1024 = 512\\pi\n     \\]\n\n   - Area of the smallest semicircle:\n     \\[\n     \\frac{1}{2} \\pi (36)^2 = \\frac{1}{2} \\pi \\times 1296 = 648\\pi\n     \\]\n\n3. **Calculate the area of the shaded region:**\n\n   The shaded region is the area of the largest semicircle minus the areas of the two smaller semicircles:\n   \\[\n   2312\\pi - 512\\pi - 648\\pi = 1152\\pi\n   \\]\n\nTherefore, the area of the shaded region is \\(\\boxed{1152\\pi}\\).'
'To find the area of trapezoid \\(BCDE\\) in the regular octagon \\(ABCDEFGH\\) with side length 12 cm, we first need to determine the lengths of the bases \\(BC\\) and \\(DE\\), and the height of the trapezoid.\n\nSince \\(ABCDEFGH\\) is a regular octagon, all its sides are equal, and each internal angle is \\(135^\\circ\\). The octagon can be divided into 8 isosceles triangles by drawing lines from the center to each vertex. Each of these triangles has a central angle of \\(45^\\circ\\).\n\nThe trapezoid \\(BCDE\\) consists of two parallel sides \\(BC\\) and \\(DE\\), and two non-parallel sides \\(CD\\) and \\(BE\\). Since \\(BC\\) and \\(DE\\) are sides of the octagon, they are both equal to 12 cm.\n\nTo find the height of the trapezoid, we can use the fact that the octagon can be inscribed in a circle. The radius \\(R\\) of this circle can be found using the formula for the circumradius of a regular octagon:\n\\[\nR = \\frac{s}{2 \\sin(\\pi/8)}\n\\]\nwhere \\(s = 12\\) cm is the side length of the octagon. Calculating \\(R\\):\n\\[\nR = \\frac{12}{2 \\sin(22.5^\\circ)}\n\\]\n\nThe height \\(h\\) of the trapezoid \\(BCDE\\) is the vertical distance between the parallel sides \\(BC\\) and \\(DE\\). This height is equal to the difference in the y-coordinates of the midpoints of \\(BC\\) and \\(DE\\). Since the octagon is symmetric, the height can be calculated as:\n\\[\nh = R \\cdot (\\cos(22.5^\\circ) - \\cos(67.5^\\circ))\n\\]\n\nNow, the area \\(A\\) of trapezoid \\(BCDE\\) is given by:\n\\[\nA = \\frac{1}{2} \\times (BC + DE) \\times h = \\frac{1}{2} \\times (12 + 12) \\times h = 12h\n\\]\n\nSubstituting the expression for \\(h\\):\n\\[\nA = 12 \\times R \\times (\\cos(22.5^\\circ) - \\cos(67.5^\\circ))\n\\]\n\nUsing trigonometric identities and calculations:\n\\[\n\\cos(22.5^\\circ) = \\sqrt{\\frac{2 + \\sqrt{2}}{4}}, \\quad \\cos(67.5^\\circ) = \\sqrt{\\frac{2 - \\sqrt{2}}{4}}\n\\]\n\nThus:\n\\[\nA = 12 \\times \\frac{12}{2 \\sin(22.5^\\circ)} \\times \\left(\\sqrt{\\frac{2 + \\sqrt{2}}{4}} - \\sqrt{\\frac{2 - \\sqrt{2}}{4}}\\right)\n\\]\n\nAfter simplifying, the area of trapezoid \\(BCDE\\) is:\n\\[\nA = 288 \\times \\left(\\sqrt{\\frac{2 + \\sqrt{2}}{4}} - \\sqrt{\\frac{2 - \\sqrt{2}}{4}}\\right)\n\\]\n\nThis simplifies to:\n\\[\nA = 288 \\times \\frac{\\sqrt{2} - 1}{2}\n\\]\n\nFinally, the area of trapezoid \\(BCDE\\) is:\n\\[\nA = 144(\\sqrt{2} - 1) \\text{ square centimeters}\n\\]\n\nTherefore, the area of trapezoid \\(BCDE\\) is \\(\\boxed{144(\\sqrt{2} - 1)}\\) square centimeters.'

'To find the area of the shaded region in the regular octagon, we first need to understand the geometry of the problem. The shaded region is formed by the intersection of four diagonals of the octagon.\n\nGiven that the octagon is regular with side length 2, we can calculate the area of the entire octagon and then use symmetry and geometric properties to find the area of the shaded region.\n\n1. **Calculate the area of the regular octagon:**\n\n   A regular octagon can be divided into 8 isosceles triangles, each with a central angle of \\(45^\\circ\\). The formula for the area of a regular octagon with side length \\(s\\) is:\n\n   \\[\n   \\text{Area} = 2(1+\\sqrt{2})s^2\n   \\]\n\n   Substituting \\(s = 2\\):\n\n   \\[\n   \\text{Area} = 2(1+\\sqrt{2})(2)^2 = 8(1+\\sqrt{2})\n   \\]\n\n2. **Determine the area of the shaded region:**\n\n   The shaded region is a quadrilateral formed by the intersection of the diagonals. In a regular octagon, the diagonals intersect in such a way that they form a smaller, similar octagon in the center. The shaded region is part of this smaller octagon.\n\n   The diagonals divide the octagon into smaller regions, and by symmetry, the shaded region is one of these smaller regions. The key is to recognize that the diagonals divide the octagon into congruent regions.\n\n   Since the octagon is regular and symmetric, the diagonals divide it into 8 congruent smaller regions. Therefore, the area of the shaded region is one-eighth of the total area of the octagon.\n\n3. **Calculate the area of the shaded region:**\n\n   \\[\n   \\text{Area of shaded region} = \\frac{1}{8} \\times 8(1+\\sqrt{2}) = 1+\\sqrt{2}\n   \\]\n\nThus, the area of the shaded region is \\(\\boxed{1+\\sqrt{2}}\\).'
"To solve this problem, we need to find the length of the chord that is parallel to the given chords and midway between them. Let's denote the circle's center as \\( O \\).\n\nFirst, consider the two given chords. Let the chord of length 14 be \\( AB \\) and the chord of length 10 be \\( CD \\). The distance between these chords is 6, and we need to find the length of the chord \\( EF \\) that is midway between them.\n\nSince \\( EF \\) is midway between \\( AB \\) and \\( CD \\), the perpendicular distance from the center \\( O \\) to \\( EF \\) is the average of the perpendicular distances from \\( O \\) to \\( AB \\) and \\( CD \\).\n\nLet \\( d_1 \\) be the perpendicular distance from \\( O \\) to \\( AB \\), and \\( d_2 \\) be the perpendicular distance from \\( O \\) to \\( CD \\). We know that \\( d_1 - d_2 = 6 \\).\n\nThe length of a chord in a circle can be determined using the formula:\n\\[\n\\text{Length of chord} = 2 \\sqrt{r^2 - d^2}\n\\]\nwhere \\( r \\) is the radius of the circle and \\( d \\) is the perpendicular distance from the center to the chord.\n\nFor chord \\( AB \\) of length 14:\n\\[\n14 = 2 \\sqrt{r^2 - d_1^2} \\implies 7 = \\sqrt{r^2 - d_1^2} \\implies r^2 - d_1^2 = 49\n\\]\n\nFor chord \\( CD \\) of length 10:\n\\[\n10 = 2 \\sqrt{r^2 - d_2^2} \\implies 5 = \\sqrt{r^2 - d_2^2} \\implies r^2 - d_2^2 = 25\n\\]\n\nWe have two equations:\n1. \\( r^2 - d_1^2 = 49 \\)\n2. \\( r^2 - d_2^2 = 25 \\)\n\nSubtract the second equation from the first:\n\\[\n(r^2 - d_1^2) - (r^2 - d_2^2) = 49 - 25 \\implies d_2^2 - d_1^2 = 24\n\\]\n\nWe also know:\n\\[\nd_1 - d_2 = 6\n\\]\n\nLet \\( x = d_1 - d_2 = 6 \\). Then:\n\\[\nd_1 = d_2 + 6\n\\]\n\nSubstitute \\( d_1 = d_2 + 6 \\) into \\( d_2^2 - d_1^2 = 24 \\):\n\\[\nd_2^2 - (d_2 + 6)^2 = 24\n\\]\n\\[\nd_2^2 - (d_2^2 + 12d_2 + 36) = 24\n\\]\n\\[\n-12d_2 - 36 = 24\n\\]\n\\[\n-12d_2 = 60\n\\]\n\\[\nd_2 = -5\n\\]\n\nSince \\( d_2 \\) is a distance, we take the positive value:\n\\[\nd_2 = 5\n\\]\n\nNow, find \\( d_1 \\):\n\\[\nd_1 = d_2 + 6 = 5 + 6 = 11\n\\]\n\nThe perpendicular distance from \\( O \\) to \\( EF \\) is the average of \\( d_1 \\) and \\( d_2 \\):\n\\[\nd = \\frac{d_1 + d_2}{2} = \\frac{11 + 5}{2} = 8\n\\]\n\nNow, find the length of \\( EF \\):\n\\[\n\\text{Length of } EF = 2 \\sqrt{r^2 - d^2}\n\\]\n\nWe know \\( r^2 - d_1^2 = 49 \\), so \\( r^2 = 49 + d_1^2 = 49 + 11^2 = 49 + 121 = 170 \\).\n\nThus:\n\\[\n\\text{Length of } EF = 2 \\sqrt{170 - 8^2} = 2 \\sqrt{170 - 64} = 2 \\sqrt{106}\n\\]\n\nTherefore, the value of \\( a \\) is:\n\\[\na = 106\n\\]\n\nThe answer is \\(\\boxed{106}\\)."
'In an isosceles triangle, two sides are equal, and the angles opposite those sides are also equal. Therefore, we have:\n   \\[\n   2\\angle P + 40^\\circ = 180^\\circ\n   \\]\n   \\[\n   2\\angle P = 140^\\circ\n   \\]\n   \\[\n   \\angle P = 70^\\circ\n   \\]\n\n2. **Case 2:** \\(\\angle P = \\angle R\\)\n\n   In this case, \\(\\angle P = 40^\\circ\\). Since \\(\\angle P = \\angle R\\), \\(\\angle Q\\) is the remaining angle:\n   \\[\n   \\angle Q = 180^\\circ - 40^\\circ - 40^\\circ = 100^\\circ\n   \\]\n\nThus, the possible measures for \\(\\angle P\\) are \\(40^\\circ\\), \\(70^\\circ\\), and \\(100^\\circ\\). 'To find the radius of a sphere whose volume and surface area are numerically equal, we start by using the formulas for the volume and surface area of a sphere.\n\nThe volume \\( V \\) of a sphere with radius \\( r \\) is given by:\n\\[\nV = \\frac{4}{3} \\pi r^3\n\\]\n\nThe surface area \\( A \\) of a sphere with radius \\( r \\) is given by:\n\\[\nA = 4 \\pi r^2\n\\]\n\nWe are given that the volume and surface area are numerically equal, so we set the two expressions equal to each other:\n\\[\n\\frac{4}{3} \\pi r^3 = 4 \\pi r^2\n\\]\n\nTo solve for \\( r \\), we can divide both sides by \\( 4 \\pi r^2 \\) (assuming \\( r \\neq 0 \\)):\n\\[\n\\frac{1}{3} r = 1\n\\]\n\nMultiplying both sides by 3 gives:\n\\[\nr = 3\n\\]\n\nThus, the radius of the sphere is \\( \\boxed{3} \\) units.'

"To solve this problem, we need to understand the geometry of the folds and the resulting quadrilateral \\(DRQC\\).\n\n1. **Initial Setup:**\n   - We have a rectangle \\(ABCD\\) with \\(AB = 5\\) cm and \\(AD = 8\\) cm.\n   - The first fold places edge \\(CD\\) along edge \\(AD\\), creating a crease \\(DP\\).\n   - The second fold places edge \\(AB\\) along edge \\(AD\\), creating a crease \\(AQ\\).\n   - The creases \\(DP\\) and \\(AQ\\) intersect at point \\(R\\).\n\n2. **Understanding the Creases:**\n   - When \\(CD\\) is folded onto \\(AD\\), point \\(C\\) moves to a point on \\(AD\\). Let's call this point \\(C'\\).\n   - Similarly, when \\(AB\\) is folded onto \\(AD\\), point \\(B\\) moves to a point on \\(AD\\). Let's call this point \\(B'\\).\n\n3. **Properties of the Creases:**\n   - The crease \\(DP\\) is the perpendicular bisector of segment \\(CC'\\).\n   - The crease \\(AQ\\) is the perpendicular bisector of segment \\(BB'\\).\n\n4. **Finding the Area of Quadrilateral \\(DRQC\\):**\n   - Since \\(DRQC\\) is a quadrilateral formed by the intersection of the creases and the original rectangle, we can find its area by subtracting the areas of triangles \\(PQR\\) and \\(ADR\\) from the area of rectangle \\(ABCD\\).\n\n5. **Calculating the Area:**\n   - The area of rectangle \\(ABCD\\) is \\(5 \\times 8 = 40\\) cm\\(^2\\).\n   - The triangles \\(PQR\\) and \\(ADR\\) are right triangles formed by the folds.\n   - The area of triangle \\(ADR\\) is \\(\\frac{1}{2} \\times AD \\times DR\\).\n   - The area of triangle \\(PQR\\) is \\(\\frac{1}{2} \\times PR \\times QR\\).\n\n6. **Using Symmetry and Properties:**\n   - Due to the symmetry of the folds, the triangles \\(PQR\\) and \\(ADR\\) are congruent.\n   - Therefore, the area of quadrilateral \\(DRQC\\) is half of the area of the rectangle \\(ABCD\\).\n\n7. **Final Calculation:**\n   - The area of quadrilateral \\(DRQC\\) is \\(\\frac{1}{2} \\times 40 = 20\\) cm\\(^2\\).\n\nThus, the area of quadrilateral \\(DRQC\\) is \\(\\boxed{20}\\) cm\\(^2\\)."
'The original recipe fills a 9-inch by 13-inch pan to a depth of 1 inch. Therefore, the volume of the mixture from the original recipe is:\n\n\\[ 9 \\times 13 \\times 1 = 117 \\text{ cubic inches}. \\]\n\nIf we make 1.5 times the original recipe, the volume of the mixture becomes:\n\n\\[ 1.5 \\times 117 = 175.5 \\text{ cubic inches}. \\]\n\nThis mixture is poured into a 10-inch by 15-inch pan. To find the depth to which the pan will be filled, we use the formula for volume:\n\n\\[ \\text{Volume} = \\text{Length} \\times \\text{Width} \\times \\text{Depth}. \\]\n\nSubstituting the known values:\n\n\\[ 175.5 = 10 \\times 15 \\times \\text{Depth}. \\]\n\nSolving for the depth:\n\n\\[ \\text{Depth} = \\frac{175.5}{10 \\times 15} = \\frac{175.5}{150} = 1.17. \\]\n\nTherefore, the pan will be filled to a depth of 1.17 inches. The answer is \\(\\boxed{1.17}\\).'
'In triangle \\(ABC\\), the altitudes \\(\\overline{AX}\\) and \\(\\overline{BY}\\) intersect at the orthocenter \\(H\\). The angle \\(\\angle AHB\\) is given as \\(132^\\circ\\).\n\nSince \\(H\\) is the orthocenter, the angles \\(\\angle AHB\\), \\(\\angle BHC\\), and \\(\\angle AHC\\) are related to the angles of triangle \\(ABC\\) by the following properties:\n\n- \\(\\angle AHB = 180^\\circ - \\angle ACB\\)\n- \\(\\angle BHC = 180^\\circ - \\angle BAC\\)\n- \\(\\angle AHC = 180^\\circ - \\angle ABC\\)\n\nGiven that \\(\\angle AHB = 132^\\circ\\), we can find \\(\\angle ACB\\) using the relationship:\n\n\\[\n\\angle AHB = 180^\\circ - \\angle ACB\n\\]\n\nSubstituting the given value:\n\n\\[\n132^\\circ = 180^\\circ - \\angle ACB\n\\]\n\nSolving for \\(\\angle ACB\\):\n\n\\[\n\\angle ACB = 180^\\circ - 132^\\circ = 48^\\circ\n\\]\n\nTherefore, \\(\\angle ACB\\) is \\(\\boxed{48^\\circ}\\).'
"The surface area \\( A \\) of a sphere with radius \\( r \\) is given by the formula:\n\n\\[\nA = 4\\pi r^2\n\\]\n\nFirst, we calculate the surface area of Theo's sphere, which has a radius of 5:\n\n\\[\nA_{\\text{Theo}} = 4\\pi (5)^2 = 4\\pi \\times 25 = 100\\pi\n\\]\n\nNext, we calculate the surface area of one of Akshaj's spheres, which has a radius of 2:\n\n\\[\nA_{\\text{Akshaj, one sphere}} = 4\\pi (2)^2 = 4\\pi \\times 4 = 16\\pi\n\\]\n\nSince Akshaj has two spheres, the total surface area of both spheres is:\n\n\\[\nA_{\\text{Akshaj, total}} = 2 \\times 16\\pi = 32\\pi\n\\]\n\nNow, we find the ratio of the surface area of Theo's sphere to the total surface area of Akshaj's spheres:\n\n\\[\n\\text{Ratio} = \\frac{A_{\\text{Theo}}}{A_{\\text{Akshaj, total}}} = \\frac{100\\pi}{32\\pi} = \\frac{100}{32} = \\frac{25}{8}\n\\]\n\nThus, the ratio of the surface area of Theo's sphere to the total surface area of Akshaj's spheres is \\(\\boxed{\\frac{25}{8}}\\)."
'To solve this problem, we need to understand the geometry of a regular tetrahedron and the properties of its centroid.\n\nA regular tetrahedron is a three-dimensional shape with four equilateral triangular faces. The centroid of a regular tetrahedron is the point that is equidistant from all four vertices. This point is also the center of the circumscribed sphere (circumcenter) and the inscribed sphere (incenter) of the tetrahedron.\n\nIn a regular tetrahedron, the centroid divides each median in the ratio 3:1. This means that if you take a line segment from a vertex to the centroid of the opposite face, the centroid divides this segment into two parts, with the part closer to the face being three times longer than the part closer to the vertex.\n\nIn this problem, point \\( P \\) is the centroid of the tetrahedron, and point \\( Q \\) is the point where the line segment \\( \\overrightarrow{AP} \\) intersects the face \\( BCD \\). Since \\( P \\) is the centroid, it divides the segment \\( AQ \\) in the ratio 3:1, with \\( PQ \\) being one part and \\( AQ \\) being the entire segment.\n\nTherefore, the ratio \\( PQ:AQ \\) is \\( 1:4 \\).\n\nThus, the ratio \\( PQ/AQ \\) is \\(\\boxed{\\frac{1}{4}}\\).'
'To determine the value of \\( a \\) for which there is a right triangle with sides \\( a + 1 \\), \\( 6a \\), and \\( 6a + 1 \\), we need to apply the Pythagorean theorem. The Pythagorean theorem states that in a right triangle, the square of the length of the hypotenuse is equal to the sum of the squares of the lengths of the other two sides.\n\nAssuming \\( 6a + 1 \\) is the hypotenuse, we have:\n\n\\[\n(6a + 1)^2 = (a + 1)^2 + (6a)^2\n\\]\n\nExpanding each term, we get:\n\n\\[\n(6a + 1)^2 = 36a^2 + 12a + 1\n\\]\n\n\\[\n(a + 1)^2 = a^2 + 2a + 1\n\\]\n\n\\[\n(6a)^2 = 36a^2\n\\]\n\nSubstituting these into the equation, we have:\n\n\\[\n36a^2 + 12a + 1 = a^2 + 2a + 1 + 36a^2\n\\]\n\nSimplifying both sides, we get:\n\n\\[\n36a^2 + 12a + 1 = 37a^2 + 2a + 1\n\\]\n\nSubtract \\( 36a^2 + 12a + 1 \\) from both sides:\n\n\\[\n0 = a^2 - 10a\n\\]\n\nFactor the equation:\n\n\\[\na(a - 10) = 0\n\\]\n\nThis gives us two possible solutions for \\( a \\):\n\n\\[\na = 0 \\quad \\text{or} \\quad a = 10\n\\]\n\nSince \\( a = 0 \\) would result in a degenerate triangle (with one side of length 1 and the other two sides of length 0), we discard this solution. Therefore, the value of \\( a \\) for which there is a right triangle is:\n\n\\[\n\\boxed{10}\n\\]'
'To find the number of isosceles triangles with whole-number length sides and a perimeter of 20 units, we start by letting the side lengths of the isosceles triangle be \\(a, a, b\\), where \\(a\\) is the length of the two equal sides and \\(b\\) is the base. The perimeter condition gives us:\n\n\\[ 2a + b = 20. \\]\n\nFrom this equation, we can express \\(b\\) in terms of \\(a\\):\n\n\\[ b = 20 - 2a. \\]\n\nFor these side lengths to form a valid triangle, they must satisfy the triangle inequality conditions. Specifically, for an isosceles triangle, we need:\n\n1. \\( a + a > b \\) or \\( 2a > b \\).\n2. \\( a + b > a \\) or \\( b > 0 \\).\n3. \\( a + b > a \\) or \\( b > 0 \\).\n\nThe second and third conditions are automatically satisfied since \\(b = 20 - 2a\\) and \\(b\\) must be positive for any valid triangle. Therefore, we only need to check the first condition:\n\n\\[ 2a > b. \\]\n\nSubstituting \\(b = 20 - 2a\\) into this inequality gives:\n\n\\[ 2a > 20 - 2a. \\]\n\nSolving for \\(a\\), we get:\n\n\\[ 4a > 20 \\]\n\\[ a > 5. \\]\n\nSince \\(a\\) must be a whole number, the smallest possible value for \\(a\\) is 6. Additionally, since \\(b = 20 - 2a\\) must be positive, we have:\n\n\\[ 20 - 2a > 0 \\]\n\\[ 20 > 2a \\]\n\\[ 10 > a. \\]\n\nThus, \\(a\\) can take integer values from 6 to 9. We can list these possibilities:\n\n- If \\(a = 6\\), then \\(b = 20 - 2 \\times 6 = 8\\).\n- If \\(a = 7\\), then \\(b = 20 - 2 \\times 7 = 6\\).\n- If \\(a = 8\\), then \\(b = 20 - 2 \\times 8 = 4\\).\n- If \\(a = 9\\), then \\(b = 20 - 2 \\times 9 = 2\\).\n\nEach of these combinations satisfies the triangle inequality conditions, so there are 4 possible isosceles triangles with a perimeter of 20 units. Therefore, the answer is:\n\n\\[ \\boxed{4} \\]'
'To solve this problem, we need to perform two reflections on the point \\((0,0)\\).\n\n1. **Reflection across the vertical line \\(x = 1\\):**\n\n   When a point \\((x, y)\\) is reflected across a vertical line \\(x = a\\), the x-coordinate of the point changes to \\(2a - x\\), while the y-coordinate remains the same.\n\n   For the point \\((0,0)\\) and the line \\(x = 1\\), the reflected point will be:\n   \\[\n   (2 \\times 1 - 0, 0) = (2, 0)\n   \\]\n\n2. **Reflection across the horizontal line \\(y = 2\\):**\n\n   When a point \\((x, y)\\) is reflected across a horizontal line \\(y = b\\), the y-coordinate of the point changes to \\(2b - y\\), while the x-coordinate remains the same.\n\n   For the point \\((2,0)\\) and the line \\(y = 2\\), the reflected point will be:\n   \\[\n   (2, 2 \\times 2 - 0) = (2, 4)\n   \\]\n\nTherefore, the coordinates of the resulting point after both reflections are \\((2, 4)\\).'
'To find the distance between any pair of parallel sides of a regular hexagon with side length 6 units, we can use the properties of a regular hexagon.\n\nA regular hexagon can be divided into 6 equilateral triangles. The height of one of these equilateral triangles will give us the distance between two parallel sides of the hexagon.\n\nFor an equilateral triangle with side length \\( s \\), the height \\( h \\) can be calculated using the formula:\n\n\\[\nh = \\frac{\\sqrt{3}}{2} s\n\\]\n\nIn this case, the side length \\( s \\) is 6 units. Therefore, the height \\( h \\) is:\n\n\\[\nh = \\frac{\\sqrt{3}}{2} \\times 6 = 3\\sqrt{3}\n\\]\n\nThus, the distance between any pair of parallel sides of the regular hexagon is \\( \\boxed{3\\sqrt{3}} \\) units.'
"To solve this problem, we need to find the volume of the space inside the cylinder but outside the cone. This can be done by subtracting the volume of the cone from the volume of the cylinder.\n\nFirst, let's find the dimensions of the cylinder. The volume of a cylinder is given by the formula:\n\n\\[\nV_{\\text{cylinder}} = \\pi r^2 h\n\\]\n\nwhere \\( r \\) is the radius and \\( h \\) is the height of the cylinder. We are given that the volume of the cylinder is \\( 72\\pi \\) cubic centimeters. Therefore, we have:\n\n\\[\n\\pi r^2 h = 72\\pi\n\\]\n\nDividing both sides by \\(\\pi\\), we get:\n\n\\[\nr^2 h = 72\n\\]\n\nNext, we consider the cone inscribed in the cylinder. Since the cone is inscribed, it has the same height and radius as the cylinder. The volume of a cone is given by the formula:\n\n\\[\nV_{\\text{cone}} = \\frac{1}{3} \\pi r^2 h\n\\]\n\nNow, we can find the volume of the space inside the cylinder but outside the cone by subtracting the volume of the cone from the volume of the cylinder:\n\n\\[\nV_{\\text{space}} = V_{\\text{cylinder}} - V_{\\text{cone}}\n\\]\n\nSubstituting the formulas for the volumes, we have:\n\n\\[\nV_{\\text{space}} = \\pi r^2 h - \\frac{1}{3} \\pi r^2 h\n\\]\n\nSimplifying, we get:\n\n\\[\nV_{\\text{space}} = \\left(1 - \\frac{1}{3}\\right) \\pi r^2 h = \\frac{2}{3} \\pi r^2 h\n\\]\n\nSince \\( r^2 h = 72 \\), we substitute this into the equation:\n\n\\[\nV_{\\text{space}} = \\frac{2}{3} \\times 72\\pi = 48\\pi\n\\]\n\nTherefore, the number of cubic centimeters in the space inside the cylinder but outside the cone is \\(\\boxed{48\\pi}\\)."
'In a trapezoid, the sum of the interior angles is \\(360^\\circ\\). If the angles form an arithmetic sequence and the smallest angle is \\(60^\\circ\\), we can denote the angles as \\(60^\\circ, 60^\\circ + d, 60^\\circ + 2d, 60^\\circ + 3d\\).\n\nThe sum of these angles is:\n\n\\[\n60^\\circ + (60^\\circ + d) + (60^\\circ + 2d) + (60^\\circ + 3d) = 360^\\circ\n\\]\n\nSimplifying, we have:\n\n\\[\n240^\\circ + 6d = 360^\\circ\n\\]\n\nSubtracting \\(240^\\circ\\) from both sides gives:\n\n\\[\n6d = 120^\\circ\n\\]\n\nDividing by 6, we find:\n\n\\[\nd = 20^\\circ\n\\]\n\nThus, the angles of the trapezoid are:\n\n- \\(60^\\circ\\)\n- \\(60^\\circ + 20^\\circ = 80^\\circ\\)\n- \\(60^\\circ + 2 \\times 20^\\circ = 100^\\circ\\)\n- \\(60^\\circ + 3 \\times 20^\\circ = 120^\\circ\\)\n\nThe largest angle is \\(120^\\circ\\). Therefore, the measure of the largest angle is \\(\\boxed{120}\\) degrees.'
"To solve this problem, we need to determine the area of the region swept out by the interior of a unit square when it is rotated \\(45^\\circ\\) about its center.\n\n1. **Initial Setup**: Consider a unit square with vertices at \\((0,0)\\), \\((1,0)\\), \\((1,1)\\), and \\((0,1)\\). The center of the square is at \\((0.5, 0.5)\\).\n\n2. **Rotation**: When the square is rotated \\(45^\\circ\\) about its center, each vertex of the square moves along a circular arc centered at \\((0.5, 0.5)\\) with radius \\(\\sqrt{0.5^2 + 0.5^2} = \\frac{\\sqrt{2}}{2}\\).\n\n3. **Swept Region**: The region swept out by the square is a larger square with its sides parallel to the original square's sides, but with its vertices at the points where the original square's vertices reach after a \\(45^\\circ\\) rotation.\n\n4. **Calculating the Area**: The diagonal of the larger square is the same as the diagonal of the original square, which is \\(\\sqrt{2}\\). The side length of the larger square is \\(\\frac{\\sqrt{2}}{\\sqrt{2}} = 1\\), which is the same as the original square.\n\n5. **Conclusion**: Since the side length of the larger square is the same as the original square, the area of the region swept out by the square is the same as the area of the original square, which is \\(1\\).\n\nTherefore, the area of the region swept out by the interior of the square is \\(\\boxed{1}\\)."
'Let the side length of the square base of the original pyramid be \\( s \\), and let the altitude of the original pyramid be \\( h \\).\n\nThe surface area of the original pyramid consists of the base area and the lateral surface area. The base area is \\( s^2 \\). The lateral surface area is composed of 4 triangular faces, each with a base \\( s \\) and a slant height \\( l \\). The slant height \\( l \\) can be found using the Pythagorean theorem in the right triangle formed by the altitude \\( h \\), half the base \\( \\frac{s}{2} \\), and the slant height \\( l \\):\n\n\\[\nl = \\sqrt{h^2 + \\left(\\frac{s}{2}\\right)^2} = \\sqrt{h^2 + \\frac{s^2}{4}}\n\\]\n\nThe area of one triangular face is:\n\n\\[\n\\frac{1}{2} \\times s \\times l = \\frac{1}{2} \\times s \\times \\sqrt{h^2 + \\frac{s^2}{4}}\n\\]\n\nThus, the total lateral surface area is:\n\n\\[\n2s \\times \\sqrt{h^2 + \\frac{s^2}{4}}\n\\]\n\nThe total surface area of the original pyramid is:\n\n\\[\ns^2 + 2s \\times \\sqrt{h^2 + \\frac{s^2}{4}}\n\\]\n\nThe smaller pyramid, which is cut from the top, is similar to the original pyramid. Since the plane is parallel to the base and 2 units from the base, the height of the smaller pyramid is \\( h - 2 \\).\n\nThe ratio of similarity between the smaller pyramid and the original pyramid is:\n\n\\[\n\\frac{h - 2}{h}\n\\]\n\nThe surface area of the smaller pyramid is:\n\n\\[\n\\left(\\frac{h - 2}{h}\\right)^2 \\times \\left(s^2 + 2s \\times \\sqrt{h^2 + \\frac{s^2}{4}}\\right)\n\\]\n\nAccording to the problem, the surface area of the smaller pyramid is half the surface area of the original pyramid:\n\n\\[\n\\left(\\frac{h - 2}{h}\\right)^2 \\times \\left(s^2 + 2s \\times \\sqrt{h^2 + \\frac{s^2}{4}}\\right) = \\frac{1}{2} \\times \\left(s^2 + 2s \\times \\sqrt{h^2 + \\frac{s^2}{4}}\\right)\n\\]\n\nDividing both sides by \\( s^2 + 2s \\times \\sqrt{h^2 + \\frac{s^2}{4}} \\), we get:\n\n\\[\n\\left(\\frac{h - 2}{h}\\right)^2 = \\frac{1}{2}\n\\]\n\nTaking the square root of both sides:\n\n\\[\n\\frac{h - 2}{h} = \\frac{1}{\\sqrt{2}}\n\\]\n\nSolving for \\( h \\):\n\n\\[\nh - 2 = \\frac{h}{\\sqrt{2}}\n\\]\n\n\\[\nh - \\frac{h}{\\sqrt{2}} = 2\n\\]\n\n\\[\nh \\left(1 - \\frac{1}{\\sqrt{2}}\\right) = 2\n\\]\n\n\\[\nh \\left(\\frac{\\sqrt{2} - 1}{\\sqrt{2}}\\right) = 2\n\\]\n\n\\[\nh = \\frac{2\\sqrt{2}}{\\sqrt{2} - 1}\n\\]\n\nRationalizing the denominator:\n\n\\[\nh = \\frac{2\\sqrt{2}(\\sqrt{2} + 1)}{(\\sqrt{2} - 1)(\\sqrt{2} + 1)} = \\frac{2\\sqrt{2}(\\sqrt{2} + 1)}{2 - 1} = 2\\sqrt{2}(\\sqrt{2} + 1)\n\\]\n\n\\[\nh = 2(2 + \\sqrt{2}) = 4 + 2\\sqrt{2}\n\\]\n\nThus, the altitude of the original pyramid is \\( \\boxed{4 + 2\\sqrt{2}} \\).'
'To find the area of \\(\\triangle QPS\\), we can use the given information and the properties of triangles.\n\nFirst, note that \\(\\angle RPS = 90^\\circ\\), which means \\(\\triangle PRS\\) is a right triangle. We can use the Pythagorean theorem to find \\(RS\\).\n\nIn \\(\\triangle PRS\\):\n\\[\nPR^2 + RS^2 = PS^2\n\\]\nGiven \\(PR = 12\\) and \\(\\angle PRQ = 120^\\circ\\), we can find \\(PS\\) using the law of cosines in \\(\\triangle PRQ\\):\n\\[\nPQ^2 = PR^2 + QR^2 - 2 \\cdot PR \\cdot QR \\cdot \\cos(120^\\circ)\n\\]\n\\[\nPQ^2 = 12^2 + 8^2 - 2 \\cdot 12 \\cdot 8 \\cdot \\left(-\\frac{1}{2}\\right)\n\\]\n\\[\nPQ^2 = 144 + 64 + 96 = 304\n\\]\n\\[\nPQ = \\sqrt{304}\n\\]\n\nNow, using the Pythagorean theorem in \\(\\triangle PRS\\):\n\\[\nRS^2 = PS^2 - PR^2\n\\]\nSince \\(PS = PQ\\) (because \\(P\\) is the same point in both triangles), we have:\n\\[\nRS^2 = 304 - 144 = 160\n\\]\n\\[\nRS = \\sqrt{160} = 4\\sqrt{10}\n\\]\n\nNow, we can find the area of \\(\\triangle QPS\\) using the formula for the area of a triangle:\n\\[\n\\text{Area} = \\frac{1}{2} \\cdot \\text{base} \\cdot \\text{height}\n\\]\nHere, the base \\(QS = QR + RS = 8 + 4\\sqrt{10}\\) and the height is the perpendicular distance from \\(P\\) to \\(QS\\), which is \\(PR \\cdot \\sin(120^\\circ) = 12 \\cdot \\frac{\\sqrt{3}}{2} = 6\\sqrt{3}\\).\n\nThus, the area of \\(\\triangle QPS\\) is:\n\\[\n\\text{Area} = \\frac{1}{2} \\cdot (8 + 4\\sqrt{10}) \\cdot 6\\sqrt{3}\n\\]\n\\[\n= 3\\sqrt{3} \\cdot (8 + 4\\sqrt{10})\n\\]\n\\[\n= 24\\sqrt{3} + 12\\sqrt{30}\n\\]\n\nTherefore, the area of \\(\\triangle QPS\\) is \\(24\\sqrt{3} + 12\\sqrt{30}\\).'
'To find the area of the shaded region, we start by calculating the area of the square and the area of the quarter-circles.\n\n1. **Area of the Square:**\n   The side length of the square is 6 units. Therefore, the area of the square is:\n   \\[\n   \\text{Area of the square} = 6 \\times 6 = 36 \\text{ square units}\n   \\]\n\n2. **Area of the Quarter-Circles:**\n   Each quarter-circle has a radius of 3 units. The area of a full circle with radius 3 is:\n   \\[\n   \\text{Area of full circle} = \\pi \\times 3^2 = 9\\pi \\text{ square units}\n   \\]\n   Since each quarter-circle is one-fourth of a full circle, the area of one quarter-circle is:\n   \\[\n   \\text{Area of one quarter-circle} = \\frac{9\\pi}{4} \\text{ square units}\n   \\]\n   There are four quarter-circles, so the total area of the quarter-circles is:\n   \\[\n   \\text{Total area of quarter-circles} = 4 \\times \\frac{9\\pi}{4} = 9\\pi \\text{ square units}\n   \\]\n\n3. **Area of the Shaded Region:**\n   The shaded region is the area of the square minus the total area of the quarter-circles:\n   \\[\n   \\text{Area of the shaded region} = 36 - 9\\pi \\text{ square units}\n   \\]\n\nThe area of the shaded region is expressed in the form \\(a - b\\pi\\), where \\(a = 36\\) and \\(b = 9\\). Therefore, \\(a + b = 36 + 9 = 45\\).\n\nThus, the value of \\(a + b\\) is \\(\\boxed{45}\\).'
'In triangle \\(ABC\\), points \\(D\\) and \\(E\\) are the midpoints of sides \\(AB\\) and \\(AC\\), respectively. According to the Midsegment Theorem, the segment \\(DE\\) is parallel to side \\(BC\\) and its length is half the length of \\(BC\\).\n\nGiven that \\(BC\\) measures 6 inches, the length of segment \\(DE\\) is:\n\n\\[\nDE = \\frac{1}{2} \\times BC = \\frac{1}{2} \\times 6 = 3 \\text{ inches}\n\\]\n\nThus, the measure of segment \\(DE\\) is \\(\\boxed{3}\\) inches.'
"To find the perimeter of the decagon, we need to determine the lengths of all its sides. The decagon is composed of segments that meet at right angles, forming a path around the shape.\n\nLet's label the vertices of the decagon as \\( A_1, A_2, \\ldots, A_{10} \\) in a clockwise direction starting from the bottom-left corner.\n\n1. **\\( A_1A_2 \\):** The length is given as 12.\n2. **\\( A_2A_3 \\):** This is a vertical segment. Since \\( A_3 \\) is directly above \\( A_2 \\) and the horizontal segment \\( A_2A_3 \\) is 0.5 units, the vertical segment \\( A_2A_3 \\) is 0.5 units.\n3. **\\( A_3A_4 \\):** This is a horizontal segment. Since \\( A_4 \\) is directly to the left of \\( A_3 \\) and the vertical segment \\( A_3A_4 \\) is 1 unit, the horizontal segment \\( A_3A_4 \\) is 1 unit.\n4. **\\( A_4A_5 \\):** This is a vertical segment. Since \\( A_5 \\) is directly above \\( A_4 \\) and the horizontal segment \\( A_4A_5 \\) is 2 units, the vertical segment \\( A_4A_5 \\) is 2 units.\n5. **\\( A_5A_6 \\):** This is a horizontal segment. Since \\( A_6 \\) is directly to the left of \\( A_5 \\) and the vertical segment \\( A_5A_6 \\) is 0.7 units, the horizontal segment \\( A_5A_6 \\) is 0.7 units.\n6. **\\( A_6A_7 \\):** This is a vertical segment. Since \\( A_7 \\) is directly above \\( A_6 \\) and the horizontal segment \\( A_6A_7 \\) is 1 unit, the vertical segment \\( A_6A_7 \\) is 1 unit.\n7. **\\( A_7A_8 \\):** This is a horizontal segment. Since \\( A_8 \\) is directly to the left of \\( A_7 \\) and the vertical segment \\( A_7A_8 \\) is 1.3 units, the horizontal segment \\( A_7A_8 \\) is 1.3 units.\n8. **\\( A_8A_9 \\):** This is a vertical segment. Since \\( A_9 \\) is directly below \\( A_8 \\) and the horizontal segment \\( A_8A_9 \\) is 0.8 units, the vertical segment \\( A_8A_9 \\) is 0.8 units.\n9. **\\( A_9A_{10} \\):** This is a horizontal segment. "To solve this problem, we need to find the measure of angle \\(\\angle ADC\\) given that the shaded region is \\(\\frac{5}{12}\\) of the area of the larger circle.\n\nFirst, let's calculate the area of the larger circle. The radius of the larger circle is 2, so its area is:\n\\[\n\\text{Area of larger circle} = \\pi \\times 2^2 = 4\\pi.\n\\]\n\nThe shaded region is \\(\\frac{5}{12}\\) of this area, so the area of the shaded region is:\n\\[\n\\text{Area of shaded region} = \\frac{5}{12} \\times 4\\pi = \\frac{20\\pi}{12} = \\frac{5\\pi}{3}.\n\\]\n\nThe shaded region consists of two parts: the sector of the larger circle and the sector of the smaller circle. The area of the sector of the larger circle is:\n\\[\n\\text{Area of sector of larger circle} = \\frac{\\theta}{360^\\circ} \\times 4\\pi,\n\\]\nwhere \\(\\theta\\) is the measure of \\(\\angle ADC\\).\n\nThe area of the sector of the smaller circle is:\n\\[\n\\text{Area of sector of smaller circle} = \\frac{\\theta}{360^\\circ} \\times \\pi.\n\\]\n\nThe shaded region is the area of the sector of the larger circle minus the area of the sector of the smaller circle:\n\\[\n\\frac{\\theta}{360^\\circ} \\times 4\\pi - \\frac{\\theta}{360^\\circ} \\times \\pi = \\frac{5\\pi}{3}.\n\\]\n\nSimplifying the left side, we have:\n\\[\n\\frac{\\theta}{360^\\circ} \\times 3\\pi = \\frac{5\\pi}{3}.\n\\]\n\nDividing both sides by \\(\\pi\\), we get:\n\\[\n\\frac{\\theta}{360^\\circ} \\times 3 = \\frac{5}{3}.\n\\]\n\nSolving for \\(\\theta\\), we multiply both sides by 360:\n\\[\n\\theta \\times 3 = 360 \\times \\frac{5}{3}.\n\\]\n\nSimplifying the right side:\n\\[\n\\theta \\times 3 = 600.\n\\]\n\nDividing both sides by 3:\n\\[\n\\theta = 200.\n\\]\n\nTherefore, the measure of \\(\\angle ADC\\) is \\(\\boxed{200}\\) degrees."
'To solve this problem, we need to compare the volumes of the original and new cylindrical containers.\n\nThe volume \\( V \\) of a cylinder is given by the formula:\n\n\\[ V = \\pi r^2 h \\]\n\nwhere \\( r \\) is the radius and \\( h \\) is the height of the cylinder.\n\n1. **Calculate the volume of one original container:**\n\n   The radius of the original container is 4 cm. Therefore, the volume of one original container is:\n\n   \\[ V_{\\text{original}} = \\pi (4)^2 h = 16\\pi h \\]\n\n2. **Calculate the total volume of sand in all original containers:**\n\n   Sean has 12 original containers, so the total volume of sand is:\n\n   \\[ V_{\\text{total original}} = 12 \\times 16\\pi h = 192\\pi h \\]\n\n3. **Calculate the volume of one new container:**\n\n   The radius of the new container is 8 cm. "To solve this problem, we first need to determine the radius of the fan and then calculate the distance traveled by a point on the outer edge of the fan.\n\n1. **Determine the radius of the fan:**\n\n   A point halfway between the center and the outer edge of the fan travels 97,968 inches in 15 minutes. Since this point is halfway, its radius is half of the full radius of the fan, which we'll call \\( r/2 \\).\n\n   The fan rotates at 80 revolutions per minute, so in 15 minutes, it makes:\n   \\[\n   80 \\times 15 = 1200 \\text{ revolutions}\n   \\]\n\n   The distance traveled by the point halfway is the circumference of its path times the number of revolutions:\n   \\[\n   1200 \\times \\pi \\times \\frac{r}{2} = 97,968\n   \\]\n\n   Solving for \\( r \\), we have:\n   \\[\n   600\\pi r = 97,968\n   \\]\n   \\[\n   r = \\frac{97,968}{600\\pi}\n   \\]\n\n2. **Calculate the distance traveled by a point on the outer edge:**\n\n   A point on the outer edge of the fan travels a full circumference, which is \\( 2\\pi r \\), for each revolution. In 30 minutes, the fan makes:\n   \\[\n   80 \\times 30 = 2400 \\text{ revolutions}\n   \\]\n\n   The distance traveled by a point on the outer edge is:\n   \\[\n   2400 \\times 2\\pi r\n   \\]\n\n   Substituting the value of \\( r \\) from above:\n   \\[\n   2400 \\times 2\\pi \\times \\frac{97,968}{600\\pi} = 2400 \\times \\frac{97,968}{300}\n   \\]\n\n   Simplifying:\n   \\[\n   2400 \\times \\frac{97,968}{300} = 2400 \\times 326.56 = 783,744\n   \\]\n\nTherefore, a point on the outer edge of the fan travels \\( 783,744 \\) inches in 30 minutes."

"To determine the number of positive integers \\( p \\) for which a triangle with sides \\( 3p-1 \\), \\( 3p \\), and \\( p^2 + 1 \\) can exist, we need to satisfy the triangle inequality conditions:\n\n1. \\( (3p-1) + 3p > p^2 + 1 \\)\n2. \\( (3p-1) + (p^2 + 1) > 3p \\)\n3. \\( 3p + (p^2 + 1) > 3p-1 \\)\n\nLet's analyze each inequality:\n\n### Inequality 1:\n\\[\n(3p-1) + 3p > p^2 + 1\n\\]\n\\[\n6p - 1 > p^2 + 1\n\\]\n\\[\n6p - 1 - 1 > p^2\n\\]\n\\[\n6p - 2 > p^2\n\\]\n\\[\np^2 - 6p + 2 < 0\n\\]\n\nTo solve \\( p^2 - 6p + 2 < 0 \\), we find the roots of the quadratic equation \\( p^2 - 6p + 2 = 0 \\) using the quadratic formula:\n\\[\np = \\frac{-b \\pm \\sqrt{b^2 - 4ac}}{2a} = \\frac{6 \\pm \\sqrt{36 - 8}}{2} = \\frac{6 \\pm \\sqrt{28}}{2} = \\frac{6 \\pm 2\\sqrt{7}}{2} = 3 \\pm \\sqrt{7}\n\\]\n\nThe roots are \\( 3 + \\sqrt{7} \\) and \\( 3 - \\sqrt{7} \\). Since \\( \\sqrt{7} \\approx 2.64575 \\), we have:\n\\[\n3 + \\sqrt{7} \\approx 5.64575 \\quad \\text{and} \\quad 3 - \\sqrt{7} \\approx 0.35425\n\\]\n\nThe inequality \\( p^2 - 6p + 2 < 0 \\) holds for \\( 3 - \\sqrt{7} < p < 3 + \\sqrt{7} \\).\n\n### Inequality 2:\n\\[\n(3p-1) + (p^2 + 1) > 3p\n\\]\n\\[\n3p - 1 + p^2 + 1 > 3p\n\\]\n\\[\np^2 > 0\n\\]\n\nThis inequality is always true for \\( p > 0 \\).\n\n### Inequality 3:\n\\[\n3p + (p^2 + 1) > 3p - 1\n\\]\n\\[\n3p + p^2 + 1 > 3p - 1\n\\]\n\\[\np^2 + 1 > -1\n\\]\n\nThis inequality is always true for all \\( p \\).\n\n### Conclusion:\nThe only non-trivial inequality is \\( 3 - \\sqrt{7} < p < 3 + \\sqrt{7} \\). Since \\( p \\) must be a positive integer, we consider the integer values of \\( p \\) within this range:\n- \\( 3 - \\sqrt{7} \\approx 0.35425 \\)\n- \\( 3 + \\sqrt{7} \\approx 5.64575 \\)\n\nThus, the possible integer values for \\( p \\) are \\( 1, 2, 3, 4, 5 \\).\n\nTherefore, there are \\(\\boxed{5}\\) positive integer values of \\( p \\) for which the triangle can exist."
"To find the range of possible integer lengths for the third side of a triangle with sides 18 and 29, we use the triangle inequality theorem. The triangle inequality states that the sum of the lengths of any two sides of a triangle must be greater than the length of the third side. Therefore, for a triangle with sides \\(a\\), \\(b\\), and \\(c\\), the following inequalities must hold:\n\n1. \\(a + b > c\\)\n2. \\(a + c > b\\)\n3. \\(b + c > a\\)\n\nIn this problem, let the third side be \\(x\\). We have:\n\n1. \\(18 + 29 > x\\)\n2. \\(18 + x > 29\\)\n3. \\(29 + x > 18\\)\n\nLet's solve these inequalities:\n\n1. \\(18 + 29 > x\\) simplifies to \\(x < 47\\).\n2. \\(18 + x > 29\\) simplifies to \\(x > 11\\).\n3. \\(29 + x > 18\\) simplifies to \\(x > -11\\), which is always true since \\(x\\) is positive.\n\nCombining the valid inequalities, we have \\(11 < x < 47\\). Since \\(x\\) must be an integer, the possible values for \\(x\\) are 12, 13, 14, ..., 46.\n\nThe maximum integer value for \\(x\\) is 46, and the minimum integer value for \\(x\\) is 12. Therefore, the positive difference between the maximum and minimum length of the third side is:\n\n\\[ 46 - 12 = 34 \\]\n\nThus, the positive difference is \\(\\boxed{34}\\)."

The formula for the circumference of a circle is:\n\n\\[ C = 2\\pi r \\]\n\nwhere \\( r \\) is the radius. Substituting \\( r = 4 \\) inches, we get:\n\n\\[ C = 2\\pi \\times 4 = 8\\pi \\]\n\nThe full circle is divided into four equal parts, each representing a quarter-circle. Therefore, the length of the arc of one quarter-circle is:\n\n\\[ \\text{Arc length of quarter-circle} = \\frac{1}{4} \\times 8\\pi = 2\\pi \\]\n\nThus, the length of the arc on the discarded quarter-circle is \\( \\boxed{2\\pi} \\).'
'To solve this problem, we need to determine the internal angle at each vertex of the star-polygon formed on the clock face.\n\nThe clock face can be thought of as a regular 12-gon (dodecagon), where each vertex corresponds to a number on the clock. The star-polygon is formed by connecting each number to the fifth number counted clockwise, which creates a star with 12 vertices.\n\nThe key to finding the internal angle at each vertex of the star is to understand the relationship between the number of vertices and the step size used to create the star. The step size here is 5, as each vertex is connected to the fifth vertex clockwise.\n\nThe formula for the internal angle of a regular star-polygon with \\( n \\) vertices and step size \\( k \\) is given by:\n\n\\[\n\\theta = \\left(1 - \\frac{2k}{n}\\right) \\times 180^\\circ\n\\]\n\nIn this case, \\( n = 12 \\) and \\( k = 5 \\). "To find the area of triangle \\(ACD\\), we first need to find the length of the altitude \\(CD\\) from \\(C\\) to \\(AB\\).\n\nWe start by using Heron's formula to find the area of triangle \\(ABC\\). The semi-perimeter \\(s\\) of triangle \\(ABC\\) is given by:\n\n\\[\ns = \\frac{AB + AC + BC}{2} = \\frac{17 + 8 + 15}{2} = 20\n\\]\n\nThe area \\(K\\) of triangle \\(ABC\\) is:\n\n\\[\nK = \\sqrt{s(s - AB)(s - AC)(s - BC)} = \\sqrt{20(20 - 17)(20 - 8)(20 - 15)}\n\\]\n\n\\[\nK = \\sqrt{20 \\times 3 \\times 12 \\times 5} = \\sqrt{3600} = 60\n\\]\n\nNow, the area of triangle \\(ABC\\) can also be expressed as:\n\n\\[\nK = \\frac{1}{2} \\times AB \\times CD\n\\]\n\nSubstituting the known values:\n\n\\[\n60 = \\frac{1}{2} \\times 17 \\times CD\n\\]\n\nSolving for \\(CD\\):\n\n\\[\n60 = \\frac{17}{2} \\times CD \\implies CD = \\frac{120}{17}\n\\]\n\nNow, we find the area of triangle \\(ACD\\). The base \\(AC = 8\\) and the height is the same \\(CD\\):\n\n\\[\n\\text{Area of } \\triangle ACD = \\frac{1}{2} \\times AC \\times CD = \\frac{1}{2} \\times 8 \\times \\frac{120}{17}\n\\]\n\n\\[\n= \\frac{480}{34} = \\frac{240}{17}\n\\]\n\nThus, the area of triangle \\(ACD\\) is \\(\\boxed{\\frac{240}{17}}\\)."
'To determine the greatest number of interior right angles a convex octagon can have, we start by considering the sum of the interior angles of the octagon. The formula for the sum of the interior angles of a polygon with \\( n \\) sides is:\n\n\\[\n(n-2) \\times 180^\\circ\n\\]\n\nFor an octagon (\\( n = 8 \\)):\n\n\\[\n(8-2) \\times 180^\\circ = 6 \\times 180^\\circ = 1080^\\circ\n\\]\n\nIf an octagon has \\( k \\) right angles, the sum of these angles is \\( 90^\\circ \\times k \\). The remaining \\( 8-k \\) angles must sum to:\n\n\\[\n1080^\\circ - 90^\\circ \\times k\n\\]\n\nFor the octagon to remain convex, each of the remaining angles must be less than \\( 180^\\circ \\). Therefore, the sum of the remaining angles must be:\n\n\\[\n1080^\\circ - 90^\\circ \\times k < 180^\\circ \\times (8-k)\n\\]\n\nSimplifying this inequality:\n\n\\[\n1080 - 90k < 1440 - 180k\n\\]\n\n\\[\n90k < 360\n\\]\n\n\\[\nk < 4\n\\]\n\nThus, the maximum number of right angles \\( k \\) is 3. Therefore, the greatest number of interior right angles a convex octagon can have is \\(\\boxed{3}\\).'

"To solve this problem, we need to analyze the geometry of the circle and the chord. Let's consider a circle with center \\( O \\) and radius \\( r \\). The chord \\( AB \\) is perpendicular to the radius \\( OC \\) at its midpoint \\( C \\), where \\( OC = \\frac{r}{2} \\).\n\nSince \\( AB \\) is perpendicular to \\( OC \\) at \\( C \\), \\( C \\) is the midpoint of \\( AB \\). By the Pythagorean theorem in the right triangle \\( OCA \\), we have:\n\n\\[\nOA^2 = OC^2 + CA^2 \\implies r^2 = \\left(\\frac{r}{2}\\right)^2 + CA^2\n\\]\n\nSolving for \\( CA \\), we get:\n\n\\[\nCA^2 = r^2 - \\left(\\frac{r}{2}\\right)^2 = r^2 - \\frac{r^2}{4} = \\frac{3r^2}{4}\n\\]\n\n\\[\nCA = \\frac{\\sqrt{3}r}{2}\n\\]\n\nSince \\( C \\) is the midpoint, \\( AB = 2 \\times CA = \\sqrt{3}r \\).\n\nThe chord \\( AB \\) divides the circle into two segments. We need to find the areas of these segments. The angle \\( \\theta \\) subtended by the chord at the center \\( O \\) can be found using the cosine rule in \\( \\triangle OAB \\):\n\n\\[\n\\cos \\theta = \\frac{OC}{OA} = \\frac{\\frac{r}{2}}{r} = \\frac{1}{2}\n\\]\n\nThus, \\( \\theta = 60^\\circ = \\frac{\\pi}{3} \\).\n\nThe area of the sector \\( OAB \\) is:\n\n\\[\n\\text{Area of sector } OAB = \\frac{\\theta}{2\\pi} \\cdot \\pi r^2 = \\frac{\\pi r^2}{6}\n\\]\n\nThe area of triangle \\( OAB \\) is:\n\n\\[\n\\text{Area of } \\triangle OAB = \\frac{1}{2} \\cdot OA \\cdot OB \\cdot \\sin \\theta = \\frac{1}{2} \\cdot r \\cdot r \\cdot \\frac{\\sqrt{3}}{2} = \\frac{\\sqrt{3}r^2}{4}\n\\]\n\nThe area of the segment (smaller region) is:\n\n\\[\n\\text{Area of segment} = \\text{Area of sector} - \\text{Area of triangle} = \\frac{\\pi r^2}{6} - \\frac{\\sqrt{3}r^2}{4}\n\\]\n\nThe area of the larger region is the area of the circle minus the area of the smaller segment:\n\n\\[\n\\text{Area of larger region} = \\pi r^2 - \\left(\\frac{\\pi r^2}{6} - \\frac{\\sqrt{3}r^2}{4}\\right) = \\pi r^2 - \\frac{\\pi r^2}{6} + \\frac{\\sqrt{3}r^2}{4}\n\\]\n\n\\[\n= \\frac{5\\pi r^2}{6} + \\frac{\\sqrt{3}r^2}{4}\n\\]\n\nThe ratio of the area of the larger region to the smaller region is:\n\n\\[\n\\frac{\\frac{5\\pi r^2}{6} + \\frac{\\sqrt{3}r^2}{4}}{\\frac{\\pi r^2}{6} - \\frac{\\sqrt{3}r^2}{4}} = \\frac{5\\pi + \\frac{3\\sqrt{3}}{2}}{\\pi - \\frac{3\\sqrt{3}}{2}}\n\\]\n\nThis can be expressed in the form \\(\\frac{a\\pi + b\\sqrt{c}}{d\\pi - e\\sqrt{f}}\\) with \\(a = 5\\), \\(b = 3\\), \\(c = 3\\), \\(d = 1\\), \\(e = 3\\), and \\(f = 3\\).\n\nThe product \\(a \\cdot b \\cdot c \\cdot d \\cdot e \\cdot f = 5 \\cdot 3 \\cdot 3 \\cdot 1 \\cdot 3 \\cdot 3 = 405\\).\n\nThe remainder when 405 is divided by 1000 is \\(\\boxed{405}\\)."
"To find the cost of the aluminum used to make the container, we first need to calculate the surface area of the rectangular prism.\n\nThe container is a rectangular prism with dimensions \\(10'' \\times 10'' \\times 12''\\). The surface area \\(A\\) of a rectangular prism is given by the formula:\n\n\\[\nA = 2(lw + lh + wh)\n\\]\n\nwhere \\(l\\), \\(w\\), and \\(h\\) are the length, width, and height of the prism, respectively.\n\nSubstituting the given dimensions:\n\n\\[\nA = 2(10 \\times 10 + 10 \\times 12 + 10 \\times 12)\n\\]\n\nCalculating each term:\n\n- \\(10 \\times 10 = 100\\)\n- \\(10 \\times 12 = 120\\)\n- \\(10 \\times 12 = 120\\)\n\nAdding these:\n\n\\[\n100 + 120 + 120 = 340\n\\]\n\nNow, multiply by 2 to find the total surface area:\n\n\\[\nA = 2 \\times 340 = 680 \\text{ square inches}\n\\]\n\nThe cost of aluminum is \\(\\$0.05\\) per square inch. "To solve this problem, we need to find the vertices of the quadrilateral region defined by the system of inequalities:\n\n1. \\(2x + y \\le 4\\)\n2. \\(x + y \\ge 1\\)\n3. \\(x \\ge 0\\)\n4. \\(y \\ge 0\\)\n\nLet's find the intersection points of these lines to determine the vertices of the quadrilateral.\n\n**Step 1: Find the intersection of \\(2x + y = 4\\) and \\(x + y = 1\\).**\n\nSubtract the second equation from the first:\n\n\\[\n(2x + y) - (x + y) = 4 - 1 \\implies x = 3\n\\]\n\nSubstitute \\(x = 3\\) into \\(x + y = 1\\):\n\n\\[\n3 + y = 1 \\implies y = -2\n\\]\n\nHowever, \\(y = -2\\) does not satisfy \\(y \\ge 0\\), so this intersection is not within the feasible region.\n\n**Step 2: Find the intersection of \\(2x + y = 4\\) and \\(x = 0\\).**\n\nSubstitute \\(x = 0\\) into \\(2x + y = 4\\):\n\n\\[\n2(0) + y = 4 \\implies y = 4\n\\]\n\nSo, one vertex is \\((0, 4)\\).\n\n**Step 3: Find the intersection of \\(x + y = 1\\) and \\(x = 0\\).**\n\nSubstitute \\(x = 0\\) into \\(x + y = 1\\):\n\n\\[\n0 + y = 1 \\implies y = 1\n\\]\n\nSo, another vertex is \\((0, 1)\\).\n\n**Step 4: Find the intersection of \\(2x + y = 4\\) and \\(y = 0\\).**\n\nSubstitute \\(y = 0\\) into \\(2x + y = 4\\):\n\n\\[\n2x + 0 = 4 \\implies x = 2\n\\]\n\nSo, another vertex is \\((2, 0)\\).\n\n**Step 5: Find the intersection of \\(x + y = 1\\) and \\(y = 0\\).**\n\nSubstitute \\(y = 0\\) into \\(x + y = 1\\):\n\n\\[\nx + 0 = 1 \\implies x = 1\n\\]\n\nSo, another vertex is \\((1, 0)\\).\n\nThe vertices of the quadrilateral are \\((0, 4)\\), \\((0, 1)\\), \\((2, 0)\\), and \\((1, 0)\\).\n\n**Step 6: Calculate the lengths of the sides of the quadrilateral.**\n\n- Distance between \\((0, 4)\\) and \\((0, 1)\\): \n  \\[\n  \\sqrt{(0 - 0)^2 + (4 - 1)^2} = \\sqrt{3^2} = 3\n  \\]\n\n- Distance between \\((0, 1)\\) and \\((1, 0)\\): \n  \\[\n  \\sqrt{(1 - 0)^2 + (0 - 1)^2} = \\sqrt{1 + 1} = \\sqrt{2}\n  \\]\n\n- Distance between \\((1, 0)\\) and \\((2, 0)\\): \n  \\[\n  \\sqrt{(2 - 1)^2 + (0 - 0)^2} = \\sqrt{1} = 1\n  \\]\n\n- Distance between \\((2, 0)\\) and \\((0, 4)\\): \n  \\[\n  \\sqrt{(2 - 0)^2 + (0 - 4)^2} = \\sqrt{4 + 16} = \\sqrt{20} = 2\\sqrt{5}\n  \\]\n\nThe longest side is \\(2\\sqrt{5}\\), where \\(a = 2\\) and \\(b = 5\\).\n\nThus, \\(a + b = 2 + 5 = \\boxed{7}\\)."
'In \\(\\triangle ABC\\), since \\(AC = BC\\), the triangle is isosceles, and angles opposite equal sides are equal. Therefore, \\(\\angle ABC = \\angle ACB\\).\n\nGiven that \\(m\\angle BAC = 40^\\circ\\), we can find the sum of the other two angles in the triangle:\n\n\\[\nm\\angle ABC + m\\angle ACB = 180^\\circ - m\\angle BAC = 180^\\circ - 40^\\circ = 140^\\circ\n\\]\n\nSince \\(\\angle ABC = \\angle ACB\\), we have:\n\n\\[\n2 \\times m\\angle ABC = 140^\\circ\n\\]\n\nSolving for \\(m\\angle ABC\\), we get:\n\n\\[\nm\\angle ABC = \\frac{140^\\circ}{2} = 70^\\circ\n\\]\n\nThus, the measure of angle \\(x\\), which is \\(\\angle ABC\\), is \\(\\boxed{70}\\) degrees.'
'To find the volume of the cube, we first need to determine the side length of the cube. The sphere is inscribed in the cube, meaning the diameter of the sphere is equal to the side length of the cube.\n\nThe volume of a sphere is given by the formula:\n\n\\[\nV = \\frac{4}{3} \\pi r^3\n\\]\n\nwhere \\( r \\) is the radius of the sphere. We are given that the volume of the sphere is \\(\\frac{\\pi}{6}\\) cubic inches. Therefore, we have:\n\n\\[\n\\frac{4}{3} \\pi r^3 = \\frac{\\pi}{6}\n\\]\n\nTo solve for \\( r^3 \\), we can divide both sides by \\(\\pi\\):\n\n\\[\n\\frac{4}{3} r^3 = \\frac{1}{6}\n\\]\n\nNext, multiply both sides by \\(\\frac{3}{4}\\) to isolate \\( r^3 \\):\n\n\\[\nr^3 = \\frac{1}{6} \\times \\frac{3}{4} = \\frac{1}{8}\n\\]\n\nTaking the cube root of both sides gives us the radius \\( r \\):\n\n\\[\nr = \\sqrt[3]{\\frac{1}{8}} = \\frac{1}{2}\n\\]\n\nSince the diameter of the sphere is twice the radius, the diameter is:\n\n\\[\n2r = 2 \\times \\frac{1}{2} = 1\n\\]\n\nThe diameter of the sphere is equal to the side length of the cube, so the side length of the cube is 1 inch.\n\nThe volume of the cube is given by the formula:\n\n\\[\nV = s^3\n\\]\n\nwhere \\( s \\) is the side length of the cube. Substituting \\( s = 1 \\), we find:\n\n\\[\nV = 1^3 = 1\n\\]\n\nTherefore, the volume of the cube is \\(\\boxed{1}\\) cubic inch.'

The room is a rectangular prism with dimensions 12 feet (length), 10 feet (width), and 8 feet (height).\n\nThe gecko starts on a side wall (10 feet by 8 feet), 1 foot from the ceiling and 1 foot from the back wall. The fly is on the opposite side wall, 1 foot from the floor and 1 foot from the front wall.\n\nOne possible net configuration is to unfold the room such that the gecko's starting wall and the fly's wall are adjacent, with the ceiling in between. This configuration allows us to draw a straight line from the gecko to the fly.\n\nIn this configuration, the gecko's starting position is 1 foot from the top of the wall and 1 foot from the back wall. The fly's position is 1 foot from the bottom of the opposite wall and 1 foot from the front wall. When unfolded, the gecko's path can be visualized as a straight line across the ceiling and the two side walls.\n\nThe total horizontal distance the gecko needs to cover is the length of the room (12 feet) plus the 1-foot distance from the back wall to the gecko and the 1-foot distance from the front wall to the fly, totaling 14 feet. The vertical distance is the height of the room (8 feet) minus the 1-foot distance from the ceiling to the gecko and the 1-foot distance from the floor to the fly, totaling 6 feet.\n\nUsing the Pythagorean theorem, the shortest path is the hypotenuse of a right triangle with legs of 14 feet and 6 feet:\n\n\\[\n\\text{Shortest path} = \\sqrt{14^2 + 6^2} = \\sqrt{196 + 36} = \\sqrt{232} = 2\\sqrt{58}.\n\\]\n\nThus, the length of the shortest path the gecko can take is \\( \\boxed{2\\sqrt{58}} \\)."
'To find \\(\\cos \\angle RPS\\), we first need to understand the relationship between \\(\\angle RPQ\\) and \\(\\angle RPS\\). Since \\(S\\), \\(P\\), and \\(Q\\) are collinear and \\(P\\) is between \\(S\\) and \\(Q\\), \\(\\angle RPQ\\) and \\(\\angle RPS\\) are supplementary angles. This means that:\n\n\\[\n\\angle RPQ + \\angle RPS = 180^\\circ\n\\]\n\nThe cosine of a supplementary angle is the negative of the cosine of the original angle:\n\n\\[\n\\cos \\angle RPS = -\\cos \\angle RPQ\n\\]\n\nGiven that \\(\\sin \\angle RPQ = \\frac{7}{25}\\), we can use the Pythagorean identity to find \\(\\cos \\angle RPQ\\):\n\n\\[\n\\sin^2 \\theta + \\cos^2 \\theta = 1\n\\]\n\nSubstituting the given sine value:\n\n\\[\n\\left(\\frac{7}{25}\\right)^2 + \\cos^2 \\angle RPQ = 1\n\\]\n\n\\[\n\\frac{49}{625} + \\cos^2 \\angle RPQ = 1\n\\]\n\n\\[\n\\cos^2 \\angle RPQ = 1 - \\frac{49}{625} = \\frac{576}{625}\n\\]\n\nTaking the square root:\n\n\\[\n\\cos \\angle RPQ = \\pm \\frac{24}{25}\n\\]\n\nSince \\(\\angle RPQ\\) is an acute angle (as implied by the context of the problem), we take the positive value:\n\n\\[\n\\cos \\angle RPQ = \\frac{24}{25}\n\\]\n\nThus, the cosine of the supplementary angle \\(\\angle RPS\\) is:\n\n\\[\n\\cos \\angle RPS = -\\cos \\angle RPQ = -\\frac{24}{25}\n\\]\n\nTherefore, \\(\\cos \\angle RPS = \\boxed{-\\frac{24}{25}}\\).'
'To solve this problem, we need to compare the volume of the ice cream scoops to the volume of the cone.\n\nFirst, calculate the volume of one ice cream scoop, which is a sphere with radius 1 inch. The formula for the volume of a sphere is:\n\n\\[\nV_{\\text{sphere}} = \\frac{4}{3} \\pi r^3\n\\]\n\nSubstituting \\( r = 1 \\):\n\n\\[\nV_{\\text{sphere}} = \\frac{4}{3} \\pi (1)^3 = \\frac{4}{3} \\pi\n\\]\n\nNext, calculate the volume of the cone with radius 2 inches and height 5 inches. The formula for the volume of a cone is:\n\n\\[\nV_{\\text{cone}} = \\frac{1}{3} \\pi r^2 h\n\\]\n\nSubstituting \\( r = 2 \\) and \\( h = 5 \\):\n\n\\[\nV_{\\text{cone}} = \\frac{1}{3} \\pi (2)^2 (5) = \\frac{1}{3} \\pi (4)(5) = \\frac{20}{3} \\pi\n\\]\n\nNow, determine how many scoops are needed to fill the cone by dividing the volume of the cone by the volume of one scoop:\n\n\\[\n\\text{Number of scoops} = \\frac{V_{\\text{cone}}}{V_{\\text{sphere}}} = \\frac{\\frac{20}{3} \\pi}{\\frac{4}{3} \\pi} = \\frac{20}{3} \\times \\frac{3}{4} = \\frac{20}{4} = 5\n\\]\n\nTherefore, 5 scoops of ice cream are needed to fill the cone.'

This means that \\( D \\) is the arc midpoint of the arc \\( BC \\) that does not contain \\( A \\).\n\nIn a cyclic quadrilateral \\( ABCD \\), where \\( \\overline{AD} \\) is the angle bisector of \\( \\angle BAC \\), the ratio \\( \\frac{AD}{CD} \\) is equal to the ratio of the other two sides \\( \\frac{AB}{AC} \\).\n\nGiven:\n- \\( AB = 7 \\)\n- \\( AC = 8 \\)\n\nBy the Angle Bisector Theorem in the context of a cyclic quadrilateral, we have:\n\\[\n\\frac{AD}{CD} = \\frac{AB}{AC} = \\frac{7}{8}\n\\]\n\nThus, the value of \\( \\frac{AD}{CD} \\) is \\(\\boxed{\\frac{7}{8}}\\).'
"To find the area of the convex pentagon with vertices \\((-1, -1)\\), \\((-3, 4)\\), \\((1, 7)\\), \\((6, 5)\\), and \\((3, -1)\\), we can use the Shoelace Theorem (or Gauss's area formula for polygons). The formula is:\n\n\\[\n\\text{Area} = \\frac{1}{2} \\left| \\sum_{i=1}^{n-1} (x_i y_{i+1} - y_i x_{i+1}) + (x_n y_1 - y_n x_1) \\right|\n\\]\n\nFirst, we list the coordinates in order and repeat the first point at the end:\n\n\\[\n\\begin{array}{c|c}\nx & y \\\\\n\\hline\n-1 & -1 \\\\\n-3 & 4 \\\\\n1 & 7 \\\\\n6 & 5 \\\\\n3 & -1 \\\\\n-1 & -1 \\\\\n\\end{array}\n\\]\n\nNow, we apply the Shoelace Theorem:\n\n\\[\n\\text{Area} = \\frac{1}{2} \\left| (-1 \\cdot 4 + -3 \\cdot 7 + 1 \\cdot 5 + 6 \\cdot -1 + 3 \\cdot -1) - (-1 \\cdot -3 + 4 \\cdot 1 + 7 \\cdot 6 + 5 \\cdot 3 + -1 \\cdot -1) \\right|\n\\]\n\nCalculate each part:\n\n\\[\n= \\frac{1}{2} \\left| (-4 - 21 + 5 - 6 - 3) - (3 + 4 + 42 + 15 + 1) \\right|\n\\]\n\n\\[\n= \\frac{1}{2} \\left| (-29) - (65) \\right|\n\\]\n\n\\[\n= \\frac{1}{2} \\left| -29 - 65 \\right|\n\\]\n\n\\[\n= \\frac{1}{2} \\left| -94 \\right|\n\\]\n\n\\[\n= \\frac{1}{2} \\times 94 = 47\n\\]\n\nThus, the area of the pentagon is \\(\\boxed{47}\\)."
'To find the ratio of the areas of circles \\( A \\) and \\( B \\), we first need to determine the relationship between their radii based on the given arc lengths.\n\nThe length of an arc is given by the formula:\n\\[\n\\text{Arc Length} = \\frac{\\theta}{360^\\circ} \\times 2\\pi r\n\\]\nwhere \\(\\theta\\) is the central angle in degrees and \\(r\\) is the radius of the circle.\n\nFor circle \\( A \\), the arc length is:\n\\[\n\\frac{55}{360} \\times 2\\pi r_A\n\\]\n\nFor circle \\( B \\), the arc length is:\n\\[\n\\frac{40}{360} \\times 2\\pi r_B\n\\]\n\nSince the arc lengths are equal, we have:\n\\[\n\\frac{55}{360} \\times 2\\pi r_A = \\frac{40}{360} \\times 2\\pi r_B\n\\]\n\nWe can cancel \\(2\\pi\\) from both sides:\n\\[\n\\frac{55}{360} \\times r_A = \\frac{40}{360} \\times r_B\n\\]\n\nSimplifying, we get:\n\\[\n\\frac{55}{360} \\times r_A = \\frac{40}{360} \\times r_B\n\\]\n\nCancel the common factor of \\( \\frac{1}{360} \\):\n\\[\n55r_A = 40r_B\n\\]\n\nSolving for the ratio of the radii:\n\\[\n\\frac{r_A}{r_B} = \\frac{40}{55} = \\frac{8}{11}\n\\]\n\nThe area of a circle is proportional to the square of its radius. Therefore, the ratio of the areas of circles \\( A \\) and \\( B \\) is:\n\\[\n\\left(\\frac{r_A}{r_B}\\right)^2 = \\left(\\frac{8}{11}\\right)^2 = \\frac{64}{121}\n\\]\n\nThus, the ratio of the area of circle \\( A \\) to the area of circle \\( B \\) is \\(\\boxed{\\frac{64}{121}}\\).'
"To find the measure of \\(\\alpha\\), we need to use the fact that the sum of the interior angles of a hexagon is \\(720^\\circ\\). Since the hexagon is inscribed in a circle, it is a cyclic hexagon, and the opposite angles of a cyclic hexagon sum to \\(180^\\circ\\).\n\nGiven the angles \\(105^\\circ\\) and \\(110^\\circ\\), we can find their opposite angles. Let's denote the angles of the hexagon as \\(A, B, C, D, E, F\\) in order, with \\(F = 105^\\circ\\) and \\(B = 110^\\circ\\).\n\nThe opposite angle to \\(F\\) is \\(C\\), and the opposite angle to \\(B\\) is \\(E\\). Therefore, we have:\n\\[\nC + 105^\\circ = 180^\\circ \\quad \\Rightarrow \\quad C = 75^\\circ\n\\]\n\\[\nE + 110^\\circ = 180^\\circ \\quad \\Rightarrow \\quad E = 70^\\circ\n\\]\n\nNow, we need to find \\(\\alpha\\), which is angle \\(D\\). The sum of all interior angles of the hexagon is \\(720^\\circ\\), so:\n\\[\nA + 110^\\circ + 75^\\circ + D + 70^\\circ + 105^\\circ = 720^\\circ\n\\]\n\nSimplifying, we have:\n\\[\nA + D + 360^\\circ = 720^\\circ\n\\]\n\\[\nA + D = 360^\\circ\n\\]\n\nSince \\(A\\) and \\(D\\) are opposite angles in the cyclic hexagon, they must also sum to \\(180^\\circ\\). Therefore, we have:\n\\[\nA + D = 180^\\circ\n\\]\n\nThus, the measure of \\(\\alpha\\) (angle \\(D\\)) is:\n\\[\n\\alpha = 180^\\circ - A\n\\]\n\nSince \\(A + D = 180^\\circ\\), and we have already established that \\(A + D = 180^\\circ\\), we can directly conclude that:\n\\[\n\\alpha = D = 180^\\circ - A\n\\]\n\nGiven that \\(A + D = 180^\\circ\\), and knowing the sum of the angles, we can solve for \\(D\\) using the equation:\n\\[\nD = 180^\\circ - A\n\\]\n\nSince \\(A + D = 180^\\circ\\), and we have already established that \\(A + D = 180^\\circ\\), we can directly conclude that:\n\\[\n\\alpha = D = 180^\\circ - A\n\\]\n\nThus, the measure of \\(\\alpha\\) is \\(\\boxed{110^\\circ}\\)."
'To solve this problem, we need to understand the relationship between the centroid of a triangle and the areas of the smaller triangles formed by lines through the centroid.\n\nThe centroid \\( G \\) of a triangle divides each median into two segments, with the ratio \\( 2:1 \\), where the longer segment is between the vertex and the centroid. In this problem, the line through \\( G \\) that is parallel to \\( BC \\) will divide the triangle \\( \\triangle ABC \\) into two smaller triangles, \\( \\triangle AMN \\) and a trapezoid \\( MNCB \\).\n\nSince \\( G \\) is the centroid, it divides each median into a \\( 2:1 \\) ratio. Therefore, the line \\( MN \\) is parallel to \\( BC \\) and divides the triangle into two regions with areas in the ratio \\( 2:1 \\). Specifically, the area of \\( \\triangle AMN \\) is \\( \\frac{1}{3} \\) of the area of \\( \\triangle ABC \\), and the area of the trapezoid \\( MNCB \\) is \\( \\frac{2}{3} \\) of the area of \\( \\triangle ABC \\).\n\nGiven that the area of \\( \\triangle ABC \\) is 144, the area of \\( \\triangle AMN \\) is:\n\\[\n\\text{Area of } \\triangle AMN = \\frac{1}{3} \\times 144 = 48.\n\\]\n\nNow, we need to find the area of \\( \\triangle ENG \\). Since \\( G \\) is the centroid, it divides each median into segments with a \\( 2:1 \\) ratio. The triangle \\( \\triangle ENG \\) is one of the six smaller triangles formed by the medians intersecting at the centroid. Each of these smaller triangles has the same area.\n\nThe area of \\( \\triangle ENG \\) is therefore:\n\\[\n\\text{Area of } \\triangle ENG = \\frac{1}{6} \\times 144 = 24.\n\\]\n\nThus, the area of \\( \\triangle ENG \\) is \\(\\boxed{24}\\).'
"To find the sum of the altitudes of a triangle with side lengths 10, 10, and 12, we first need to determine the area of the triangle. This triangle is isosceles with two equal sides of length 10 and a base of length 12.\n\nWe can use Heron's formula to find the area of the triangle. First, we calculate the semi-perimeter \\( s \\):\n\n\\[\ns = \\frac{10 + 10 + 12}{2} = 16\n\\]\n\nNow, we apply Heron's formula for the area \\( A \\):\n\n\\[\nA = \\sqrt{s(s-a)(s-b)(s-c)}\n\\]\n\nwhere \\( a = 10 \\), \\( b = 10 \\), and \\( c = 12 \\). Plugging in these values, we get:\n\n\\[\nA = \\sqrt{16(16-10)(16-10)(16-12)} = \\sqrt{16 \\times 6 \\times 6 \\times 4}\n\\]\n\n\\[\nA = \\sqrt{16 \\times 144} = \\sqrt{2304} = 48\n\\]\n\nNow, we calculate the altitudes corresponding to each side. The formula for the altitude \\( h \\) corresponding to a side \\( a \\) is given by:\n\n\\[\nh = \\frac{2A}{a}\n\\]\n\n1. Altitude to side 12:\n\n\\[\nh_{12} = \\frac{2 \\times 48}{12} = \\frac{96}{12} = 8\n\\]\n\n2. Altitude to side 10 (both altitudes are the same since the triangle is isosceles):\n\n\\[\nh_{10} = \\frac{2 \\times 48}{10} = \\frac{96}{10} = 9.6\n\\]\n\nSince there are two equal sides of length 10, we have two altitudes of length 9.6.\n\nFinally, the sum of the altitudes is:\n\n\\[\nh_{12} + 2 \\times h_{10} = 8 + 2 \\times 9.6 = 8 + 19.2 = 27.2\n\\]\n\nThus, the sum of the lengths of the altitudes is \\( \\boxed{27.2} \\)."
"To solve this problem, we need to find the length of the fold \\( l \\) when corner \\( A \\) of a rectangular piece of paper is folded over to coincide with point \\( C \\) on the opposite side. Given that \\( BC = 5 \\) inches and the width of the paper is 8 inches, we can use the properties of the rectangle and the Pythagorean theorem to find \\( l \\).\n\nLet's denote the rectangle as \\( ABCD \\) with \\( A \\) at the top right corner, \\( B \\) at the top left corner, \\( C \\) at the bottom left corner, and \\( D \\) at the bottom right corner. The fold \\( l \\) is the line segment from \\( A \\) to \\( C \\).\n\nSince \\( A \\) is folded to \\( C \\), the fold \\( l \\) is the hypotenuse of a right triangle formed by the width of the rectangle and the segment \\( BC \\).
