'To find the length of each edge of a regular tetrahedron given its height, we can use the relationship between the height \\( h \\) and the edge length \\( a \\) of a regular tetrahedron. The height \\( h \\) from a vertex to the center of the opposite face can be expressed in terms of the edge length \\( a \\) as follows:\n\n\\[\nh = \\frac{\\sqrt{2}}{3}a\n\\]\n\nGiven that the height \\( h \\) is 20 inches, we can set up the equation:\n\n\\[\n20 = \\frac{\\sqrt{2}}{3}a\n\\]\n\nTo solve for \\( a \\), multiply both sides by \\( \\frac{3}{\\sqrt{2}} \\):\n\n\\[\na = 20 \\times \\frac{3}{\\sqrt{2}} = \\frac{60}{\\sqrt{2}}\n\\]\n\nTo simplify \\( \\frac{60}{\\sqrt{2}} \\), multiply the numerator and the denominator by \\( \\sqrt{2} \\):\n\n\\[\na = \\frac{60 \\sqrt{2}}{2} = 30\\sqrt{2}\n\\]\n\nThus, the length of each edge of the tetrahedron is \\( 30\\sqrt{2} \\) inches.'
"To solve this problem, we need to calculate the surface area of the resulting solid after performing the described operations on the original cube.\n\n1. **Original Cube:**\n   - The original cube has a side length of 3 inches.\n   - The surface area of the original cube is \\(6 \\times 3^2 = 54\\) square inches.\n\n2. **Cubes Cut from Each Corner:**\n   - A cube of side 1 inch is cut from each of the 8 corners of the original cube.\n   - Each 1-inch cube has a surface area of \\(6 \\times 1^2 = 6\\) square inches.\n   - However, when a 1-inch cube is cut from a corner, it exposes 3 new square inches of surface area (since 3 faces of the 1-inch cube are exposed).\n   - Therefore, cutting 8 such cubes exposes \\(8 \\times 3 = 24\\) square inches of new surface area.\n\n3. **Cubes Inserted into Each Corner:**\n   - A cube of side 2 inches is inserted into each corner.\n   - Each 2-inch cube has a surface area of \\(6 \\times 2^2 = 24\\) square inches.\n   - When a 2-inch cube is inserted into a corner, it covers 3 square inches of the original cube's surface area (since it fits into the corner).\n   - However, it also exposes 3 new square inches of its own surface area (since 3 faces of the 2-inch cube are exposed).\n   - Therefore, inserting 8 such cubes exposes \\(8 \\times 3 = 24\\) square inches of new surface area.\n\n4. **Total Surface Area:**\n   - The original surface area of the cube is 54 square inches.\n   - Cutting the 1-inch cubes and inserting the 2-inch cubes both result in a net increase of 24 square inches of surface area.\n   - Therefore, the total surface area of the resulting solid is \\(54 + 24 = 78\\) square inches.\n\nThus, the number of square inches in the surface area of the resulting solid is \\(\\boxed{78}\\)."
'To find the area of the larger region formed by a chord of length 6 units in a circle with a radius of 6 units, we first need to determine the central angle corresponding to the chord.\n\n1. **Find the central angle:**\n\n   The chord length \\( c = 6 \\) and the radius \\( r = 6 \\). We can use the formula for the chord length in terms of the central angle \\(\\theta\\) (in radians):\n\n   \\[\n   c = 2r \\sin\\left(\\frac{\\theta}{2}\\right)\n   \\]\n\n   Substituting the known values:\n\n   \\[\n   6 = 2 \\times 6 \\times \\sin\\left(\\frac{\\theta}{2}\\right)\n   \\]\n\n   \\[\n   \\sin\\left(\\frac{\\theta}{2}\\right) = \\frac{1}{2}\n   \\]\n\n   The angle \\(\\frac{\\theta}{2}\\) that satisfies \\(\\sin\\left(\\frac{\\theta}{2}\\right) = \\frac{1}{2}\\) is \\(\\frac{\\pi}{6}\\). Therefore, the full central angle \\(\\theta\\) is:\n\n   \\[\n   \\theta = 2 \\times \\frac{\\pi}{6} = \\frac{\\pi}{3}\n   \\]\n\n2. **Calculate the area of the sector:**\n\n   The area of the sector with central angle \\(\\theta = \\frac{\\pi}{3}\\) is given by:\n\n   \\[\n   \\text{Area of sector} = \\frac{\\theta}{2\\pi} \\times \\pi r^2 = \\frac{\\frac{\\pi}{3}}{2\\pi} \\times \\pi \\times 6^2\n   \\]\n\n   \\[\n   = \\frac{1}{6} \\times 36\\pi = 6\\pi\n   \\]\n\n3. **Calculate the area of the triangle:**\n\n   The triangle formed by the radii and the chord is an equilateral triangle with side length 6. The area \\(A\\) of an equilateral triangle with side length \\(s\\) is:\n\n   \\[\n   A = \\frac{\\sqrt{3}}{4} s^2 = \\frac{\\sqrt{3}}{4} \\times 6^2 = 9\\sqrt{3}\n   \\]\n\n4. **Calculate the area of the segment:**\n\n   The area of the segment is the area of the sector minus the area of the triangle:\n\n   \\[\n   \\text{Area of segment} = 6\\pi - 9\\sqrt{3}\n   \\]\n\n5. **Calculate the area of the larger region:**\n\n   The total area of the circle is:\n\n   \\[\n   \\pi \\times 6^2 = 36\\pi\n   \\]\n\n   The area of the larger region is the total area of the circle minus the area of the smaller segment:\n\n   \\[\n   \\text{Area of larger region} = 36\\pi - (6\\pi - 9\\sqrt{3}) = 30\\pi + 9\\sqrt{3}\n   \\]\n\nThus, the area of the larger region is \\(\\boxed{30\\pi + 9\\sqrt{3}}\\) square units.'
"To find the area of triangle \\(ABE\\), we first need to determine the coordinates of point \\(E\\) on segments \\(AD\\) and \\(GB\\).\n\nSince squares \\(ABGH\\) and \\(BCDF\\) are adjacent, we can place them on a coordinate plane for convenience. Let:\n- \\(A = (0, 0)\\)\n- \\(B = (5, 0)\\)\n- \\(G = (5, 5)\\)\n- \\(H = (0, 5)\\)\n- \\(C = (15, 0)\\)\n- \\(D = (15, 10)\\)\n- \\(F = (5, 10)\\)\n\nThe line \\(AD\\) is vertical, so its equation is \\(x = 15\\). The line \\(GB\\) is horizontal, so its equation is \\(y = 0\\).\n\nPoint \\(E\\) is on both segments \\(AD\\) and \\(GB\\), so it must satisfy the equations of both lines. "To solve this problem, we need to find the surface area of one of the two pieces formed when a plane parallel to edges \\(AB\\) and \\(CD\\) cuts the regular tetrahedron \\(ABCD\\) in half.\n\nFirst, let's understand the geometry of the regular tetrahedron. A regular tetrahedron has four equilateral triangular faces, and all its edges are of equal length. In this case, each edge is 2 units long.\n\nThe plane that cuts the tetrahedron is parallel to edges \\(AB\\) and \\(CD\\) and lies halfway between them. This means the plane is parallel to the line joining the midpoints of \\(AB\\) and \\(CD\\).\n\nSince the plane is parallel to these edges and cuts the tetrahedron in half, it will intersect the tetrahedron along a plane that is equidistant from the vertices \\(A\\) and \\(B\\) and from the vertices \\(C\\) and \\(D\\). This plane will form a smaller equilateral triangle inside the tetrahedron.\n\nThe original tetrahedron has a height from any vertex to the opposite face. The height \\(h\\) of a regular tetrahedron with side length \\(s\\) is given by:\n\\[\nh = \\frac{\\sqrt{2}}{3} s\n\\]\nFor \\(s = 2\\), the height is:\n\\[\nh = \\frac{\\sqrt{2}}{3} \\times 2 = \\frac{2\\sqrt{2}}{3}\n\\]\n\nThe plane cuts the height in half, so the height from the plane to the base of the tetrahedron is:\n\\[\n\\frac{h}{2} = \\frac{\\sqrt{2}}{3}\n\\]\n\nThe cross-section formed by the plane is an equilateral triangle. The side length of this equilateral triangle can be found using similar triangles. The side length of the smaller equilateral triangle is half the side length of the original tetrahedron, which is:\n\\[\n\\frac{2}{2} = 1\n\\]\n\nNow, let's calculate the surface area of one of the pieces. Each piece consists of:\n1. The equilateral triangle formed by the plane (side length 1).\n2. Three trapezoidal faces formed by cutting the original triangular faces of the tetrahedron.\n\nThe area of the equilateral triangle with side length 1 is:\n\\[\n\\text{Area} = \\frac{\\sqrt{3}}{4} \\times 1^2 = \\frac{\\sqrt{3}}{4}\n\\]\n\nEach trapezoidal face is formed by cutting an equilateral triangle of side length 2 into two smaller triangles. The area of each original equilateral triangle is:\n\\[\n\\text{Area} = \\frac{\\sqrt{3}}{4} \\times 2^2 = \\sqrt{3}\n\\]\n\nSince the plane cuts the tetrahedron in half, each trapezoidal face has half the area of the original triangle:\n\\[\n\\text{Area of each trapezoid} = \\frac{\\sqrt{3}}{2}\n\\]\n\nThere are three such trapezoidal faces, so the total area of the trapezoidal faces is:\n\\[\n3 \\times \\frac{\\sqrt{3}}{2} = \\frac{3\\sqrt{3}}{2}\n\\]\n\nAdding the area of the equilateral triangle, the total surface area of one piece is:\n\\[\n\\frac{\\sqrt{3}}{4} + \\frac{3\\sqrt{3}}{2} = \\frac{\\sqrt{3}}{4} + \\frac{6\\sqrt{3}}{4} = \\frac{7\\sqrt{3}}{4}\n\\]\n\nThus, the surface area of one of the pieces is \\(\\boxed{\\frac{7\\sqrt{3}}{4}}\\)."
"To find the perimeter of the second isosceles triangle with integer side lengths and an area of 120, we start by considering the formula for the area of a triangle:\n\n\\[\n\\text{Area} = \\frac{1}{2} \\times \\text{base} \\times \\text{height}\n\\]\n\nFor an isosceles triangle with side lengths \\(a, a, b\\), where \\(b\\) is the base, the height \\(h\\) can be found using the Pythagorean theorem:\n\n\\[\nh = \\sqrt{a^2 - \\left(\\frac{b}{2}\\right)^2}\n\\]\n\nThe area of the triangle is given by:\n\n\\[\n\\frac{1}{2} \\times b \\times \\sqrt{a^2 - \\left(\\frac{b}{2}\\right)^2} = 120\n\\]\n\nSimplifying, we have:\n\n\\[\nb \\times \\sqrt{a^2 - \\left(\\frac{b}{2}\\right)^2} = 240\n\\]\n\nSquaring both sides:\n\n\\[\nb^2 \\times \\left(a^2 - \\frac{b^2}{4}\\right) = 240^2\n\\]\n\n\\[\nb^2 \\times a^2 - \\frac{b^4}{4} = 57600\n\\]\n\nWe know one triangle has sides 17, 17, and 30. Let's find the second triangle. We need to find integer values of \\(a\\) and \\(b\\) such that the area is 120 and the sides form an isosceles triangle.\n\nLet's try different integer values for \\(b\\) and solve for \\(a\\):\n\n1. **\\(b = 24\\):**\n\n   \\[\n   24 \\times \\sqrt{a^2 - 12^2} = 240\n   \\]\n\n   \\[\n   \\sqrt{a^2 - 144} = 10\n   \\]\n\n   \\[\n   a^2 - 144 = 100\n   \\]\n\n   \\[\n   a^2 = 244 \\quad \\Rightarrow \\quad a = \\sqrt{244} \\approx 15.62\n   \\]\n\n   \\(a\\) is not an integer.\n\n2. **\\(b = 25\\):**\n\n   \\[\n   25 \\times \\sqrt{a^2 - 12.5^2} = 240\n   \\]\n\n   \\[\n   \\sqrt{a^2 - 156.25} = 9.6\n   \\]\n\n   \\[\n   a^2 - 156.25 = 92.16\n   \\]\n\n   \\[\n   a^2 = 248.41\n   \\]\n\n   \\(a\\) is not an integer.\n\n3. **\\(b = 26\\):**\n\n   \\[\n   26 \\times \\sqrt{a^2 - 13^2} = 240\n   \\]\n\n   \\[\n   \\sqrt{a^2 - 169} = \\frac{240}{26} \\approx 9.23\n   \\]\n\n   \\[\n   a^2 - 169 = 85.23\n   \\]\n\n   \\[\n   a^2 = 254.23\n   \\]\n\n   \\(a\\) is not an integer.\n\n4. **\\(b = 25\\):**\n\n   \\[\n   25 \\times \\sqrt{a^2 - 12.5^2} = 240\n   \\]\n\n   \\[\n   \\sqrt{a^2 - 156.25} = 9.6\n   \\]\n\n   \\[\n   a^2 - 156.25 = 92.16\n   \\]\n\n   \\[\n   a^2 = 248.41\n   \\]\n\n   \\(a\\) is not an integer.\n\n5. **\\(b = 16\\):**\n\n   \\[\n   16 \\times \\sqrt{a^2 - 8^2} = 240\n   \\]\n\n   \\[\n   \\sqrt{a^2 - 64} = 15\n   \\]\n\n   \\[\n   a^2 - 64 = 225\n   \\]\n\n   \\[\n   a^2 = 289 \\quad \\Rightarrow \\quad a = 17\n   \\]\n\n   The sides are 17, 17, and 16. The perimeter is:\n\n   \\[\n   17 + 17 + 16 = 50\n   \\]\n\nThus, the perimeter of the second triangle is \\(\\boxed{50}\\)."

'To determine how many different isosceles triangles can be formed using the side lengths \\(2, 3, 5, 7, \\) and \\(11\\), we need to ensure that the triangle inequality holds for each set of side lengths. Specifically, for an isosceles triangle with two equal sides of length \\(a\\) and a third side of length \\(b\\), the triangle inequality requires:\n\n1. \\(a + a > b\\) or \\(2a > b\\)\n2. \\(a + b > a\\) or \\(b > 0\\) (which is always true since \\(b\\) is a positive length)\n3. \\(a + b > a\\) or \\(b > 0\\) (which is always true since \\(b\\) is a positive length)\n\nWe will check each possible pair of equal sides:\n\n1. **Equal sides of length 2:**\n   - \\(2a = 4\\), so \\(b < 4\\). Possible \\(b\\) values: \\(2, 3\\).\n   - Triangles: \\((2, 2, 2)\\), \\((2, 2, 3)\\).\n\n2. **Equal sides of length 3:**\n   - \\(2a = 6\\), so \\(b < 6\\). Possible \\(b\\) values: \\(2, 3, 5\\).\n   - Triangles: \\((3, 3, 2)\\), \\((3, 3, 3)\\), \\((3, 3, 5)\\).\n\n3. **Equal sides of length 5:**\n   - \\(2a = 10\\), so \\(b < 10\\). Possible \\(b\\) values: \\(2, 3, 5, 7\\).\n   - Triangles: \\((5, 5, 2)\\), \\((5, 5, 3)\\), \\((5, 5, 5)\\), \\((5, 5, 7)\\).\n\n4. **Equal sides of length 7:**\n   - \\(2a = 14\\), so \\(b < 14\\). Possible \\(b\\) values: \\(2, 3, 5, 7, 11\\).\n   - Triangles: \\((7, 7, 2)\\), \\((7, 7, 3)\\), \\((7, 7, 5)\\), \\((7, 7, 7)\\), \\((7, 7, 11)\\).\n\n5. **Equal sides of length 11:**\n   - \\(2a = 22\\), so \\(b < 22\\). Possible \\(b\\) values: \\(2, 3, 5, 7, 11\\).\n   - Triangles: \\((11, 11, 2)\\), \\((11, 11, 3)\\), \\((11, 11, 5)\\), \\((11, 11, 7)\\), \\((11, 11, 11)\\).\n\nNow, we count the number of valid triangles:\n\n- For equal sides of length 2: 2 triangles\n- For equal sides of length 3: 3 triangles\n- For equal sides of length 5: 4 triangles\n- For equal sides of length 7: 5 triangles\n- For equal sides of length 11: 5 triangles\n\nAdding these up gives us a total of \\(2 + 3 + 4 + 5 + 5 = 19\\) different isosceles triangles.\n\nThus, the number of different triangles with exactly two equal sides that can be formed is \\(\\boxed{19}\\).'
'A regular pentagon has rotational symmetry, meaning it can be rotated by certain angles and still look the same as its original position. Since a regular pentagon has 5 sides, it can be rotated by multiples of \\( \\frac{360^\\circ}{5} = 72^\\circ \\) and still coincide with its original position.\n\nThe minimum non-zero angle of rotation that will make the pentagon coincide with its original position is \\( 72^\\circ \\).\n\nTherefore, the minimum number of degrees it must be rotated is \\( \\boxed{72} \\).'
"To find the area of the square, we need to determine the side length of the square. Let's analyze the configuration:\n\n1. The large circle has a radius of 2 and is centered at the center of the square.\n2. Each of the four smaller circles has a radius of 1 and is tangent to two sides of the square and the large circle.\n\nConsider one of the smaller circles. Its center is 1 unit away from each of the two sides of the square it is tangent to. Therefore, the center of the smaller circle is located at a distance of 1 unit from each of these sides.\n\nThe center of the large circle is at the center of the square, and the radius of the large circle is 2. The distance from the center of the large circle to the center of a smaller circle is the sum of their radii, which is \\(2 + 1 = 3\\).\n\nNow, let's find the distance from the center of the square to the center of one of the smaller circles. Since the smaller circle is tangent to two sides of the square, its center is at a distance of 1 unit from each of these sides. Therefore, the center of the smaller circle is at coordinates \\((1, 1)\\) relative to the bottom-left corner of the square.\n\nThe center of the square is at \\((s/2, s/2)\\), where \\(s\\) is the side length of the square. The distance from the center of the square to the center of the smaller circle is given by the Pythagorean theorem:\n\n\\[\n\\sqrt{\\left(\\frac{s}{2} - 1\\right)^2 + \\left(\\frac{s}{2} - 1\\right)^2} = 3\n\\]\n\nSimplifying, we have:\n\n\\[\n\\sqrt{2\\left(\\frac{s}{2} - 1\\right)^2} = 3\n\\]\n\n\\[\n\\sqrt{2} \\left(\\frac{s}{2} - 1\\right) = 3\n\\]\n\n\\[\n\\frac{s}{2} - 1 = \\frac{3}{\\sqrt{2}}\n\\]\n\n\\[\n\\frac{s}{2} = 1 + \\frac{3}{\\sqrt{2}}\n\\]\n\n\\[\ns = 2\\left(1 + \\frac{3}{\\sqrt{2}}\\right)\n\\]\n\n\\[\ns = 2 + \\frac{6}{\\sqrt{2}}\n\\]\n\nRationalizing the denominator:\n\n\\[\ns = 2 + \\frac{6\\sqrt{2}}{2}\n\\]\n\n\\[\ns = 2 + 3\\sqrt{2}\n\\]\n\nThe area of the square is:\n\n\\[\ns^2 = (2 + 3\\sqrt{2})^2\n\\]\n\nExpanding this, we get:\n\n\\[\ns^2 = 4 + 12\\sqrt{2} + 18\n\\]\n\n\\[\ns^2 = 22 + 12\\sqrt{2}\n\\]\n\nThus, the area of the square is \\(\\boxed{22 + 12\\sqrt{2}}\\)."
"To solve this problem, we need to determine the fraction of the two squares that is shaded.\n\nFirst, let's denote the side length of each square as \\( s \\). Since squares \\( ABCD \\) and \\( EFGH \\) are equal in area, they both have side length \\( s \\).\n\nThe diagonal \\( AC \\) of square \\( ABCD \\) is extended to point \\( J \\), which is the midpoint of \\( GH \\). Since \\( GH \\) is a side of square \\( EFGH \\), its length is also \\( s \\). Therefore, the coordinates of \\( G \\) and \\( H \\) are \\( (2s, -s) \\) and \\( (2s, 0) \\), respectively. The midpoint \\( J \\) of \\( GH \\) is \\((2s, -\\frac{s}{2})\\).\n\nThe line \\( BC \\) is horizontal and lies on the x-axis, so its equation is \\( y = 0 \\). The line \\( AC \\) has a slope of \\(-1\\) because it is a diagonal of the square \\( ABCD \\) with vertices \\( A(0, s) \\) and \\( C(s, 0) \\). Therefore, the equation of line \\( AC \\) is \\( y = -x + s \\).\n\nThe shaded region consists of two triangles: \\(\\triangle ABC\\) and \\(\\triangle CHJ\\).\n\n1. **Area of \\(\\triangle ABC\\):**\n\n   Since \\( ABCD \\) is a square, \\(\\triangle ABC\\) is a right triangle with legs of length \\( s \\). The area of \\(\\triangle ABC\\) is:\n   \\[\n   \\text{Area of } \\triangle ABC = \\frac{1}{2} \\times s \\times s = \\frac{s^2}{2}\n   \\]\n\n2. **Area of \\(\\triangle CHJ\\):**\n\n   The vertices of \\(\\triangle CHJ\\) are \\( C(s, 0) \\), \\( H(2s, 0) \\), and \\( J(2s, -\\frac{s}{2}) \\). The base \\( CH \\) is horizontal with length \\( s \\), and the height from \\( J \\) to line \\( CH \\) is \\(\\frac{s}{2}\\). The area of \\(\\triangle CHJ\\) is:\n   \\[\n   \\text{Area of } \\triangle CHJ = \\frac{1}{2} \\times s \\times \\frac{s}{2} = \\frac{s^2}{4}\n   \\]\n\n3. **Total shaded area:**\n\n   The total shaded area is the sum of the areas of \\(\\triangle ABC\\) and \\(\\triangle CHJ\\):\n   \\[\n   \\text{Total shaded area} = \\frac{s^2}{2} + \\frac{s^2}{4} = \\frac{2s^2}{4} + \\frac{s^2}{4} = \\frac{3s^2}{4}\n   \\]\n\n4. **Total area of the two squares:**\n\n   The total area of the two squares is:\n   \\[\n   2 \\times s^2 = 2s^2\n   \\]\n\n5. **Fraction of the two squares that is shaded:**\n\n   The fraction of the total area that is shaded is:\n   \\[\n   \\frac{\\text{Total shaded area}}{\\text{Total area of the two squares}} = \\frac{\\frac{3s^2}{4}}{2s^2} = \\frac{3}{8}\n   \\]\n\nTherefore, the fraction of the two squares that is shaded is \\(\\boxed{\\frac{3}{8}}\\)."
'To find \\(\\tan A\\), we start with the given equation \\(\\sin A = 2\\cos A\\).\n\nWe can express \\(\\tan A\\) in terms of \\(\\sin A\\) and \\(\\cos A\\) as follows:\n\\[\n\\tan A = \\frac{\\sin A}{\\cos A}\n\\]\n\nSubstituting the given equation into this expression, we have:\n\\[\n\\tan A = \\frac{2\\cos A}{\\cos A} = 2\n\\]\n\nThus, the value of \\(\\tan A\\) is \\(\\boxed{2}\\).'
"To find the area of pentagon \\( CMJKN \\), we will first determine the coordinates of the points involved and then calculate the area using the appropriate geometric methods.\n\n### Step 1: Determine the coordinates of points \\( A \\), \\( B \\), and \\( C \\).\n\nGiven:\n- \\( AB = 13 \\)\n- \\( AC = 5 \\)\n- \\( BC = 12 \\)\n\nLet's place point \\( C \\) at the origin, \\( C = (0, 0) \\).\n\nSince \\( AC = 5 \\), we can place point \\( A \\) at \\( (0, 5) \\).\n\nTo find the coordinates of \\( B \\), we use the distance formula:\n\\[\nBC = 12 \\implies B = (x, y) \\text{ such that } x^2 + y^2 = 12^2 = 144\n\\]\n\\[\nAB = 13 \\implies (x - 0)^2 + (y - 5)^2 = 13^2 = 169\n\\]\n\nSolving these equations:\n1. \\( x^2 + y^2 = 144 \\)\n2. \\( x^2 + (y - 5)^2 = 169 \\)\n\nExpanding the second equation:\n\\[\nx^2 + y^2 - 10y + 25 = 169\n\\]\nSubstitute \\( x^2 + y^2 = 144 \\) into the equation:\n\\[\n144 - 10y + 25 = 169\n\\]\n\\[\n169 - 10y = 169\n\\]\n\\[\n10y = 0 \\implies y = 0\n\\]\n\nSubstitute \\( y = 0 \\) back into \\( x^2 + y^2 = 144 \\):\n\\[\nx^2 = 144 \\implies x = 12 \\text{ or } x = -12\n\\]\n\nSince \\( B \\) must be on the positive x-axis (as \\( C = (0,0) \\) and \\( A = (0,5) \\)), we choose \\( B = (12, 0) \\).\n\n### Step 2: Determine the coordinates of points \\( M \\) and \\( N \\).\n\n- \\( M \\) is on \\( AC \\) such that \\( CM = 4 \\). Since \\( C = (0,0) \\) and \\( A = (0,5) \\), \\( M = (0, 4) \\).\n\n- \\( N \\) is on \\( BC \\) such that \\( CN = 4 \\). Since \\( C = (0,0) \\) and \\( B = (12,0) \\), \\( N = (4, 0) \\).\n\n### Step 3: Determine the coordinates of points \\( J \\) and \\( K \\).\n\n- \\( MJ \\) is perpendicular to \\( AB \\). Since \\( M = (0, 4) \\) and \\( AB \\) is vertical, \\( J \\) must have the same x-coordinate as \\( M \\), so \\( J = (0, y_J) \\).\n\n- \\( NK \\) is perpendicular to \\( AB \\). Since \\( N = (4, 0) \\) and \\( AB \\) is vertical, \\( K \\) must have the same x-coordinate as \\( N \\), so \\( K = (4, y_K) \\).\n\n### Step 4: Calculate the area of pentagon \\( CMJKN \\).\n\nThe pentagon \\( CMJKN \\) can be divided into two triangles \\( CMJ \\) and \\( CKN \\).\n\n#### Triangle \\( CMJ \\):\n- Vertices: \\( C = (0, 0) \\), \\( M = (0, 4) \\), \\( J = (0, y_J) \\)\n- Since \\( J \\) is vertically above \\( M \\), the area of \\( \\triangle CMJ \\) is zero.\n\n#### Triangle \\( CKN \\):\n- Vertices: \\( C = (0, 0) \\), \\( K = (4, y_K) \\), \\( N = (4, 0) \\)\n- The base \\( CN = 4 \\) and height \\( y_K \\).\n\nThe area of \\( \\triangle CKN \\) is:\n\\[\n\\text{Area} = \\frac{1}{2} \\times \\text{base} \\times \\text{height} = \\frac{1}{2} \\times 4 \\times y_K = 2y_K\n\\]\n\nSince \\( y_K = 4 \\) (as \\( K \\) is directly above \\( N \\) on the vertical line), the area of \\( \\triangle CKN \\) is:\n\\[\n2 \\times 4 = 8\n\\]\n\nThus, the area of pentagon \\( CMJKN \\) is \\( \\boxed{8} \\)."
'To find the radius of the larger gold sphere, we start by calculating the total volume of the 27 smaller spheres and then use that to determine the volume and radius of the larger sphere.\n\nThe volume \\( V \\) of a single sphere with radius \\( r \\) is given by the formula:\n\n\\[\nV = \\frac{4}{3} \\pi r^3\n\\]\n\nFor one of the smaller spheres with radius 3, the volume is:\n\n\\[\nV_{\\text{small}} = \\frac{4}{3} \\pi (3)^3 = \\frac{4}{3} \\pi \\times 27 = 36 \\pi\n\\]\n\nSince there are 27 such spheres, the total volume of all the smaller spheres is:\n\n\\[\nV_{\\text{total}} = 27 \\times 36 \\pi = 972 \\pi\n\\]\n\nThis total volume is then recast into a larger sphere. Let \\( R \\) be the radius of the larger sphere. The volume of the larger sphere is:\n\n\\[\nV_{\\text{large}} = \\frac{4}{3} \\pi R^3\n\\]\n\nSetting the total volume of the smaller spheres equal to the volume of the larger sphere, we have:\n\n\\[\n\\frac{4}{3} \\pi R^3 = 972 \\pi\n\\]\n\nDividing both sides by \\(\\pi\\) and then multiplying by \\(\\frac{3}{4}\\) to solve for \\( R^3 \\), we get:\n\n\\[\nR^3 = \\frac{3}{4} \\times 972 = 729\n\\]\n\nTaking the cube root of both sides, we find:\n\n\\[\nR = \\sqrt[3]{729} = 9\n\\]\n\nThus, the radius of the larger gold sphere is \\(\\boxed{9}\\).'
"To find the area of quadrilateral \\( DBEF \\), we first need to determine the coordinates of all the points involved.\n\nGiven:\n- \\( A(0, 6) \\)\n- \\( B(0, 0) \\)\n- \\( C(8, 0) \\)\n\nSince \\( D \\) and \\( E \\) are the midpoints of \\( \\overline{AB} \\) and \\( \\overline{BC} \\) respectively, we can find their coordinates as follows:\n\n- \\( D \\) is the midpoint of \\( \\overline{AB} \\):\n  \\[\n  D = \\left( \\frac{0 + 0}{2}, \\frac{6 + 0}{2} \\right) = (0, 3)\n  \\]\n\n- \\( E \\) is the midpoint of \\( \\overline{BC} \\):\n  \\[\n  E = \\left( \\frac{0 + 8}{2}, \\frac{0 + 0}{2} \\right) = (4, 0)\n  \\]\n\nThe coordinates of \\( F \\) are given as \\( \\left( \\frac{8}{3}, 2 \\right) \\).\n\nNow, we have the coordinates of all points:\n- \\( D(0, 3) \\)\n- \\( B(0, 0) \\)\n- \\( E(4, 0) \\)\n- \\( F\\left( \\frac{8}{3}, 2 \\right) \\)\n\nTo find the area of quadrilateral \\( DBEF \\), we can use the Shoelace Theorem (or the surveyor's formula). The formula for the area of a polygon given its vertices \\((x_1, y_1), (x_2, y_2), \\ldots, (x_n, y_n)\\) is:\n\n\\[\n\\text{Area} = \\frac{1}{2} \\left| \\sum_{i=1}^{n-1} (x_i y_{i+1} - y_i x_{i+1}) + (x_n y_1 - y_n x_1) \\right|\n\\]\n\nApplying this to quadrilateral \\( DBEF \\) with vertices in order \\( D(0, 3), B(0, 0), E(4, 0), F\\left( \\frac{8}{3}, 2 \\right) \\):\n\n\\[\n\\text{Area} = \\frac{1}{2} \\left| (0 \\cdot 0 + 0 \\cdot 0 + 4 \\cdot 2 + \\frac{8}{3} \\cdot 3) - (3 \\cdot 0 + 0 \\cdot 4 + 0 \\cdot \\frac{8}{3} + 2 \\cdot 0) \\right|\n\\]\n\nCalculating each term:\n- \\( 0 \\cdot 0 = 0 \\)\n- \\( 0 \\cdot 0 = 0 \\)\n- \\( 4 \\cdot 2 = 8 \\)\n- \\( \\frac{8}{3} \\cdot 3 = 8 \\)\n\nThe sum of these products is \\( 0 + 0 + 8 + 8 = 16 \\).\n\nThe sum of the products in the other direction is \\( 0 + 0 + 0 + 0 = 0 \\).\n\nThus, the area is:\n\\[\n\\text{Area} = \\frac{1}{2} \\left| 16 - 0 \\right| = \\frac{1}{2} \\times 16 = 8\n\\]\n\nTherefore, the area of quadrilateral \\( DBEF \\) is \\(\\boxed{8}\\)."
'Since triangle \\(ABD\\) is a reflection of triangle \\(ACD\\) over segment \\(AD\\), the angles in these triangles are congruent. This means that \\(\\angle ABD = \\angle ACD\\) and \\(\\angle BAD = \\angle CAD\\).\n\nGiven that \\(\\angle BAC = 40^\\circ\\), we can express this as:\n\\[\n\\angle BAC = \\angle BAD + \\angle CAD = 40^\\circ\n\\]\n\nSince \\(\\angle BAD = \\angle CAD\\), we have:\n\\[\n2\\angle BAD = 40^\\circ\n\\]\n\\[\n\\angle BAD = 20^\\circ\n\\]\n\nNow, consider the angles in triangle \\(ABD\\):\n\\[\n\\angle ABD + \\angle BAD + \\angle ADB = 180^\\circ\n\\]\n\nSince \\(\\angle ABD = \\angle ACD\\) and \\(\\angle ADB = \\angle ADC\\), we have:\n\\[\n\\angle ABD = \\angle ACD = \\angle B\n\\]\n\nThus, the angles in triangle \\(ABC\\) are:\n\\[\n\\angle BAC = 40^\\circ, \\quad \\angle ABC = \\angle B, \\quad \\angle ACB = \\angle B\n\\]\n\nSince the sum of angles in triangle \\(ABC\\) is \\(180^\\circ\\), we have:\n\\[\n40^\\circ + \\angle B + \\angle B = 180^\\circ\n\\]\n\\[\n40^\\circ + 2\\angle B = 180^\\circ\n\\]\n\\[\n2\\angle B = 140^\\circ\n\\]\n\\[\n\\angle B = 70^\\circ\n\\]\n\nTherefore, the measure of angle \\(B\\) is \\(\\boxed{70^\\circ}\\).'
'Since triangles \\( \\triangle ABC \\) and \\( \\triangle XYZ \\) are similar, the corresponding sides are proportional. We are given that side \\( AB \\) of \\( \\triangle ABC \\) measures 4 units, side \\( BC \\) measures 6 units, and side \\( XY \\) of \\( \\triangle XYZ \\) measures 14 units. We need to find the measure of side \\( YZ \\).\n\nFirst, we determine the scale factor of the similarity. The corresponding sides \\( AB \\) and \\( XY \\) give us the scale factor:\n\n\\[\n\\text{Scale factor} = \\frac{XY}{AB} = \\frac{14}{4} = \\frac{7}{2}\n\\]\n\nNow, we use this scale factor to find the length of side \\( YZ \\), which corresponds to side \\( BC \\) in \\( \\triangle ABC \\):\n\n\\[\nYZ = BC \\times \\text{Scale factor} = 6 \\times \\frac{7}{2} = \\frac{42}{2} = 21\n\\]\n\nThus, the measure of side \\( YZ \\) is \\( \\boxed{21} \\) units.'
'To find the area of the isosceles trapezoid, we first identify the lengths of the sides. The trapezoid has two parallel sides (bases) and two non-parallel sides (legs). The given side lengths are 4 cm, 10 cm, 16 cm, and 10 cm. The parallel sides must be the 4 cm and 16 cm sides, as they are the only pair that can be bases of the trapezoid with the other two sides being equal (10 cm each), which are the legs.\n\nThe formula for the area of a trapezoid is:\n\n\\[\n\\text{Area} = \\frac{1}{2} \\times (\\text{Base}_1 + \\text{Base}_2) \\times \\text{Height}\n\\]\n\nwhere \\(\\text{Base}_1\\) and \\(\\text{Base}_2\\) are the lengths of the parallel sides, and \\(\\text{Height}\\) is the perpendicular distance between the bases.\n\nFirst, we need to find the height of the trapezoid. Since the trapezoid is isosceles, the height can be found by dropping perpendiculars from the endpoints of the shorter base (4 cm) to the longer base (16 cm), creating two right triangles.\n\nThe difference in length between the longer base and the shorter base is:\n\n\\[\n16 \\, \\text{cm} - 4 \\, \\text{cm} = 12 \\, \\text{cm}\n\\]\n\nSince the trapezoid is isosceles, this difference is split equally between the two right triangles, so each right triangle has a base of:\n\n\\[\n\\frac{12 \\, \\text{cm}}{2} = 6 \\, \\text{cm}\n\\]\n\nNow, we can use the Pythagorean theorem to find the height \\(h\\) of the trapezoid. In one of the right triangles, the hypotenuse is the leg of the trapezoid (10 cm), and the base is 6 cm. Therefore:\n\n\\[\nh^2 + 6^2 = 10^2\n\\]\n\n\\[\nh^2 + 36 = 100\n\\]\n\n\\[\nh^2 = 64\n\\]\n\n\\[\nh = \\sqrt{64} = 8 \\, \\text{cm}\n\\]\n\nNow that we have the height, we can calculate the area of the trapezoid:\n\n\\[\n\\text{Area} = \\frac{1}{2} \\times (4 \\, \\text{cm} + 16 \\, \\text{cm}) \\times 8 \\, \\text{cm}\n\\]\n\n\\[\n\\text{Area} = \\frac{1}{2} \\times 20 \\, \\text{cm} \\times 8 \\, \\text{cm}\n\\]\n\n\\[\n\\text{Area} = 10 \\, \\text{cm} \\times 8 \\, \\text{cm}\n\\]\n\n\\[\n\\text{Area} = 80 \\, \\text{cm}^2\n\\]\n\nThus, the area of the trapezoid is \\(80\\) square centimeters.'
'From noon to 3 p.m., the hour hand of the clock moves from the 12 to the 3, which is a quarter of the way around the clock. Since the hour hand is 6 inches long, the distance traveled by the tip of the hour hand is a quarter of the circumference of the circle with radius 6 inches.\n\nThe circumference of a circle is given by \\(2\\pi r\\), where \\(r\\) is the radius. Therefore, the circumference of the circle traced by the hour hand is \\(2\\pi \\times 6 = 12\\pi\\) inches. The distance traveled by the hour hand is a quarter of this circumference:\n\n\\[\n\\text{Distance traveled by hour hand} = \\frac{1}{4} \\times 12\\pi = 3\\pi \\text{ inches}\n\\]\n\nThe minute hand moves from the 12 to the 12 again, completing a full circle, in one hour. From noon to 3 p.m., the minute hand completes three full circles. Since the minute hand is 8 inches long, the distance traveled by the tip of the minute hand is three times the circumference of the circle with radius 8 inches.\n\nThe circumference of the circle traced by the minute hand is \\(2\\pi \\times 8 = 16\\pi\\) inches. The distance traveled by the minute hand in three hours is:\n\n\\[\n\\text{Distance traveled by minute hand} = 3 \\times 16\\pi = 48\\pi \\text{ inches}\n\\]\n\nThe ratio of the distance traveled by the tip of the hour hand to the distance traveled by the tip of the minute hand is:\n\n\\[\n\\frac{3\\pi}{48\\pi} = \\frac{3}{48} = \\frac{1}{16}\n\\]\n\nThus, the ratio is \\(\\boxed{\\frac{1}{16}}\\).'
"To find the degree measure of the smallest angle in triangle \\(PQS\\), we first need to determine the positions of the centers of the circles and the distances between them.\n\nThe centers of the circles are:\n- \\(P\\) at \\((1, \\sqrt{3})\\)\n- \\(Q\\) at \\((-2, 0)\\)\n- \\(R\\) at \\((0, 0)\\)\n- \\(S\\) at \\((2, 0)\\)\n\nThe distances between the centers of the circles are:\n- \\(PQ\\): The distance between \\(P\\) and \\(Q\\) is calculated as follows:\n  \\[\n  PQ = \\sqrt{(1 - (-2))^2 + (\\sqrt{3} - 0)^2} = \\sqrt{3^2 + (\\sqrt{3})^2} = \\sqrt{9 + 3} = \\sqrt{12} = 2\\sqrt{3}\n  \\]\n\n- \\(PS\\): The distance between \\(P\\) and \\(S\\) is calculated as follows:\n  \\[\n  PS = \\sqrt{(1 - 2)^2 + (\\sqrt{3} - 0)^2} = \\sqrt{(-1)^2 + (\\sqrt{3})^2} = \\sqrt{1 + 3} = \\sqrt{4} = 2\n  \\]\n\n- \\(QS\\): The distance between \\(Q\\) and \\(S\\) is calculated as follows:\n  \\[\n  QS = \\sqrt{(-2 - 2)^2 + (0 - 0)^2} = \\sqrt{(-4)^2} = \\sqrt{16} = 4\n  \\]\n\nNow, we have the side lengths of triangle \\(PQS\\):\n- \\(PQ = 2\\sqrt{3}\\)\n- \\(PS = 2\\)\n- \\(QS = 4\\)\n\nTo find the smallest angle, we use the cosine rule. The cosine rule states:\n\\[\n\\cos C = \\frac{a^2 + b^2 - c^2}{2ab}\n\\]\nwhere \\(C\\) is the angle opposite side \\(c\\).\n\nLet's find \\(\\angle PSQ\\) (opposite side \\(PQ\\)):\n\\[\n\\cos \\angle PSQ = \\frac{PS^2 + QS^2 - PQ^2}{2 \\cdot PS \\cdot QS} = \\frac{2^2 + 4^2 - (2\\sqrt{3})^2}{2 \\cdot 2 \\cdot 4}\n\\]\n\\[\n= \\frac{4 + 16 - 12}{16} = \\frac{8}{16} = \\frac{1}{2}\n\\]\n\nThus, \\(\\angle PSQ = \\cos^{-1}\\left(\\frac{1}{2}\\right) = 60^\\circ\\).\n\nNext, let's find \\(\\angle PQS\\) (opposite side \\(PS\\)):\n\\[\n\\cos \\angle PQS = \\frac{PQ^2 + QS^2 - PS^2}{2 \\cdot PQ \\cdot QS} = \\frac{(2\\sqrt{3})^2 + 4^2 - 2^2}{2 \\cdot 2\\sqrt{3} \\cdot 4}\n\\]\n\\[\n= \\frac{12 + 16 - 4}{16\\sqrt{3}} = \\frac{24}{16\\sqrt{3}} = \\frac{3}{2\\sqrt{3}} = \\frac{\\sqrt{3}}{2}\n\\]\n\nThus, \\(\\angle PQS = \\cos^{-1}\\left(\\frac{\\sqrt{3}}{2}\\right) = 30^\\circ\\).\n\nFinally, the remaining angle \\(\\angle QPS\\) is:\n\\[\n\\angle QPS = 180^\\circ - \\angle PSQ - \\angle PQS = 180^\\circ - 60^\\circ - 30^\\circ = 90^\\circ\n\\]\n\nTherefore, the smallest angle in triangle \\(PQS\\) is \\(\\boxed{30^\\circ}\\)."
"To solve this problem, we need to follow the steps of folding and calculate the area of the resulting trapezoidal figure.\n\n1. **Initial Square and First Fold:**\n   - We start with an 8-inch by 8-inch square.\n   - The square is folded along a diagonal, creating a right triangle with legs of 8 inches each. The hypotenuse of this triangle is \\(8\\sqrt{2}\\) inches.\n\n2. **Second Fold:**\n   - The right angle vertex of the triangle is folded to meet the midpoint of the hypotenuse.\n   - The midpoint of the hypotenuse is at \\(\\frac{8\\sqrt{2}}{2} = 4\\sqrt{2}\\) inches from each vertex along the hypotenuse.\n\n3. **Resulting Trapezoid:**\n   - The fold creates a trapezoid. The base of the trapezoid is the segment from the midpoint of the hypotenuse to one of the original vertices of the hypotenuse.\n   - The height of the trapezoid is half the height of the original triangle, which is 4 inches (since the original triangle's height is 8 inches).\n\n4. **Calculating the Area:**\n   - The trapezoid has one base of length \\(4\\sqrt{2}\\) inches (half the hypotenuse) and the other base is the same length because the fold is symmetric.\n   - The height of the trapezoid is 4 inches.\n\nThe area \\(A\\) of a trapezoid is given by the formula:\n\\[\nA = \\frac{1}{2} \\times (\\text{Base}_1 + \\text{Base}_2) \\times \\text{Height}\n\\]\nSubstituting the known values:\n\\[\nA = \\frac{1}{2} \\times (4\\sqrt{2} + 4\\sqrt{2}) \\times 4 = \\frac{1}{2} \\times 8\\sqrt{2} \\times 4 = 16\\sqrt{2}\n\\]\n\nTherefore, the area of the resulting trapezoidal figure is \\(16\\sqrt{2}\\) square inches."
"The solid in the diagram consists of a cylinder and a cone, both with the same base radius and height. We need to find the volume of this solid in terms of \\(\\pi\\).\n\nFirst, let's calculate the volume of the cylinder. The formula for the volume of a cylinder is:\n\n\\[\nV_{\\text{cylinder}} = \\pi r^2 h\n\\]\n\nwhere \\(r\\) is the radius of the base and \\(h\\) is the height. For the cylinder, the radius \\(r = \\sqrt{3}\\) and the height \\(h = 10\\). Substituting these values into the formula gives:\n\n\\[\nV_{\\text{cylinder}} = \\pi (\\sqrt{3})^2 \\cdot 10 = \\pi \\cdot 3 \\cdot 10 = 30\\pi\n\\]\n\nNext, let's calculate the volume of the cone. The formula for the volume of a cone is:\n\n\\[\nV_{\\text{cone}} = \\frac{1}{3} \\pi r^2 h\n\\]\n\nFor the cone, the radius \\(r = \\sqrt{3}\\) and the height \\(h = 10\\). Substituting these values into the formula gives:\n\n\\[\nV_{\\text{cone}} = \\frac{1}{3} \\pi (\\sqrt{3})^2 \\cdot 10 = \\frac{1}{3} \\pi \\cdot 3 \\cdot 10 = 10\\pi\n\\]\n\nFinally, the total volume of the solid is the sum of the volumes of the cylinder and the cone:\n\n\\[\nV_{\\text{total}} = V_{\\text{cylinder}} + V_{\\text{cone}} = 30\\pi + 10\\pi = 40\\pi\n\\]\n\nThus, the volume of the solid is \\(\\boxed{40\\pi}\\)."
'To find the volume of the new cube, we first need to determine the side length of the original cube.\n\nThe surface area of a cube is given by the formula:\n\\[ \\text{Surface Area} = 6s^2, \\]\nwhere \\( s \\) is the side length of the cube.\n\nGiven that the surface area is 600 square inches, we can set up the equation:\n\\[ 6s^2 = 600. \\]\n\nSolving for \\( s^2 \\), we divide both sides by 6:\n\\[ s^2 = 100. \\]\n\nTaking the square root of both sides, we find:\n\\[ s = 10 \\text{ inches}. \\]\n\nNow, when the edges of the cube are doubled in length, the new side length becomes:\n\\[ 2s = 2 \\times 10 = 20 \\text{ inches}. \\]\n\nThe volume of a cube is given by the formula:\n\\[ \\text{Volume} = s^3. \\]\n\nFor the new cube with side length 20 inches, the volume is:\n\\[ \\text{Volume} = 20^3 = 20 \\times 20 \\times 20 = 8000 \\text{ cubic inches}. \\]\n\nTherefore, the volume of the new cube is \\(\\boxed{8000}\\) cubic inches.'
"To find the area of the entire star, we first need to determine the side length of the equilateral triangles and the central hexagon.\n\nGiven that \\(\\overline{AC}\\) is 3 units long, we note that \\(\\overline{AC}\\) is the diagonal of the central hexagon, which is composed of two equilateral triangles. Since \\(\\overline{AC}\\) spans two sides of the equilateral triangles, each side of the equilateral triangles is \\(\\frac{3}{2}\\) units long.\n\nNow, let's calculate the area of one equilateral triangle with side length \\(s = \\frac{3}{2}\\). The formula for the area of an equilateral triangle is:\n\n\\[\n\\text{Area} = \\frac{\\sqrt{3}}{4} s^2\n\\]\n\nSubstituting \\(s = \\frac{3}{2}\\):\n\n\\[\n\\text{Area} = \\frac{\\sqrt{3}}{4} \\left(\\frac{3}{2}\\right)^2 = \\frac{\\sqrt{3}}{4} \\cdot \\frac{9}{4} = \\frac{9\\sqrt{3}}{16}\n\\]\n\nThere are 6 equilateral triangles in the star, so the total area of the triangles is:\n\n\\[\n6 \\times \\frac{9\\sqrt{3}}{16} = \\frac{54\\sqrt{3}}{16} = \\frac{27\\sqrt{3}}{8}\n\\]\n\nNext, we calculate the area of the central hexagon. The central hexagon can be divided into 6 smaller equilateral triangles, each with side length \\(\\frac{3}{2}\\). The area of one such triangle is \\(\\frac{9\\sqrt{3}}{16}\\), as calculated earlier.\n\nThus, the area of the central hexagon is:\n\n\\[\n6 \\times \\frac{9\\sqrt{3}}{16} = \\frac{54\\sqrt{3}}{16} = \\frac{27\\sqrt{3}}{8}\n\\]\n\nFinally, the total area of the star, which includes both the triangles and the central hexagon, is:\n\n\\[\n\\frac{27\\sqrt{3}}{8} + \\frac{27\\sqrt{3}}{8} = \\frac{54\\sqrt{3}}{8} = \\frac{27\\sqrt{3}}{4}\n\\]\n\nTherefore, the area of the entire star is \\(\\boxed{\\frac{27\\sqrt{3}}{4}}\\) square units."
'Let the original diameter of the pizza be \\( d \\) inches. The original radius is \\( \\frac{d}{2} \\) inches, so the original area is \n\n\\[\nA = \\pi \\left(\\frac{d}{2}\\right)^2 = \\frac{\\pi d^2}{4}.\n\\]\n\nWhen the diameter increases by 2 inches, the new diameter is \\( d + 2 \\) inches, and the new radius is \\( \\frac{d + 2}{2} \\) inches. The new area is \n\n\\[\nA_{\\text{new}} = \\pi \\left(\\frac{d + 2}{2}\\right)^2 = \\frac{\\pi (d + 2)^2}{4}.\n\\]\n\nAccording to the problem, the area increases by 44%, so\n\n\\[\nA_{\\text{new}} = A + 0.44A = 1.44A.\n\\]\n\nSubstituting the expressions for \\( A \\) and \\( A_{\\text{new}} \\), we have\n\n\\[\n\\frac{\\pi (d + 2)^2}{4} = 1.44 \\cdot \\frac{\\pi d^2}{4}.\n\\]\n\nCanceling \\(\\pi\\) and multiplying both sides by 4, we get\n\n\\[\n(d + 2)^2 = 1.44d^2.\n\\]\n\nExpanding the left side, we have\n\n\\[\nd^2 + 4d + 4 = 1.44d^2.\n\\]\n\nRearranging terms gives\n\n\\[\n1.44d^2 - d^2 - 4d - 4 = 0,\n\\]\n\nwhich simplifies to\n\n\\[\n0.44d^2 - 4d - 4 = 0.\n\\]\n\nTo eliminate the decimals, multiply the entire equation by 100:\n\n\\[\n44d^2 - 400d - 400 = 0.\n\\]\n\nDivide the entire equation by 4:\n\n\\[\n11d^2 - 100d - 100 = 0.\n\\]\n\nUsing the quadratic formula \\( d = \\frac{-b \\pm \\sqrt{b^2 - 4ac}}{2a} \\), where \\( a = 11 \\), \\( b = -100 \\), and \\( c = -100 \\), we find\n\n\\[\nd = \\frac{-(-100) \\pm \\sqrt{(-100)^2 - 4 \\cdot 11 \\cdot (-100)}}{2 \\cdot 11}.\n\\]\n\nThis simplifies to\n\n\\[\nd = \\frac{100 \\pm \\sqrt{10000 + 4400}}{22},\n\\]\n\n\\[\nd = \\frac{100 \\pm \\sqrt{14400}}{22},\n\\]\n\n\\[\nd = \\frac{100 \\pm 120}{22}.\n\\]\n\nThe two possible solutions are\n\n\\[\nd = \\frac{220}{22} = 10 \\quad \\text{and} \\quad d = \\frac{-20}{22} = -\\frac{10}{11}.\n\\]\n\nSince a diameter cannot be negative, we have \\( d = 10 \\).\n\nThe original area is\n\n\\[\nA = \\frac{\\pi d^2}{4} = \\frac{\\pi \\times 10^2}{4} = \\frac{100\\pi}{4} = 25\\pi.\n\\]\n\nThus, the area of the original pizza is \\(\\boxed{25\\pi}\\) square inches.'
'To find the total surface area of a cube, we use the formula for the surface area of a cube, which is given by:\n\n\\[ \\text{Surface Area} = 6a^2 \\]\n\nwhere \\( a \\) is the length of an edge of the cube.\n\nIn this case, each edge of the cube has a length of 3 inches. Therefore, we substitute \\( a = 3 \\) into the formula:\n\n\\[ \\text{Surface Area} = 6 \\times (3)^2 = 6 \\times 9 = 54 \\]\n\nThus, the total surface area of the cube is 54 square inches.'
'Since \\(\\triangle ABC\\) is isosceles with \\(AB = AC\\), and \\(B(0, 0)\\) and \\(C(20, 0)\\), the base \\(BC\\) is 20 units long. The area of \\(\\triangle ABC\\) is given as 240. The formula for the area of a triangle is:\n\n\\[\n\\text{Area} = \\frac{1}{2} \\times \\text{base} \\times \\text{height}\n\\]\n\nSubstituting the known values:\n\n\\[\n240 = \\frac{1}{2} \\times 20 \\times h\n\\]\n\nSolving for \\(h\\), the height:\n\n\\[\n240 = 10h \\implies h = 24\n\\]\n\nSince the height of the triangle is the \\(y\\)-coordinate of point \\(A\\) (as it is directly above the midpoint of \\(BC\\) on the \\(y\\)-axis), the \\(y\\)-coordinate of \\(A\\) is \\(\\boxed{24}\\).'
'The given triangle with sides \\(7\\), \\(24\\), and \\(25\\) is a right triangle because \\(7^2 + 24^2 = 49 + 576 = 625 = 25^2\\).\n\nIn a right triangle, the hypotenuse is the longest side, which is \\(25\\) in this case. The area of the triangle can be calculated using the two legs as the base and height:\n\n\\[\n\\text{Area} = \\frac{1}{2} \\times 7 \\times 24 = 84\n\\]\n\nThe altitudes of the triangle are the perpendicular distances from each vertex to the opposite side. We need to find the lengths of these altitudes.\n\n1. **Altitude to the hypotenuse (25):**\n\n   Let \\(h_c\\) be the altitude to the hypotenuse. Using the area formula:\n\n   \\[\n   \\frac{1}{2} \\times 25 \\times h_c = 84\n   \\]\n\n   Solving for \\(h_c\\):\n\n   \\[\n   25h_c = 168 \\quad \\Rightarrow \\quad h_c = \\frac{168}{25} = 6.72\n   \\]\n\n2. **Altitude to the side of length 7:**\n\n   Let \\(h_a\\) be the altitude to the side of length 7. Using the area formula:\n\n   \\[\n   \\frac{1}{2} \\times 7 \\times h_a = 84\n   \\]\n\n   Solving for \\(h_a\\):\n\n   \\[\n   7h_a = 168 \\quad \\Rightarrow \\quad h_a = \\frac{168}{7} = 24\n   \\]\n\n3. **Altitude to the side of length 24:**\n\n   Let \\(h_b\\) be the altitude to the side of length 24. Using the area formula:\n\n   \\[\n   \\frac{1}{2} \\times 24 \\times h_b = 84\n   \\]\n\n   Solving for \\(h_b\\):\n\n   \\[\n   24h_b = 168 \\quad \\Rightarrow \\quad h_b = \\frac{168}{24} = 7\n   \\]\n\nFinally, the sum of the lengths of the three altitudes is:\n\n\\[\nh_a + h_b + h_c = 24 + 7 + 6.72 = 37.72\n\\]\n\nThus, the sum of the lengths of the three altitudes is \\(\\boxed{37.72}\\).'
"To solve this problem, we need to calculate the total area of the two non-shaded crescent-shaped regions formed by the semicircles on the legs of the right triangle.\n\nFirst, let's find the hypotenuse of the right triangle using the Pythagorean theorem. The legs of the triangle are 6 inches and 8 inches, so the hypotenuse \\( c \\) is:\n\n\\[\nc = \\sqrt{6^2 + 8^2} = \\sqrt{36 + 64} = \\sqrt{100} = 10 \\text{ inches}\n\\]\n\nNow, let's calculate the area of each semicircle:\n\n1. **Semicircle on the 6-inch leg:**\n   - Radius = \\( \\frac{6}{2} = 3 \\) inches\n   - Area = \\( \\frac{1}{2} \\pi \\times 3^2 = \\frac{9\\pi}{2} \\)\n\n2. **Semicircle on the 8-inch leg:**\n   - Radius = \\( \\frac{8}{2} = 4 \\) inches\n   - Area = \\( \\frac{1}{2} \\pi \\times 4^2 = 8\\pi \\)\n\n3. **Semicircle on the 10-inch hypotenuse (shaded):**\n   - Radius = \\( \\frac{10}{2} = 5 \\) inches\n   - Area = \\( \\frac{1}{2} \\pi \\times 5^2 = \\frac{25\\pi}{2} \\)\n\nThe total area of the two non-shaded crescent-shaped regions is the sum of the areas of the semicircles on the legs minus the area of the semicircle on the hypotenuse:\n\n\\[\n\\left(\\frac{9\\pi}{2} + 8\\pi\\right) - \\frac{25\\pi}{2}\n\\]\n\nFirst, let's find a common denominator to combine the terms:\n\n\\[\n\\frac{9\\pi}{2} + \\frac{16\\pi}{2} = \\frac{25\\pi}{2}\n\\]\n\nNow, subtract the area of the shaded semicircle:\n\n\\[\n\\frac{25\\pi}{2} - \\frac{25\\pi}{2} = 0\n\\]\n\nThus, the total area of the two non-shaded crescent-shaped regions is \\(\\boxed{0}\\)."
"To find the radius of the inscribed circle (inradius) of triangle \\(ABC\\), we can use the formula for the inradius \\(r\\) of a triangle:\n\n\\[\nr = \\frac{A}{s}\n\\]\n\nwhere \\(A\\) is the area of the triangle and \\(s\\) is the semi-perimeter of the triangle.\n\nFirst, we calculate the semi-perimeter \\(s\\):\n\n\\[\ns = \\frac{AB + AC + BC}{2} = \\frac{10 + 17 + 21}{2} = 24\n\\]\n\nNext, we use Heron's formula to find the area \\(A\\) of the triangle:\n\n\\[\nA = \\sqrt{s(s-a)(s-b)(s-c)}\n\\]\n\nwhere \\(a = 10\\), \\(b = 17\\), and \\(c = 21\\). Plugging in these values, we get:\n\n\\[\nA = \\sqrt{24(24-10)(24-17)(24-21)} = \\sqrt{24 \\times 14 \\times 7 \\times 3}\n\\]\n\nCalculating step-by-step:\n\n\\[\n24 - 10 = 14, \\quad 24 - 17 = 7, \\quad 24 - 21 = 3\n\\]\n\n\\[\nA = \\sqrt{24 \\times 14 \\times 7 \\times 3}\n\\]\n\n\\[\nA = \\sqrt{24 \\times 294}\n\\]\n\n\\[\nA = \\sqrt{7056}\n\\]\n\n\\[\nA = 84\n\\]\n\nNow, we can find the inradius \\(r\\):\n\n\\[\nr = \\frac{A}{s} = \\frac{84}{24} = 3.5\n\\]\n\nThus, the radius of the inscribed circle is \\(\\boxed{3.5}\\)."
"To find the area of rectangle \\(ABCD\\), we need to determine the lengths of its sides. Let's denote the length of \\(AB\\) as \\(h\\) and the length of \\(BC\\) as \\(w\\).\n\nGiven:\n- The area of \\(\\triangle ABE\\) is 8.\n- The area of \\(\\triangle ADF\\) is 5.\n- The area of \\(\\triangle CEF\\) is 9.\n\nSince \\(ABCD\\) is a rectangle, the height \\(h\\) is the same for both triangles \\(\\triangle ABE\\) and \\(\\triangle ADF\\). We can use the formula for the area of a triangle, \\(\\text{Area} = \\frac{1}{2} \\times \\text{base} \\times \\text{height}\\).\n\n1. **For \\(\\triangle ABE\\):**\n   \\[\n   \\frac{1}{2} \\times BE \\times h = 8 \\implies BE \\times h = 16\n   \\]\n\n2. **For \\(\\triangle ADF\\):**\n   \\[\n   \\frac{1}{2} \\times DF \\times h = 5 \\implies DF \\times h = 10\n   \\]\n\n3. **For \\(\\triangle CEF\\):**\n   \\[\n   \\frac{1}{2} \\times EF \\times (w - h) = 9 \\implies EF \\times (w - h) = 18\n   \\]\n\nSince \\(E\\) is on \\(BC\\) and \\(F\\) is on \\(CD\\), we have:\n- \\(BE + EC = w\\)\n- \\(DF + FC = h\\)\n\nFrom the areas of \\(\\triangle ABE\\) and \\(\\triangle ADF\\), we have:\n- \\(BE \\times h = 16\\)\n- \\(DF \\times h = 10\\)\n\nThus, \\(BE = \\frac{16}{h}\\) and \\(DF = \\frac{10}{h}\\).\n\nSince \\(BE + EC = w\\), we have:\n\\[\n\\frac{16}{h} + EC = w \\implies EC = w - \\frac{16}{h}\n\\]\n\nSince \\(DF + FC = h\\), we have:\n\\[\n\\frac{10}{h} + FC = h \\implies FC = h - \\frac{10}{h}\n\\]\n\nNow, consider \\(\\triangle CEF\\):\n- The base \\(EF\\) is the same for both \\(EC\\) and \\(FC\\), so:\n  \\[\n  EF = EC + FC = \\left(w - \\frac{16}{h}\\right) + \\left(h - \\frac{10}{h}\\right) = w + h - \\frac{26}{h}\n  \\]\n\nSubstitute \\(EF\\) into the area equation for \\(\\triangle CEF\\):\n\\[\n(w + h - \\frac{26}{h}) \\times (w - h) = 18\n\\]\n\nThis equation is complex, so let's solve it by considering the total area of the rectangle \\(ABCD\\):\n- The total area of the rectangle is \\(w \\times h\\).\n\nWe know:\n- \\(BE \\times h = 16\\)\n- \\(DF \\times h = 10\\)\n- \\(EF \\times (w - h) = 18\\)\n\nThe total area of the rectangle is the sum of the areas of the triangles:\n\\[\n\\text{Area of } ABCD = \\text{Area of } \\triangle ABE + \\text{Area of } \\triangle ADF + \\text{Area of } \\triangle CEF = 8 + 5 + 9 = 22\n\\]\n\nThus, the area of rectangle \\(ABCD\\) is \\(\\boxed{22}\\)."
"To solve this problem, we need to find the area of the isosceles right triangle \\( \\triangle ABC \\) that encloses a semicircle with an area of \\( 2\\pi \\).\n\nFirst, let's find the radius of the semicircle. The area of a semicircle is given by the formula:\n\n\\[\n\\text{Area} = \\frac{1}{2} \\pi r^2\n\\]\n\nGiven that the area of the semicircle is \\( 2\\pi \\), we can set up the equation:\n\n\\[\n\\frac{1}{2} \\pi r^2 = 2\\pi\n\\]\n\nSolving for \\( r^2 \\), we divide both sides by \\( \\pi \\):\n\n\\[\n\\frac{1}{2} r^2 = 2\n\\]\n\nMultiply both sides by 2:\n\n\\[\nr^2 = 4\n\\]\n\nTaking the square root of both sides, we find:\n\n\\[\nr = 2\n\\]\n\nThe radius of the semicircle is 2. Since the semicircle is tangent to the two legs of the isosceles right triangle and its center \\( O \\) is on the hypotenuse \\( \\overline{AB} \\), the distance from \\( O \\) to each leg is equal to the radius, which is 2.\n\nIn an isosceles right triangle, the legs are equal, and the hypotenuse is \\( \\sqrt{2} \\) times the length of a leg. Let the length of each leg be \\( x \\). The center of the semicircle is at a distance of 2 from each leg, so the length of each leg is:\n\n\\[\nx = 2 + 2 = 4\n\\]\n\nNow, we can find the area of the triangle. The area \\( A \\) of an isosceles right triangle with leg length \\( x \\) is given by:\n\n\\[\nA = \\frac{1}{2} x^2\n\\]\n\nSubstituting \\( x = 4 \\):\n\n\\[\nA = \\frac{1}{2} \\times 4^2 = \\frac{1}{2} \\times 16 = 8\n\\]\n\nThus, the area of triangle \\( \\triangle ABC \\) is \\(\\boxed{8}\\)."
"To solve this problem, we need to use the properties of medians and altitudes in a right triangle.\n\nGiven:\n- \\(\\triangle ABC\\) is a right triangle with \\(\\angle B = 90^\\circ\\).\n- \\(D\\) is the midpoint of \\(AC\\), so \\(BD\\) is a median.\n- \\(BE\\) is an altitude, so \\(E\\) is the foot of the perpendicular from \\(B\\) to \\(AC\\).\n- \\(BD = 2 \\cdot DE\\).\n\nWe need to find \\(\\frac{AB}{EC}\\).\n\nFirst, let's denote:\n- \\(AB = c\\),\n- \\(BC = a\\),\n- \\(AC = b\\).\n\nSince \\(D\\) is the midpoint of \\(AC\\), we have \\(AD = DC = \\frac{b}{2}\\).\n\nThe length of the median \\(BD\\) in a right triangle is given by:\n\\[\nBD = \\frac{1}{2} \\sqrt{2c^2 + 2a^2 - b^2}\n\\]\n\nSince \\(BE\\) is an altitude, we have:\n\\[\nBE = \\frac{ac}{b}\n\\]\n\nGiven \\(BD = 2 \\cdot DE\\), we can express \\(DE\\) in terms of \\(BD\\):\n\\[\nDE = \\frac{BD}{2}\n\\]\n\nSince \\(DE\\) is the segment from \\(D\\) to \\(E\\) along \\(AC\\), and \\(E\\) is the foot of the altitude, we have:\n\\[\nDE = \\frac{b}{2} - EC\n\\]\n\nThus, we have:\n\\[\nBD = 2 \\left(\\frac{b}{2} - EC\\right)\n\\]\n\nSubstituting the expression for \\(BD\\):\n\\[\n\\frac{1}{2} \\sqrt{2c^2 + 2a^2 - b^2} = 2 \\left(\\frac{b}{2} - EC\\right)\n\\]\n\nSimplifying:\n\\[\n\\sqrt{2c^2 + 2a^2 - b^2} = 4 \\left(\\frac{b}{2} - EC\\right)\n\\]\n\n\\[\n\\sqrt{2c^2 + 2a^2 - b^2} = 2b - 4EC\n\\]\n\nNow, we need to find \\(\\frac{AB}{EC}\\):\n- We know \\(AB = c\\).\n- We need to express \\(EC\\) in terms of known quantities.\n\nFrom the equation:\n\\[\n2b - 4EC = \\sqrt{2c^2 + 2a^2 - b^2}\n\\]\n\nSolving for \\(EC\\):\n\\[\n4EC = 2b - \\sqrt{2c^2 + 2a^2 - b^2}\n\\]\n\n\\[\nEC = \\frac{2b - \\sqrt{2c^2 + 2a^2 - b^2}}{4}\n\\]\n\nFinally, the ratio \\(\\frac{AB}{EC}\\) is:\n\\[\n\\frac{AB}{EC} = \\frac{c}{\\frac{2b - \\sqrt{2c^2 + 2a^2 - b^2}}{4}}\n\\]\n\n\\[\n= \\frac{4c}{2b - \\sqrt{2c^2 + 2a^2 - b^2}}\n\\]\n\nThis expression gives the ratio \\(\\frac{AB}{EC}\\) in terms of the sides of the triangle. However, without specific numerical values for \\(a\\), \\(b\\), and \\(c\\), this is the most simplified form we can provide."
'Since \\(\\angle ABC = \\angle ACB\\), \\(\\triangle ABC\\) is an isosceles triangle with \\(AB = AC\\).\n\nLet \\(AB = AC = x\\). The perimeter of the triangle is given by:\n\n\\[\nAB + BC + AC = 32\n\\]\n\nSubstituting the known values:\n\n\\[\nx + 12 + x = 32\n\\]\n\nSimplifying, we have:\n\n\\[\n2x + 12 = 32\n\\]\n\nSubtract 12 from both sides:\n\n\\[\n2x = 20\n\\]\n\nDivide both sides by 2:\n\n\\[\nx = 10\n\\]\n\nThus, the length of \\(AB\\) is \\(\\boxed{10}\\).'
'To solve this problem, we can use the concept of similar triangles. Since \\(\\overline{MN} \\parallel \\overline{AB}\\), triangles \\(\\triangle CMN\\) and \\(\\triangle CAB\\) are similar by the AA (Angle-Angle) similarity criterion.\n\nGiven the lengths:\n- \\(CM = 3\\)\n- \\(MA = 5\\)\n- \\(CN = 2.4\\)\n\nWe need to find the length of \\(\\overline{BN}\\).\n\nSince the triangles are similar, the ratio of corresponding sides is equal. Therefore, we have:\n\n\\[\n\\frac{CM}{CA} = \\frac{CN}{CB}\n\\]\n\nFirst, calculate \\(CA\\):\n\\[\nCA = CM + MA = 3 + 5 = 8\n\\]\n\nNow, set up the proportion using the similarity of the triangles:\n\\[\n\\frac{3}{8} = \\frac{2.4}{CB}\n\\]\n\nSolve for \\(CB\\):\n\\[\nCB = \\frac{2.4 \\times 8}{3} = \\frac{19.2}{3} = 6.4\n\\]\n\nNow, calculate \\(BN\\):\n\\[\nBN = CB - CN = 6.4 - 2.4 = 4\n\\]\n\nThus, the length of \\(\\overline{BN}\\) is \\(\\boxed{4}\\) units.'
"To find the area of parallelogram \\( UVRW \\), we need to understand the relationship between the triangles and the parallelogram.\n\nIn \\(\\triangle PQR\\), \\(U\\), \\(W\\), and \\(V\\) are the midpoints of the sides. Therefore, \\(UV\\), \\(VW\\), and \\(WU\\) are medians of \\(\\triangle PQR\\). Similarly, in \\(\\triangle STU\\), \\(R\\), \\(V\\), and \\(W\\) are the midpoints of the sides, making \\(RV\\), \\(VW\\), and \\(WR\\) medians of \\(\\triangle STU\\).\n\nSince \\(U\\), \\(V\\), \\(W\\), and \\(R\\) are midpoints, each of the triangles \\(\\triangle PQR\\) and \\(\\triangle STU\\) is divided into four smaller triangles of equal area by the medians. Each of these smaller triangles has an area of \\(\\frac{1}{4}\\) of the original triangle's area.\n\nThe parallelogram \\(UVRW\\) is formed by two of these smaller triangles from each of the original triangles. Specifically, it consists of two triangles from \\(\\triangle PQR\\) and two triangles from \\(\\triangle STU\\), each with an area of \\(\\frac{1}{4}\\).\n\nThus, the area of parallelogram \\(UVRW\\) is the sum of the areas of these four smaller triangles:\n\\[\n\\text{Area of } UVRW = 2 \\times \\frac{1}{4} + 2 \\times \\frac{1}{4} = \\frac{1}{2} + \\frac{1}{2} = 1.\n\\]\n\nTherefore, the area of parallelogram \\(UVRW\\) is \\(\\boxed{1}\\)."
"To solve this problem, we need to find the side length \\( s \\) of the cube and then determine the side length of the square.\n\nFirst, let's set up the equations for the cube:\n\n1. The surface area of the cube is \\( 6s^2 \\).\n2. The volume of the cube is \\( s^3 \\).\n\nAccording to the problem, the surface area equals \\(\\frac{1}{6}\\) of the volume:\n\n\\[\n6s^2 = \\frac{1}{6} s^3\n\\]\n\nMultiply both sides by 6 to eliminate the fraction:\n\n\\[\n36s^2 = s^3\n\\]\n\nDivide both sides by \\( s^2 \\) (assuming \\( s \\neq 0 \\)):\n\n\\[\n36 = s\n\\]\n\nSo, the side length of the cube is \\( s = 36 \\).\n\nNext, we need to find the side length of the square such that the area of the square equals the volume of the cube. The volume of the cube is:\n\n\\[\ns^3 = 36^3\n\\]\n\nCalculate \\( 36^3 \\):\n\n\\[\n36^3 = 36 \\times 36 \\times 36 = 1296 \\times 36 = 46656\n\\]\n\nThe area of the square is \\( x^2 \\), where \\( x \\) is the side length of the square. We set this equal to the volume of the cube:\n\n\\[\nx^2 = 46656\n\\]\n\nTo find \\( x \\), take the square root of both sides:\n\n\\[\nx = \\sqrt{46656}\n\\]\n\nCalculate the square root:\n\n\\[\nx = 216\n\\]\n\nTherefore, the side length of the square should be \\( \\boxed{216} \\)."
"To find the area of quadrilateral \\(ABCD\\) with vertices \\(A(3,2)\\), \\(B(3,-2)\\), \\(C(-3,-2)\\), and \\(D(-3,0)\\), we can use the Shoelace Theorem (or the surveyor's formula). The formula for the area of a polygon given its vertices \\((x_1, y_1), (x_2, y_2), \\ldots, (x_n, y_n)\\) is:\n\n\\[\n\\text{Area} = \\frac{1}{2} \\left| \\sum_{i=1}^{n-1} (x_i y_{i+1} - y_i x_{i+1}) + (x_n y_1 - y_n x_1) \\right|\n\\]\n\nApplying this to quadrilateral \\(ABCD\\), we list the coordinates in order and repeat the first point at the end:\n\n\\[\n\\begin{array}{c|c}\nx & y \\\\\n\\hline\n3 & 2 \\\\\n3 & -2 \\\\\n-3 & -2 \\\\\n-3 & 0 \\\\\n3 & 2 \\\\\n\\end{array}\n\\]\n\nNow, we calculate the sum of the products of diagonals:\n\n\\[\n\\begin{align*}\n\\text{Sum of } x_iy_{i+1} &: \\\\\n3 \\cdot (-2) & = -6 \\\\\n3 \\cdot (-2) & = -6 \\\\\n-3 \\cdot 0 & = 0 \\\\\n-3 \\cdot 2 & = -6 \\\\\n\\hline\n\\text{Total} & = -18 \\\\\n\\end{align*}\n\\]\n\n\\[\n\\begin{align*}\n\\text{Sum of } y_ix_{i+1} &: \\\\\n2 \\cdot 3 & = 6 \\\\\n-2 \\cdot (-3) & = 6 \\\\\n-2 \\cdot (-3) & = 6 \\\\\n0 \\cdot 3 & = 0 \\\\\n\\hline\n\\text{Total} & = 18 \\\\\n\\end{align*}\n\\]\n\nThe area is then:\n\n\\[\n\\text{Area} = \\frac{1}{2} \\left| -18 - 18 \\right| = \\frac{1}{2} \\times 36 = 18\n\\]\n\nThus, the area of quadrilateral \\(ABCD\\) is \\(\\boxed{18}\\)."
'In right triangle \\(ABC\\) with \\(\\angle A = 90^\\circ\\), we are given that \\(AB = 5\\) and \\(BC = 8\\). We need to find \\(\\sin B\\).\n\nFirst, we use the Pythagorean theorem to find the length of the third side, \\(AC\\):\n\n\\[\nAC^2 + AB^2 = BC^2\n\\]\n\n\\[\nAC^2 + 5^2 = 8^2\n\\]\n\n\\[\nAC^2 + 25 = 64\n\\]\n\n\\[\nAC^2 = 64 - 25\n\\]\n\n\\[\nAC^2 = 39\n\\]\n\n\\[\nAC = \\sqrt{39}\n\\]\n\nNow, \\(\\sin B\\) is defined as the ratio of the length of the side opposite angle \\(B\\) to the hypotenuse. In this triangle, the side opposite angle \\(B\\) is \\(AC\\), and the hypotenuse is \\(BC\\). Therefore:\n\n\\[\n\\sin B = \\frac{AC}{BC} = \\frac{\\sqrt{39}}{8}\n\\]\n\nThus, \\(\\sin B = \\frac{\\sqrt{39}}{8}\\).'
'In a right triangle, the cosine of one of the non-right angles is the ratio of the length of the adjacent side to the hypotenuse. However, since \\(\\angle A\\) is the right angle in triangle \\(ABC\\), \\(\\cos A\\) is not defined in the usual sense because cosine is defined for angles between \\(0^\\circ\\) and \\(90^\\circ\\) (exclusive). Therefore, \\(\\cos A = 0\\) because the cosine of \\(90^\\circ\\) is \\(0\\).'
'To compute \\(\\tan 150^\\circ\\), we can use the identity for the tangent of an angle in terms of a reference angle. The angle \\(150^\\circ\\) is in the second quadrant, where the tangent function is negative. The reference angle for \\(150^\\circ\\) is \\(180^\\circ - 150^\\circ = 30^\\circ\\).\n\nWe know that:\n\\[\n\\tan 30^\\circ = \\frac{1}{\\sqrt{3}}\n\\]\n\nSince \\(150^\\circ\\) is in the second quadrant, \\(\\tan 150^\\circ\\) is negative:\n\\[\n\\tan 150^\\circ = -\\tan 30^\\circ = -\\frac{1}{\\sqrt{3}}\n\\]\n\nTo express this in a more standard form, we rationalize the denominator:\n\\[\n-\\frac{1}{\\sqrt{3}} = -\\frac{\\sqrt{3}}{3}\n\\]\n\nThus, \\(\\tan 150^\\circ = -\\frac{\\sqrt{3}}{3}\\).\n\nThe answer is \\(-\\frac{\\sqrt{3}}{3}\\).'
"To find the volume of the right rectangular prism \\(ABCDEFGH\\), we need to determine the dimensions of the prism. We know that the volume of pyramid \\(ABCH\\) is given as 20. The volume of a pyramid is calculated using the formula:\n\n\\[\nV = \\frac{1}{3} \\times \\text{Base Area} \\times \\text{Height}\n\\]\n\nIn this case, the base of the pyramid \\(ABCH\\) is the rectangle \\(ABC\\), and the height is the perpendicular distance from \\(H\\) to the plane containing \\(ABC\\), which is the same as the height of the prism.\n\nLet's denote:\n- The length of \\(AB\\) as \\(l\\),\n- The width of \\(BC\\) as \\(w\\),\n- The height of the prism (and the pyramid) as \\(h\\).\n\nThe area of the base \\(ABC\\) is \\(l \\times w\\).\n\nThe volume of the pyramid \\(ABCH\\) is:\n\n\\[\nV = \\frac{1}{3} \\times (l \\times w) \\times h = 20\n\\]\n\nThe volume of the rectangular prism \\(ABCDEFGH\\) is:\n\n\\[\nV_{\\text{prism}} = l \\times w \\times h\n\\]\n\nFrom the pyramid's volume equation, we have:\n\n\\[\n\\frac{1}{3} \\times (l \\times w) \\times h = 20\n\\]\n\nMultiplying both sides by 3 gives:\n\n\\[\n(l \\times w) \\times h = 60\n\\]\n\nThus, the volume of the rectangular prism is:\n\n\\[\nV_{\\text{prism}} = l \\times w \\times h = 60\n\\]\n\nTherefore, the volume of the rectangular prism \\(ABCDEFGH\\) is \\(\\boxed{60}\\)."
"To solve this problem, we need to find the area of the shaded region, which is the area of the circle minus the area of triangle \\(ABC\\).\n\nFirst, let's find the radius of circle \\(P\\). Since \\(AB\\) is a diameter of the circle and measures 4 cm, the radius \\(r\\) of the circle is half of the diameter:\n\n\\[\nr = \\frac{4}{2} = 2 \\text{ cm}\n\\]\n\nThe area of the circle is given by the formula:\n\n\\[\n\\text{Area of circle} = \\pi r^2 = \\pi (2)^2 = 4\\pi \\text{ square cm}\n\\]\n\nNext, we need to find the area of triangle \\(ABC\\). Since \\(AB\\) is a diameter of the circle and \\(C\\) is a point on the circle, triangle \\(ABC\\) is a right triangle with \\(AB\\) as the hypotenuse. The length of \\(BC\\) is given as 2 cm.\n\nUsing the Pythagorean theorem in triangle \\(ABC\\), we can find the length of \\(AC\\):\n\n\\[\nAB^2 = AC^2 + BC^2\n\\]\n\n\\[\n4^2 = AC^2 + 2^2\n\\]\n\n\\[\n16 = AC^2 + 4\n\\]\n\n\\[\nAC^2 = 12\n\\]\n\n\\[\nAC = \\sqrt{12} = 2\\sqrt{3} \\text{ cm}\n\\]\n\nNow, we can find the area of triangle \\(ABC\\) using the formula for the area of a right triangle:\n\n\\[\n\\text{Area of } \\triangle ABC = \\frac{1}{2} \\times AC \\times BC = \\frac{1}{2} \\times 2\\sqrt{3} \\times 2\n\\]\n\n\\[\n= 2\\sqrt{3} \\text{ square cm}\n\\]\n\nFinally, the area of the shaded region is the area of the circle minus the area of triangle \\(ABC\\):\n\n\\[\n\\text{Area of shaded region} = 4\\pi - 2\\sqrt{3} \\text{ square cm}\n\\]\n\nThus, the area of the shaded region is \\(4\\pi - 2\\sqrt{3}\\) square centimeters."
'The sum of the interior angles of a polygon with \\( n \\) sides is given by the formula:\n\n\\[\n(n-2) \\times 180^\\circ\n\\]\n\nFor a hexagon, \\( n = 6 \\). Plugging this into the formula gives:\n\n\\[\n(6-2) \\times 180^\\circ = 4 \\times 180^\\circ = 720^\\circ\n\\]\n\nTherefore, the sum of the measures of the interior angles in the convex hexagon is \\( \\boxed{720^\\circ} \\).'
'To find the volume of the right square pyramid \\(PABCD\\), we need to determine the side length of the square base \\(ABCD\\) and the height of the pyramid.\n\nGiven that \\(PBD\\) is an equilateral triangle with side length 6, we can use this information to find the height of the pyramid. Since \\(PBD\\) is equilateral, the height from \\(P\\) to the base \\(BD\\) is \\(\\frac{\\sqrt{3}}{2} \\times 6 = 3\\sqrt{3}\\).\n\nThe diagonal \\(BD\\) of the square base \\(ABCD\\) is also 6, as it is a side of the equilateral triangle \\(PBD\\). For a square with side length \\(s\\), the diagonal is given by \\(s\\sqrt{2}\\). Therefore, we have:\n\\[\ns\\sqrt{2} = 6\n\\]\nSolving for \\(s\\), we get:\n\\[\ns = \\frac{6}{\\sqrt{2}} = 3\\sqrt{2}\n\\]\n\nNow, we need to find the height \\(h\\) of the pyramid from \\(P\\) to the center of the square base. Since \\(PBD\\) is equilateral, the height from \\(P\\) to the midpoint of \\(BD\\) is \\(3\\sqrt{3}\\). The midpoint of \\(BD\\) is also the center of the square base.\n\nThe volume \\(V\\) of the pyramid is given by:\n\\[\nV = \\frac{1}{3} \\times \\text{Base Area} \\times \\text{Height}\n\\]\nThe area of the square base is:\n\\[\ns^2 = (3\\sqrt{2})^2 = 18\n\\]\n\nThus, the volume of the pyramid is:\n\\[\nV = \\frac{1}{3} \\times 18 \\times 3\\sqrt{3} = 18\\sqrt{3}\n\\]\n\nTherefore, the volume of the pyramid \\(PABCD\\) is \\(\\boxed{18\\sqrt{3}}\\).'
"To solve this problem, we need to compare the areas of squares inscribed in a semicircle and a circle, given that the area of the semicircle is half the area of the circle.\n\nLet's denote the radius of the semicircle in Figure A as \\( r_A \\) and the radius of the circle in Figure B as \\( r_B \\).\n\n1. **Area Relationship:**\n   - The area of the semicircle in Figure A is \\(\\frac{1}{2} \\pi r_A^2\\).\n   - The area of the circle in Figure B is \\(\\pi r_B^2\\).\n   - We are given that \\(\\frac{1}{2} \\pi r_A^2 = \\frac{1}{2} \\pi r_B^2\\), which implies \\( r_A^2 = \\frac{1}{2} r_B^2 \\).\n\n2. **Inscribed Square in the Semicircle:**\n   - The side length of a square inscribed in a semicircle of radius \\( r_A \\) is \\( r_A \\sqrt{2} \\).\n   - Therefore, the area of this square is \\((r_A \\sqrt{2})^2 = 2r_A^2\\).\n\n3. **Inscribed Square in the Circle:**\n   - The side length of a square inscribed in a circle of radius \\( r_B \\) is \\( r_B \\sqrt{2} \\).\n   - Therefore, the area of this square is \\((r_B \\sqrt{2})^2 = 2r_B^2\\).\n\n4. **Ratio of Areas:**\n   - The ratio of the area of the square inscribed in the semicircle to the area of the square inscribed in the circle is:\n   \\[\n   \\frac{2r_A^2}{2r_B^2} = \\frac{r_A^2}{r_B^2}\n   \\]\n   - From the area relationship, we have \\( r_A^2 = \\frac{1}{2} r_B^2 \\).\n   - Substituting this into the ratio gives:\n   \\[\n   \\frac{r_A^2}{r_B^2} = \\frac{\\frac{1}{2} r_B^2}{r_B^2} = \\frac{1}{2}\n   \\]\n\nThus, the area of the square inscribed in the semicircle is \\(\\frac{1}{2}\\) the area of the square inscribed in the circle. Therefore, the answer is \\(\\boxed{\\frac{1}{2}}\\)."

Since the golf balls are touching each other and the ends of the box, the total height of the three golf balls is equal to the height of the box.\n\nLet \\( d \\) be the diameter of each golf ball. Since there are three golf balls stacked vertically, the total height is \\( 3d \\).\n\nGiven that the total height is 13.5 cm, we have:\n\\[ 3d = 13.5 \\]\n\nSolving for \\( d \\), we divide both sides by 3:\n\\[ d = \\frac{13.5}{3} = 4.5 \\]\n\nNow that we have the diameter, we can find the circumference of each golf ball. "To solve this problem, we need to find the area of quadrilateral \\(ABDE\\) given the conditions of the triangle \\(\\triangle ABC\\).\n\nFirst, let's analyze the given information:\n- \\(\\triangle ABC\\) is an isosceles triangle with \\(AB = AC\\).\n- \\(AD\\) is an altitude, so it is perpendicular to \\(BC\\).\n- \\(AB \\parallel DE\\), which implies that \\(\\triangle ADE\\) is similar to \\(\\triangle ABC\\).\n\nGiven:\n- \\(BC = 12\\)\n- The area of \\(\\triangle ABC\\) is 180.\n\nSince \\(AD\\) is an altitude, the area of \\(\\triangle ABC\\) can be expressed as:\n\\[\n\\text{Area of } \\triangle ABC = \\frac{1}{2} \\times BC \\times AD = 180\n\\]\n\\[\n\\frac{1}{2} \\times 12 \\times AD = 180\n\\]\n\\[\n6 \\times AD = 180\n\\]\n\\[\nAD = 30\n\\]\n\nNow, since \\(\\triangle ADE \\sim \\triangle ABC\\) and \\(AB \\parallel DE\\), the ratio of similarity is the same as the ratio of the corresponding sides. Let \\(k\\) be the ratio of similarity, then:\n\\[\n\\frac{DE}{BC} = \\frac{AD}{AD + DE} = k\n\\]\n\nSince \\(AB \\parallel DE\\), the height from \\(D\\) to \\(AB\\) is the same as the height from \\(A\\) to \\(BC\\), which is \\(AD = 30\\).\n\nThe area of \\(\\triangle ADE\\) is:\n\\[\n\\text{Area of } \\triangle ADE = \\frac{1}{2} \\times DE \\times AD = \\frac{1}{2} \\times k \\times 12 \\times 30\n\\]\n\nSince \\(\\triangle ADE\\) is similar to \\(\\triangle ABC\\), the area of \\(\\triangle ADE\\) is \\(k^2\\) times the area of \\(\\triangle ABC\\):\n\\[\n\\text{Area of } \\triangle ADE = k^2 \\times 180\n\\]\n\nNow, the area of quadrilateral \\(ABDE\\) is the area of \\(\\triangle ABC\\) minus the area of \\(\\triangle ADE\\):\n\\[\n\\text{Area of } ABDE = \\text{Area of } \\triangle ABC - \\text{Area of } \\triangle ADE\n\\]\n\\[\n= 180 - k^2 \\times 180\n\\]\n\\[\n= 180(1 - k^2)\n\\]\n\nSince \\(k\\) is the ratio of the sides, and \\(DE\\) is parallel to \\(AB\\), we can find \\(k\\) by considering the similarity and the fact that \\(DE\\) is a line parallel to \\(AB\\) and divides the triangle into two similar triangles. The ratio \\(k\\) is determined by the height \\(AD\\) and the parallel line \\(DE\\).\n\nThus, the area of \\(ABDE\\) is:\n\\[\n\\text{Area of } ABDE = 180 \\times \\left(1 - \\left(\\frac{DE}{BC}\\right)^2\\right)\n\\]\n\nSince \\(DE\\) is parallel to \\(AB\\) and divides the triangle into two similar triangles, the area of \\(ABDE\\) is half of the area of \\(\\triangle ABC\\) because \\(DE\\) is parallel to \\(AB\\) and divides the triangle into two equal areas:\n\\[\n\\text{Area of } ABDE = \\frac{1}{2} \\times 180 = 90\n\\]\n\nTherefore, the area of \\(ABDE\\) is \\(\\boxed{90}\\)."
'To find the area of quadrilateral \\( DYES \\), we first need to determine the coordinates of each point based on the given information.\n\n1. **Coordinates of Points:**\n   - \\( A = (0, 5) \\)\n   - \\( B = (1, 5) \\)\n   - \\( C = (0, 4) \\)\n   - \\( D = (1, 4) \\)\n   - \\( E = (4, 1) \\)\n   - \\( F = (5, 1) \\)\n   - \\( G = (4, 0) \\)\n   - \\( H = (5, 0) \\)\n   - \\( Y = (0, 0) \\)\n   - \\( S = (5, 5) \\)\n\n2. **Vertices of Quadrilateral \\( DYES \\):**\n   - \\( D = (1, 4) \\)\n   - \\( Y = (0, 0) \\)\n   - \\( E = (4, 1) \\)\n   - \\( S = (5, 5) \\)\n\n3. **Area Calculation:**\n   We can use the Shoelace Theorem (or the formula for the area of a polygon given its vertices) to find the area of quadrilateral \\( DYES \\).\n\n   The formula for the area of a polygon with vertices \\((x_1, y_1), (x_2, y_2), \\ldots, (x_n, y_n)\\) is:\n   \\[\n   \\text{Area} = \\frac{1}{2} \\left| \\sum_{i=1}^{n-1} (x_i y_{i+1} - y_i x_{i+1}) + (x_n y_1 - y_n x_1) \\right|\n   \\]\n\n   Applying this to quadrilateral \\( DYES \\) with vertices \\( (1, 4), (0, 0), (4, 1), (5, 5) \\):\n\n   \\[\n   \\text{Area} = \\frac{1}{2} \\left| (1 \\cdot 0 + 0 \\cdot 1 + 4 \\cdot 5 + 5 \\cdot 4) - (4 \\cdot 0 + 0 \\cdot 4 + 1 \\cdot 5 + 5 \\cdot 1) \\right|\n   \\]\n\n   \\[\n   = \\frac{1}{2} \\left| (0 + 0 + 20 + 20) - (0 + 0 + 5 + 5) \\right|\n   \\]\n\n   \\[\n   = \\frac{1}{2} \\left| 40 - 10 \\right|\n   \\]\n\n   \\[\n   = \\frac{1}{2} \\times 30 = 15\n   \\]\n\nThus, the area of quadrilateral \\( DYES \\) is \\(\\boxed{15}\\).'
"To solve this problem, we need to find the perimeter of \\(\\triangle BDP\\) in rectangle \\(ABCD\\) where \\(AD = 1\\), and \\(\\overline{DB}\\) and \\(\\overline{DP}\\) trisect \\(\\angle ADC\\).\n\nFirst, let's set up the coordinates for the rectangle:\n- \\(A = (0, 1)\\)\n- \\(B = (b, 1)\\)\n- \\(C = (b, 0)\\)\n- \\(D = (0, 0)\\)\n\nSince \\(\\overline{DB}\\) and \\(\\overline{DP}\\) trisect \\(\\angle ADC\\), the angles \\(\\angle PDC\\), \\(\\angle PDB\\), and \\(\\angle BDC\\) are each \\(\\frac{\\pi}{3}\\).\n\nThe slope of \\(\\overline{DC}\\) is 0 (horizontal line), and the slope of \\(\\overline{DA}\\) is undefined (vertical line). The slope of \\(\\overline{DB}\\) is \\(\\frac{1}{b}\\).\n\nUsing the tangent of the angles, we can find the slopes of \\(\\overline{DP}\\) and \\(\\overline{DB}\\):\n- \\(\\tan(\\angle PDC) = \\tan\\left(\\frac{\\pi}{3}\\right) = \\sqrt{3}\\)\n- \\(\\tan(\\angle PDB) = \\tan\\left(\\frac{2\\pi}{3}\\right) = -\\sqrt{3}\\)\n\nThe slope of \\(\\overline{DP}\\) is \\(\\sqrt{3}\\), and the slope of \\(\\overline{DB}\\) is \\(-\\sqrt{3}\\).\n\nNow, let's find the coordinates of point \\(P\\) on \\(\\overline{AB}\\):\n- Since \\(P\\) is on \\(\\overline{AB}\\), its coordinates are \\((p, 1)\\).\n\nUsing the slope of \\(\\overline{DP}\\), we have:\n\\[\n\\frac{1 - 0}{p - 0} = \\sqrt{3} \\implies p = \\frac{1}{\\sqrt{3}}\n\\]\n\nNow, we can find the lengths of \\(\\overline{BD}\\), \\(\\overline{DP}\\), and \\(\\overline{BP}\\):\n- \\(\\overline{BD} = \\sqrt{b^2 + 1^2} = \\sqrt{b^2 + 1}\\)\n- \\(\\overline{DP} = \\sqrt{\\left(\\frac{1}{\\sqrt{3}}\\right)^2 + 1^2} = \\sqrt{\\frac{1}{3} + 1} = \\sqrt{\\frac{4}{3}} = \\frac{2}{\\sqrt{3}}\\)\n- \\(\\overline{BP} = b - \\frac{1}{\\sqrt{3}}\\)\n\nThe perimeter of \\(\\triangle BDP\\) is:\n\\[\n\\overline{BD} + \\overline{DP} + \\overline{BP} = \\sqrt{b^2 + 1} + \\frac{2}{\\sqrt{3}} + \\left(b - \\frac{1}{\\sqrt{3}}\\right)\n\\]\n\nSimplifying, we have:\n\\[\nb + \\sqrt{b^2 + 1} + \\frac{1}{\\sqrt{3}}\n\\]\n\nGiven the form \\(w + \\frac{x \\cdot \\sqrt{y}}{z}\\), we have:\n- \\(w = b\\)\n- \\(x = 1\\)\n- \\(y = 3\\)\n- \\(z = 3\\)\n\nThus, \\(w + x + y + z = b + 1 + 3 + 3 = b + 7\\).\n\nSince \\(b\\) is not specified, we assume \\(b = 8\\) (as per the diagram), so:\n\\[\nw + x + y + z = 8 + 7 = 15\n\\]\n\nTherefore, the answer is \\(\\boxed{15}\\)."
'To compute \\(\\cos(-150^\\circ)\\), we can use the fact that cosine is an even function, meaning \\(\\cos(-\\theta) = \\cos(\\theta)\\). Therefore, \\(\\cos(-150^\\circ) = \\cos(150^\\circ)\\).\n\nNext, we find \\(\\cos(150^\\circ)\\). The angle \\(150^\\circ\\) is in the second quadrant, and it can be expressed as \\(180^\\circ - 30^\\circ\\).
